Since it found in 1990, the McKinsey Global Institute (MGI) have sought 
to develop a deeper understand of the evolve global economy. As the 
business and economics research arm of McKinsey & Company, MGI aim 
to provide leader in the commercial, public, and social sector with the fact 
and insight on which to base management and policy decisions. The Lauder 
Institute at the University of Pennsylvania have ranked MGI the world’s number- 
one private-sector think tank in it Think Tank Index. 

MGI research combine the discipline of economics and management, 
employ the analytical tool of economics with the insight of business 
leaders. Our “micro-to-macro” methodology examines microeconomic 
industry trend to good understand the broad macroeconomic force 
affect business strategy and public policy. MGI’s in-depth report have 
cover more than 20 country and 30 industries. Current research focus 
on six themes: productivity and growth, natural resources, labor markets, 
the evolution of global financial markets, the economic impact of technology 
and innovation, and urbanization. Recent report have assess the 
digital economy, the impact of AI and automation on employment, income 
inequality, the productivity puzzle, the economic benefit of tackle gender 
inequality, a new era of global competition, Chinese innovation, and digital and 
financial globalization.

PREFACE 

Automation be not a new phenomenon, and fear about it transformation of the workplace 
and effect on employment date back centuries, even before the Industrial Revolution in 
the 18th and 19th centuries. In the 1960s, US President Lyndon Johnson empanel a 
“National Commission on Technology, Automation, and Economic Progress.” Among it 
conclusion be “the basic fact that technology destroys jobs, but not work.”* Fast forward 
and rapid recent advance in automation technologies, include artificial intelligence, 
autonomous systems, and robotics be now raise the fear anew—and with new urgency. 
In our January 2017 report on automation, A future that works: Automation, employment, 
and productivity, we analyze the automation potential of the global economy, the timeline 
over which the phenomenon could play out, and the powerful productivity boost that 
automation adoption could deliver. 

This report go a step further by examine both the potential labor market disruption 
from automation and some potential source of new labor demand that will create jobs. 
We develop scenario that seek to address some of the question most often raise in the 
public debate. Will there be enough work in the future to maintain full employment, and if so 
what will that work be? Which occupation will thrive, and which one will wither? What be 
the potential implication for skill and wage a machine perform some or the task that 
human now do? 

The report be part of the McKinsey Global Institute’s research program on the future of work, 
and be by no mean the final word on this topic. The technology continue to evolve, a will 
our collective understand of the economic implications. Indeed, we highlight some of the 
limitation of our analysis and scenarios, and area for further research. The report build on 
our previous research on labor markets, incomes, skills, and the expand range of model 
of work, include the gig economy, a well a the potential impact on the global economy 
of digitization, automation, robotics, and artificial intelligence. 

The research be lead by James Manyika, chairman and director of the McKinsey Global 
Institute and McKinsey senior partner base in San Francisco; Susan Lund, an MGI partner 
base in Washington, DC; Michael Chui, an MGI partner in San Francisco; Jacques Bughin, 
MGI director and McKinsey senior partner base in Brussels; and Jonathan Woetzel, 
MGI director and McKinsey senior partner in Shanghai. Parul Batra, Ryan Ko, and 
Saurabh Sanghvi head the research team at different time over the course of the project. 
The team comprise Julian Albert, Gurneet Singh Dandona, Nicholas Fletcher, Darien Lee, 
Nik Nayar, Sonia Vora, and Rachel Wong. 

We be deeply grateful to our academic advisers, who challenged our think and provide 
valuable feedback and guidance throughout the research. We thank Richard N. Cooper, 
Maurits C. Boas Professor of International Economics at Harvard University; Sir 
Christopher Pissarides, Nobel laureate and Regius Professor of Economics at the London 
School of Economics; Michael Spence, Nobel laureate and William R. Berkley Professor in 
Economics and Business at the NYU Stern School of Business; and Laura Tyson, Professor 
of Business Administration and Economics at the Haas School of Business, University of 
California, Berkeley.

IN BRIEF 

JOBS LOST, JOBS GAINED: WORKFORCE 
TRANSITIONS IN A TIME OF AUTOMATION 
In our late research on automation, we examine work 
that can be automate through 2030 and job that may 
be create in the same period. We draw from lesson 
from history and develop various scenario for the future. 
While it be hard to predict how all this will play out, our 
research provide some insight into the likely workforce 
transition that should be expect and their implications. 
Our key findings: 

 Automation technology include artificial intelligence 
and robotics will generate significant benefit for 
users, businesses, and economies, lift productivity 
and economic growth. The extent to which these 
technology displace worker will depend on the 
pace of their development and adoption, economic 
growth, and growth in demand for work. Even a it 
cause decline in some occupations, automation 
will change many more—60 percent of occupation 
have at least 30 percent of constituent work 
activity that could be automated. It will also create 
new occupation that do not exist today, much a 
technology of the past have done. 

 While about half of all work activity globally have 
the technical potential to be automate by adapt 
currently demonstrate technologies, the proportion 
of work actually displace by 2030 will likely be 
lower, because of technical, economic, and social 
factor that affect adoption. Our scenario across 46 
country suggest that between almost zero and one- 
third of work activity could be displace by 2030, 
with a midpoint of 15 percent. The proportion varies 
widely across countries, with advanced economy 
more affected by automation than develop ones, 
reflect high wage rate and thus economic 
incentive to automate. 

 Even with automation, the demand for work and 
worker could increase a economy grow, 
partly fuel by productivity growth enable 
by technological progress. Rising income and 
consumption especially in develop countries, 
increase health care for age societies, investment 
in infrastructure and energy, and other trend will 
create demand for work that could help offset the 
displacement of workers. Additional investment such 
a in infrastructure and construction, beneficial in their 
own right, could be need to reduce the risk of job 
shortage in some advanced economies. 

 Even if there be enough work to ensure full employment 
by 2030, major transition lie ahead that could match 
or even exceed the scale of historical shift out of 
agriculture and manufacturing. Our scenario suggest 
that by 2030, 75 million to 375 million worker (3 to 
14 percent of the global workforce) will need to switch 
occupational categories. Moreover, all worker will 
need to adapt, a their occupation evolve alongside 
increasingly capable machines. Some of that 
adaptation will require high educational attainment, 
or spending more time on activity that require social 
and emotional skills, creativity, high-level cognitive 
capability and other skill relatively hard to automate. 

 Income polarization could continue in the United 
States and other advanced economies, where 
demand for high-wage occupation may grow the 
most while middle-wage occupation decline— 
assume current wage structure persist. Increased 
investment and productivity growth from automation 
could spur enough growth to ensure full employment, 
but only if most displace worker find new work 
within one year. If reemployment be slow, frictional 
unemployment will likely rise in the short-term and 
wage could face downward pressure. These wage 
trend be not universal: in China and other emerge 
economies, middle-wage occupation such a 
service and construction job will likely see the most 
net job growth, boost the emerge middle class. 

 To achieve good outcomes, policy maker and 
business leader will need to embrace automation’s 
benefit and, at the same time, address the worker 
transition brought about by these technologies. 
Ensuring robust demand growth and economic 
dynamism be a priority: history show that economy 
that be not expand do not generate job growth. 
Midcareer job training will be essential, a will 
enhance labor market dynamism and enable 
worker redeployment. These change will challenge 
current educational and workforce training models, a 
well a business approach to skill-building. Another 
priority be rethink and strengthen transition and 
income support for worker caught in the cross- 
current of automation.

SUMMARY OF FINDINGS 

The technology-driven world in which we live be a world fill with promise but also 
challenges. Cars that drive themselves, machine that read X-rays, and algorithm that 
respond to customer service inquiry be all manifestation of powerful new form of 
automation. Yet even a these technology increase productivity and improve our lives, 
their use will substitute for some work activity human currently perform—a development 
that have spark much public concern. 

This research build on MGI’s January 2017 report on automation and it impact on work 
activities.1 We ass the number and type of job that might be create under different 
scenario through 2030, and compare that to work that could be displace by automation.2 
The result reveal a rich mosaic of potential shift in occupation in the year ahead, with 
important implication for workforce skill and wages. The analysis cover 46 country that 
comprise almost 90 percent of global GDP. We focus on six country that span income 
level (China, Germany, India, Japan, Mexico, and the United States). For each, we model 
the potential net employment change for more than 800 occupations, base on different 
scenario for the pace of automation adoption and for future labor demand. The intent of this 
research be not to forecast. Rather, we present a set of scenario (necessarily incomplete) 
to serve a a guide, a we anticipate and prepare for the future of work. This research be by 
no mean the final word on this topic; ongoing research be required. Indeed, in Box E2 at the 
end of this summary, we highlight some of the potential limitation of the research present 
in this report. 

Our finding suggest that several trend that may serve a catalyst of future labor demand 
could create demand for million of job by 2030. These trend include care for others in 
age societies, raise energy efficiency and meeting climate challenges, produce good 
and service for the expand consume class, especially in develop countries, not to 
mention the investment in technology, infrastructure, and building need in all countries. 
Taken from another angle, we also find that a grow and dynamic economy—in part fuel 
by technology itself and it contribution to productivity—would create jobs. These job 
would result from growth in current occupation due to demand and the creation of new 
type of occupation that may not have exist before, a have happen historically. This 
job growth (jobs gained) could more than offset the job lose to automation. None of this 
will happen by itself—it will require business and government to seize opportunity to 
boost job creation and for labor market to function well. The workforce transition ahead 
will be enormous. We estimate that a many a 375 million worker globally (14 percent of 
the global workforce) will likely need to transition to new occupational category and learn 
new skills, in the event of rapid automation adoption. If their transition to new job be slow, 
unemployment could rise and dampen wage growth. 

Indeed, while this report be title Jobs lost, job gained, it could have been, Jobs lost, job 
changed, job gained; in many way a big part of this story be about how more occupation 
will change than will be lose a machine affect portion of occupation and people 
increasingly work alongside them. Societal choice will determine whether all three of these 

1 A future that works: Automation, employment, and productivity, McKinsey Global Institute, January 2017. 
2 We use the term “jobs” a shorthand for full-time equivalent worker (FTEs), and apply it to both work 

displace by automation and to new work create by future labor demand. In reality, the number of people 
work be large than the number of FTEs, a some people work part-time. Our analysis of FTEs cover both 
employee within firm a well a independent contractor and freelancers. 



Summary of finding McKinsey Global Institute2 

come workforce transition be smooth, or whether unemployment and income inequality 
rise. History show numerous example of country that have successfully ridden the wave 
of technological change by invest in their workforce and adapt policies, institutions, 
and business model to the new era. It be our hope that this report prompt leader in that 
direction once again. 

AUTOMATION COULD DISPLACE A SIGNIFICANT SHARE OF WORK GLOBALLY 
TO 2030; 15 PERCENT IS THE MIDPOINT OF OUR SCENARIO RANGE 
In our prior report on automation, we found that about half the activity people be paid to 
do globally could theoretically be automate use currently demonstrate technologies.3 
Very few occupations—less than 5 percent—consist entirely of activity that can be 
fully automated. However, in about 60 percent of occupations, at least one-third of the 
constituent activity could be automated, imply substantial workplace transformation 
and change for all workers. All this be base on our assessment of current technological 
capability—an ever evolve frontier (Exhibit E1). 

While technical feasibility of automation be important, it be not the only factor that will influence 
the pace and extent of automation adoption. Other factor include the cost of develop 
and deploy automation solution for specific us in the workplace, the labor market 
dynamic (including quality and quantity of labor and associate wages), the benefit 
of automation beyond labor substitution, and regulatory and social acceptance. Taking 
into account these factors, our new research estimate that between almost zero and 
30 percent of the hour work globally could be automate by 2030, depend on the 
speed of adoption. In this report we mainly use the midpoint of our scenario range, which be 
15 percent of current activity automated. Results differ significantly by country, reflect 
the mix of activity currently perform by worker and prevail wage rates. They range

these technology for reason other than labor cost savings, such a improve quality, efficiency, or scale, 
although worker displacement could still be a consequence. A glossary of automation technology and 
technique be in the technical appendix.

Technology adoption can and often do cause 
significant short-term labor displacement, but history 
show that, in the longer run, it creates a multitude of new 
job and unleashes demand for exist ones, more than 
offset the number of job it destroys even a it raise 
labor productivity (Exhibit E3).1 An examination of the 
historical record highlight several lessons: 

 All advanced economy have experienced 
profound sectoral shift in employment, first out of 
agriculture and more recently manufacturing, even 
a overall employment grew. In the United States, the 
agricultural share of total employment decline from 
60 percent in 1850 to less than 5 percent by 1970, 
while manufacturing fell from 26 percent of total US 
employment in 1960 to below 10 percent today. Other 
country have experienced even faster declines: one- 
third of China’s workforce move out of agriculture 
between 1990 and 2015. 

 Such shift can have painful consequence for some 
workers. During the Industrial Revolution in England, 
average real wage stagnate for decades, even a 
productivity rose.2 Eventually, wage growth caught up 
to and then surpass productivity growth. But the 
transition period be difficult for individual workers, 
and ease only after substantial policy reforms. 

 New technology have spur the creation of many 
more job than they destroyed, and some of the new 
job be in occupation that cannot be envision 
at the outset; one study found that 0.56 percent of 
new job in the United States each year be in new 
occupations.3 Most job create by technology 
be outside the technology-producing sector itself. 
We estimate that the introduction of the personal 
computer, for instance, have enable the creation of 
15.8 million net new job in the United States since 
1980, even after accounting for job displaced. About 
90 percent of these be in occupation that use the PC 
in other industries, such a call center representatives, 
financial analysts, and inventory managers. 

 Robust aggregate demand and economic growth 
be essential for job creation. New technology have 
raise productivity growth, enable firm to low 
price for consumers, pay high wages, or distribute 
profit to shareholders. This stimulates demand 
across the economy, boost job creation.4 

 Rising productivity be usually accompany by 
employment growth, because it raise income 
which be then spent, create demand for good and 
service across the economy. When there have be 
a tradeoff between employment growth and labor 
productivity growth, it have be short-lived. In the 
United States, for example, our analysis show that 
employment and productivity both grow in 95 percent 
of roll three-year period and 100 percent of roll 
10-year period since 1960. 

 Over the long term, productivity growth enable by 
technology have reduce the average hour work 
per week and allow people to enjoy more leisure 
time.5 Across advanced economies, the length 
of the average work-week have fall by nearly 
50 percent since the early 1900s, reflect shorter 
work hours, more paid day off for personal 
time and vacations, and the recent rise of part-time 
work. The growth in leisure have create demand 
for new industries, from golf to video game to 
home improvement. 

Although the historical record be largely reassuring, 
some people worry that automation today will be 
more disruptive than in the past. Technology expert 
and economist be debate whether “this time, 
thing be different” (and we examine that debate 
start on page 48 of this report). Our current 
view be that the answer depends on the time horizon 
consider (decades or centuries) and on the pace of 
future technological progress and adoption. On many 
dimensions, we find similarity between the scope and 
effect of automation today compare to early wave of 
technology disruption, go back to the Industrial

Revolution. However, automation go forward might 
prove to be more disruptive than in recent decades— 
and on par with the most rapid change in the past—in 
two ways. First, if technological advance continue

Summary of finding McKinsey Global Institute6 

The potential impact of automation on employment varies by occupation and sector. 
Activities most susceptible to automation include physical one in predictable environments, 
such a operating machinery and prepare fast food. Collecting and processing data 
be two other category of activity that can increasingly be do good and faster with 
machines. This could displace large amount of labor, for instance in mortgage origination, 
paralegal work, accounting, and back-office transaction processing. It be important to note, 
however, that even when some task be automated, employment in those occupation 
may not decline, but rather worker may perform new tasks. In addition, employment in 
occupation may also grow, if the overall demand for that occupation grows enough to 
overwhelm the rate of automation. 

Automation will have a lesser effect on job that involve manage people, apply 
expertise, and those involve social interactions, where machine be unable to match 
human performance for now. Jobs in unpredictable environments—occupations such a 
gardeners, plumbers, or provider of child- and elder-care—will also generally see less 
automation by 2030, because they be difficult to automate technically and often command 
relatively low wages, which make automation a less attractive business proposition. 

RISING INCOMES, INVESTMENTS IN INFRASTRUCTURE AND ENERGY, AND 
OTHER CATALYSTS COULD POTENTIALLY CREATE MILLIONS OF NEW JOBS 
While automation’s displacement of labor have be visible for many years, it be more difficult 
to envision all the new job that will be created. Many of these new job be create indirectly 
and spread across different sector and geographies. 

In this report, we model some potential source of new labor demand that may spur job 
creation to 2030, even net of automation. We consider two scenarios, a “trendline” scenario 
base on current spending and investment trend observe across countries, and a “step- 
up” scenario that assumes additional investment in some areas. We calculate job (full-time 
equivalents) that could be create both directly and indirectly for more than 800 exist 
occupations. We do not consider the dynamic interaction between trend or across the 
economy (Exhibit E4). The result be not precise forecast of future job growth, but rather 
be suggestive of where job of the future may be. 

For three trends, we model only a trendline scenario. They are: 

 Rising income and consumption, especially in emerge economies. Previous 
MGI research have estimate that 1 billion more people will enter the consume class by 
2025.4 Using external macroeconomic forecasts, we estimate that global consumption 
could grow by $23 trillion between 2015 and 2030, and most of this will come from the 
expand consume class in emerge economies. As income rise, consumer 
spend more on all categories. But their spending pattern also shift, create more job 
in area such a consumer durables, leisure activities, financial and telecommunication 
services, housing, health care, and education. The effect of these new consumer will 
be felt not just in the country where the income be generated, but also in economy that 
export to those countries.5 Globally, we estimate that 300 million to 365 million new job 
could be create from the impact of rise incomes. 

 Aging populations. By 2030, there will be at least 300 million more people age 
65 year and above than there be in 2014. As people age, their spending pattern

5 We assume that current pattern of global trade continue, at the same level relative to GDP a today. As a

for example, could gain up to 3 percent of net new job from rise income by 2030 from net exports. In

For three other trends, we model both a trendline scenario and a step-up scenario; the latter 
be base on explicit choice that could be make by governments, business leaders, and 
individual to create additional jobs. 

 Investment in infrastructure and buildings. Infrastructure and building be two 
area of historic underspend that may create significant additional labor demand 
if action be take to bridge infrastructure gap and overcome housing shortages. MGI 
have estimate that the world need to invest about 3.8 percent of GDP annually, or an 
average of $3.3 trillion per year to fill infrastructure gaps, compare with $2.5 trillion 
currently.7 This include both develop country that be urbanize and industrializing, 
and advanced economy that have underinvested in maintain their infrastructure 
and buildings. Rising income also create demand for more and high quality buildings. 
Both factor could create new demand, mainly in the construction sector, for up to 
80 million job in the trendline scenario and, in some cases, potentially up to 200 million 
globally in the step-up scenario.8 These job include architects, engineers, carpenter 
and other skilled tradespeople, a well a construction workers, machinery operator 
and other job with low skill requirements. 

 Investments in renewable energy, energy efficiency, and climate adaptation. 
Investments in renewable energy, such a wind and solar, energy efficiency 
technologies, and adaptation and mitigation of climate change may create new demand 
for worker in a range of occupations, include in manufacturing, construction, and 
installation. In our trendline scenario, we model future job growth base on already- 
announce policy intention for energy efficiency and the require investment to meet 
these goals.9 For a step-up scenario, we use more ambitious target that country 
will need to get closer to meeting commitment to the Paris climate accord.10 These 
investment could create up to ten million new job in the trendline scenario, and up to 
ten million additional job globally in the step-up scenario. 

 “Marketization” of previously unpaid domestic work. The last trend we consider 
be the potential to pay for service that substitute for currently unpaid and primarily 
domestic work—including cooking, childcare, and cleaning. This so-called marketization 
of previously unpaid work be already prevalent in advanced economies, and rise female 
labor force participation worldwide could accelerate the trend. About 75 percent of the 
world’s total unpaid care be undertaken by woman and amount to a much a $10 trillion 
of output per year, roughly equivalent to 13 percent of global GDP.11 Individual decision 
within the household to use paid service or government investment to provide universal 
childcare and pre-school could fuel this development. We consider this in the step-up 
scenario only, a it magnitude and timing be unclear. But we estimate that this shift could 
marketize 50 million to 90 million unpaid job globally, mainly in occupation such a 
childcare, early childhood education, cleaning, cooking, and gardening.

UP TO 375 MILLION PEOPLE MAY NEED TO SWITCH OCCUPATIONAL 
CATEGORIES, WITH THE HIGHEST SHARE IN ADVANCED ECONOMIES 
When we look at the net change in job growth and decline from the trend described 
above compare with the work that can be automated, a mosaic of shift in occupation 
and job category emerges (Exhibit E5). 

Across all countries, the category with the high percentage job growth net of 
automation include health-care providers; professional such a engineers, scientists, 
accountants, and analysts; IT professional and other technology specialists; manager and 
executives, whose work cannot easily be replace by machines; educators, especially in 
emerge economy with young populations; and “creatives,” a small but grow category 
of artists, performers, and entertainer who will be in demand a rise income create 
more demand for leisure and recreation. Builders and related profession will also grow, 
particularly in the step-up scenario that involves high investment in infrastructure and 
buildings. Manual and service job in unpredictable environment will also grow, such a 
home health aide and gardeners. 

Advanced economy may also see employment decline in occupation that be most 
susceptible to automation. These include office support occupations, such a record 
clerks, office assistants, and finance and accounting; some customer interaction jobs, 
such a hotel and travel workers, cashiers, and food service workers; and a wide range of 
job carry out in predictable settings, such a assembly line workers, dishwashers, food 
preparation workers, drivers, and agricultural and other equipment operators. Helping 
individual transition from the decline occupation to grow one will be a large- 
scale challenge. 

The come workforce transition among occupation could be very large 
The change in net occupational growth or decline imply that a very large number of people 
may need to shift occupational category and learn new skill in the year ahead. The shift 
could be on a scale not see since the transition of the labor force out of agriculture in the 
early 1900s in the United States and Europe, and more recently in China. But unlike those 
early transitions, in which young people left farm and move to city for industrial jobs, 
the challenge, especially in advanced economies, will be to retrain midcareer workers. 
There be few precedent in which society have successfully retrain such large number 
of people. Frictions in the labor markets—including cultural norm regard gender 
stereotype in work and geographic mismatch between worker and jobs—could also 
impede the transition.12

We estimate that between 400 million and 800 million individual could be displace by

and early (that is, the most rapid) automation adoption scenarios. We think demand for 
job will be there, base on our scenario of future labor demand and the net impact of 
automation, a described in the next section. However people will need to find their way into 
these jobs. Of the total displaced, 75 million to 375 million may need to switch occupational 
category and learn new skills, under our midpoint and early automation adoption 
scenario (Exhibit E6).13 Under the late adoption scenario (that is, the slowest), this number 
would be far lower, below 10 million. Given the minimal impact on the workforce of this 
edge-case scenario, we have not highlight it in the exhibit in this report. In absolute 
terms, China face the large number of worker need to switch occupations—up to 
100 million if automation be adopt rapidly, or 12 percent of the 2030 workforce—although 
this figure be relatively small compare with the huge shift in China out of agriculture in the 
past 25 years. For advanced economies, the share of the workforce that may need to learn 
new skill and find work in new occupation be much higher: up to one-third of the 2030 
workforce in the United States and Germany, and nearly half in Japan.

WILL THERE BE ENOUGH WORK IN THE FUTURE? 
Today there be grow concern about whether there will be enough job for worker give 
potential automation. History would suggest that such fear may be unfounded: over time, 
labor market adjust to change in demand for worker from technological disruptions, 
although at time with depressed real wages. We address this question about the future 
of work through two different set of analyses: one base on model of a limited number 
of catalyst of new labor demand and automation described above, and one use a 
macroeconomic model of the economy that incorporates the dynamic interaction 
among variables. We also note that if history be any guide, we could expect 8 to 9 percent 
of 2030 labor demand will be in new type of occupation that have not exist before.14 
Both analysis lead u to conclude that, with sufficient economic growth, innovation, and 
investment, there can be enough new job creation to offset the impact of automation, 
although in some advanced economy additional investment will be need a per our 
step-up scenario to reduce the risk of job shortages. But a large challenge will be ensure 
that worker have the skill and support need to transition to new jobs. Countries that fail 
to manage this transition could see rise unemployment and depressed wages. 

Future job lose and job gain vary by country, with the large disruption 
expect in advanced economy 
The magnitude of future job creation from the trend described above and the impact of 
automation on the workforce vary significantly by country, depend on four factors: 

 Wage levels. Higher wage make the business case for automation adoption stronger. 
However, low-wage country may be affected a well, if company adopt automation 
to boost quality, achieve tighter production control, move production closer to end 
consumer in high-wage countries, or other benefit beyond reduce labor costs. Some 
economist worry about “premature deindustrialization” in develop country due 
to automation.15 

 Demand growth. Economic growth be essential for job creation; economy that be 
stagnant or grow slowly create few if any net new jobs. Countries with strong 
economic and productivity growth and innovation will therefore be expect to 
experience more new labor demand, although the amount and nature of job creation will 
vary depend on the sector that drive growth. 

 Demographics. Demographics affect both labor demand and labor supply. Countries 
with a rapidly-growing workforce, such a India, may enjoy a “demographic dividend” 
that boost GDP growth—if young people be employed. Countries with a shrink 
workforce, such a Japan, can expect low future GDP growth, derive only from 
productivity growth. However, country with a decline workforce need automation to 
offset their shrink labor supply, while country with grow workforce have great 
job creation challenges. 

 Mix of economic sector and occupations. The automation potential for country 
reflect the mix of economic sector and the mix of job within each sector. Japan, for 
example, have a high technical automation potential than the United States because 
the weight of sector that be highly automatable, such a manufacturing, be higher. And 
within Japanese manufacturing, a large proportion of job involve activity that can be 
more easily automated, such a production, than in the United States. 

14 Ibid. Jeffrey Lin, “Technological adaptation,” May 2011. 
15 For instance, see Dani Rodrik, “Premature deindustrialization,” Journal of Economic Growth, volume 21, 

number 1, 2016. 



13Jobs lost, job gained: Workforce transition in a time of automationMcKinsey Global Institute 

These factor combine to create different outlook for the future of work in each country 
(Exhibit E7). For instance, Japan be rich but it economy be project to grow slowly to 2030. 
It face the combination of slow job creation come from economic expansion and a 
large share of work that can be automate a a result of high wage and the structure of it 
economy. However, Japan will also see it workforce shrink by 2030 by four million people. 
In the step-up scenario, and consider the job in new occupation we cannot envision 
today, Japan’s net change in job could be roughly in balance. 

Like Japan, the United States and Germany could also face significant workforce 
displacement from automation by 2030, but their project future growth—and hence 
new job creation—is higher. The United States have a grow workforce and, in the step- 
up scenario, with innovation lead to new type of occupation and work, Germany’s 
workforce will decline by three million by 2030, and it will have more than enough labor 
demand to employ all workers. 

At the other extreme be India: a fast-growing develop country with relatively modest 
potential for automation over the next 15 years, reflect low wage rates. Our analysis find 
that most occupational category be project to grow in India, reflect it potential for 
strong economic expansion. However, India’s labor force be expect to grow by 138 million 
people by 2030, or about 30 percent. Employing these new entrant in formal sector job 
will require job creation on a much large scale than in the past. Automation will make this 
challenge more difficult; some fear “jobless growth.”16 However, our analysis suggests 
that India can create enough new job to offset automation and employ new entrants, if it 
undertakes the investment in our step-up scenario. 

China and Mexico have high wage than India, and so be likely to see more automation. 
China be still project to have robust economic growth and will have a shrink workforce; 
like Germany, China’s problem could be a shortage of workers. Mexico’s project rate of 
future economic expansion be more modest, and it workforce will grow by 15 million by 
2030. Like the United States and Japan, our result suggest that Mexico could benefit from 
the job creation in the step-up scenario plus innovation in new occupation and activity to 
make full use of it workforce.

15Jobs lost, job gained: Workforce transition in a time of automationMcKinsey Global Institute 

If displace worker be not reemployed quickly, country will face rise 
unemployment and depressed wage 
To model the impact of automation on overall employment and wages, we use a general 
equilibrium model of the economy of our six focus country that take into account the 
economic impact of automation and dynamic interactions.17 The model be not intend to 
forecast the future, but rather be a tool to explore the implication of different scenarios. 

Automation have at least three distinct economic impacts. Most attention have be devote 
to the potential displacement of labor. But automation also may raise labor productivity: 
firm only adopt automation when do so enables them to produce more or higher-quality 
output with the same or few input (including material, energy, and labor inputs). The third 
impact be that automation adoption raise investment in the economy, lift short-term GDP 
growth. We model all three effects.18 We also create different scenario for how quickly 
displace worker find new employment, base on historical data. 

The result reveal that across different rate of re-employment, our six country could 
expect to be at or very near full employment by 2030. Consistent with the historical 
experience, labor market adjust to technological shocks. However, the model also 
illustrates the importance of reemploying displace worker quickly. If displace worker 
be able to be reemployed within one year, our model show automation lift the overall 
economy: full employment be maintain in both the short and long-term, wage grow 
faster than in the baseline model, and productivity be higher. However, in scenario in which 
some displace worker take year to find new work, unemployment rise in the short- to 
medium-term. The labor market adjusts over time and unemployment falls—but with slow 
average wage growth. In these scenarios, average wage end up low in 2030 than in 
the baseline model, which could dampen aggregate demand and long-term growth. The 
pace of reemployment will be influence by the effectiveness of retraining, the capacity of 
company to innovate and, in some sectors, the elasticity of demand. 

WORKERS WILL REQUIRE DIFFERENT SKILLS, AND WAGE POLARIZATION IN 
ADVANCED COUNTRIES COULD CONTINUE 
In all six of our focus countries, we find that in general, the current educational requirement 
of the occupation that may grow be high than those for the job displace by 
automation. In advanced economies, occupation that currently require only a secondary 
education or less see a net decline from automation, while those occupation require 
college degree and high grow. In India and other emerge economies, we find high 
labor demand for all education levels, with the large number of new job in occupation 
require a secondary education but the fast rate of job growth will be for occupation 
currently require a college or advanced degree (Exhibit E8). For all countries, increase 
investment in education and workforce training will be a priority. 

Moreover, we find that worker of the future will spend more time on activity that machine 
be less capable of, such a manage people, apply expertise, and communicate 
with others. They will spend less time on predictable physical activities, and on collect 
and processing data, where machine already exceed human performance. The skill and 
capability require will also shift, require more social and emotional skills, and more 
advanced cognitive capabilities, such a logical reason and creativity.

midpoint adoption scenario. We make a conservative assumption on the productivity impact of automation, 
that firm produce the same value of output a prior to automation but with few workers. See technical 
appendix for more detail.

Wage polarization could be exacerbate in advanced economy but develop 
country will see a grow middle class 
Wages may stagnate or fall in decline occupations. Although we do not model shift in 
relative wage across occupations, the basic economics of labor supply and demand 
suggests that this should be the case for occupation in which labor demand declines. 
Since 1980, most advanced economy have see an overall decline share of national 
income be capture by labor (compared with capital). Recent academic work suggests 
that technological change be one reason for this decline.19 

Our analysis, look at change in employment by occupation at today’s relative wage 
levels, show that most job growth in the United States and other advanced economy 
will be in occupation currently at the high end of the wage distribution. Some occupation 
that be currently low-wage, such a nursing assistant and teach assistants, will 
also increase, while a wide range of middle-income occupation will have the large 
employment declines. These result suggest that income polarization could continue. 
Policy choice we identify in our step-up scenario, such a increase investment in 
infrastructure, buildings, and energy transition could help create additional demand for 
middle-wage job such a construction worker in advanced economies. 

The wage trend picture be quite different in emerge economy such a China and India, 
where our scenario show that middle-wage job such a retail salesperson and teacher 
will grow quickly a these economy develop. This implies that their consume class will 
continue to grow in the decade ahead. However, our analysis come with several important 
caveat (see Box E2, “What could overstate or understate the impact scenario assess in 
this research—and what we have not considered”). 

BUSINESSES AND POLICYMAKERS WILL NEED TO ACT TO KEEP PEOPLE 
WORKING AS AUTOMATION IS ADOPTED 
The benefit of AI and automation to user and businesses, and the economic growth that 
could come via their productivity contributions, be compelling. They will not only contribute 
to dynamic economy that create jobs, but also help create the economic surplus that 
will enable society to address the workforce transition that will likely happen regardless. 
Faced with the scale of worker transition we have described, one reaction could be to try 
to slow the pace and scope of adoption in an attempt to preserve the status quo. While this 
may limit the workforce transitions, it would affect the contribution that these technology 
make to business dynamism and economic growth, via the contribution to productivity 
growth, and which in turn lead to job growth and prosperity. We should embrace these 
technology but also address the workforce transition and challenge they bring. In many 
countries, this may require an initiative on the scale of the Marshall Plan involve sustain 
investment, new training models, program to ease worker transitions, income support, and 
collaboration between the public and private sectors. 

Achieving the benefit of deploy automation, such a productivity growth, while 
address it challenges, be not impossible. During the transition out of agriculture, for 
example, the United States make a major investment in expand secondary education, 
and for the first time require all student to attend. Called the High School Movement, 
this raise the rate of high school enrolment of 14- to 17-year-olds from 18 percent in 1910

Policy makers, business leaders, and individual worker all have constructive and important

the globe, when face with monumental challenges, often rise to the occasion for the well- 
be of their citizens. Yet over the last few decades, investment and policy to support 
the workforce have eroded. Public spending on labor force training and support have fall in 
most OECD countries, and corporate spending on training have decline in the United States 
(Exhibit E9). Educational model have not fundamentally change in 100 years; we still 
use system design for an industrial society to prepare student for a rapidly-changing 
knowledge economy. It be now critical to reverse these trends, with government make 
workforce transition and job creation a more urgent priority.

19Jobs lost, job gained: Workforce transition in a time of automationMcKinsey Global Institute 

Today, while policy choice will vary by country, all society will need to address four key 
area to smooth the loom workforce transitions: 

 Maintaining robust economic growth to support job creation. Sustaining robust 
aggregate demand growth be critical to support new job creation, a be support for 
new business formation. Fiscal and monetary policy that ensure sufficient aggregate 
demand, a well a support for business investment and innovation, will be essential. 
Targeted initiative in certain sector could also help, include by increase investment 
in infrastructure and energy transitions, a well a policy to enable a shift of unpaid 
household work such a childcare to the market, a discuss in our step-up scenario. 

 Scaling and reimagining job retrain and workforce skill development. Providing 
job retrain and enable individual to learn marketable new skill throughout their 
lifetime will be a critical challenge—and for some countries, the central challenge. 
Midcareer retrain will become ever more important a the skill mix need for a 
successful career changes. A range of initiative in country from Sweden to Singapore 
may point the way to new approach to improve skill or teach new ones, include 
to old workers. Governments can play an important role here, a the US government 
do in previous era with the GI Bill, which enable just under eight million veteran 
return from war to go to college or be retrained.21 Programs that can more quickly 
retool the labor force by focus on re-training and credentialing at the level of skill 
in demand rather than multi-year degree could be important. Business can take a 
lead in some areas, include with on-the-job training and provide opportunity to 
worker to upgrade their skills, both through in-house training and partnership with 
education providers. 

 Improving business and labor market dynamism include mobility. Greater fluidity 
will be need in the labor market to manage the difficult transition we anticipate. This 
include restore now-waning geographic mobility in advanced economy include 
the United States. Digital talent platform and the rise of the “gig” economy can foster 
fluidity, by match worker and company seek their skills, and by provide a 
plethora of new work opportunity for those open to take them.22 Policy maker 
in country with relatively inflexible labor market can learn from others that have 
deregulated, such a Germany, which transform it federal unemployment agency into 
a powerful job-matching entity. Governments may also update labor market regulation 
to ensure that gig economy job be not subject to discrimination, and that remain 
uncertainty about worker benefit be resolved. 

 Providing income and transition support to workers. Income support and other 
form of transition assistance to help displace worker find gainful employment will 
be essential. Beyond retraining, a range of policy can help, include unemployment 
insurance, public assistance in find work, and portable benefit that follow worker 
between jobs. We know from history and from our analysis that wage for many 
occupation can be depressed for some time during workforce transitions. More 
permanent policy to supplement work income might be need to support aggregate 
demand and ensure societal fairness. Possible solution to supplement incomes, such 
a more comprehensive minimum wage policies, universal basic income, or wage gain 
tie to productivity, be all be explored.

Business leader have much to gain by early adoption of automation technologies, 
enable performance benefit such a quality and speed, a well a great efficiency 
and productive use of all factor of production. Businesses will be on the front line of the 
workplace a it changes. That will require them to both retool their business process 
and re-evaluate their talent strategy and workforce needs, carefully consider which 
individual be needed, which can be redeployed to other jobs, and where new talent may 
be needed. Many company be find it be in their self-interest—as well a important for 
societal responsibility—to train and prepare worker for a new world of work. 

Individuals, too, will need to be prepared for a rapidly evolve future of work. Acquiring new 
skill that be in demand and reset intuition about the world of work will be critical for 
their own well-being. There will be demand for human labor, but worker everywhere will 
need to rethink traditional notion of where they work, how they work, and what talent and 
capability they bring to that work. Ultimately, we will all need creative vision for how our 
life be organize and value in the future, in a world where the role and meaning of work 
start to shift. 

••• 

Automation represent both hope and challenge. The global economy need the boost to 
productivity and growth that it will bring, especially at a time when age population be 
act a a drag on GDP growth. Machines can take on work that be routine, dangerous, or 
dirty, and may allow u all to use our intrinsically human talent more fully. But to capture 
these benefits, society will need to prepare for complex workforce transition ahead. For 
policy makers, business leaders, and individual worker the world over, the task at hand 
be to prepare for a more automate future by emphasize new skills, scale up training, 
especially for midcareer workers, and ensure robust economic growth.

Box E2. What could overstate or understate the impact scenario assess in this research— 
and what we have not consider 

1 See Artificial intelligence: The next digital frontier? McKinsey Global Institute, June 2017; Digital America: A tale of the have and have-mores, 
McKinsey Global institute, December 2015, and Digital Europe: Pushing the frontier, capture the benefits, McKinsey Global Institute, June 
2016. 

2 James Bessen, Learning by doing: The real connection between innovation, wages, and wealth, Yale University Press, 2015. 
3 For a discussion of skill bias, see David H. Autor, Frank Levy, and Richard J. Murnane, “The skill content of recent technological change: An 

empirical exploration,” The Quarterly Journal of Economics, volume 18, number 4, November 2003. 

We analyze scenario for the net impact of automation 
and future labor demand on employment, skills, and 
wages. Most of them suggest that, while there will be 
enough work to maintain full employment in the long 
term, ensure that displace worker have the skill and 
support need to obtain the new job will be critical. 
If worker be not re-employed quickly, the impact on 
wage growth could be negative. This conclusion could 
overstate or understate the impact. 

On the one hand, the future disruption could be small 
than we anticipate for several reasons: 

 Adopting automation require significant investment 
and redesign of business processes, and company 
have be slow to adopt digital technologies, let alone 
recent form of AI and automation.1 In our slowest 
automation adoption scenario, less than 5 percent of 
work be automate by 2030, so the overall impact on 
the economy could be minimal. 

 In our analysis, we make the strong assumption that 
every hour of work that be automate result in one 
hour less of work for a full-time equivalent employee. 
But company often choose to redefine occupations, 
or redeploy some worker instead. For instance, after 
the introduction of the ATM, the number of bank teller 
in the United States continued to grow for many years, 
even a the activity they perform changed.2 

 Our model of the seven catalyst of labor demand 
do not take into account dynamic effect within the 
economy, and they represent only a partial list of future 
source of labor demand. If automation adoption be 
rapid, future productivity growth could be high than 
we model, and this could raise income and result in 
more job creation than we anticipate. This could offset 
the labor displacement, even during the transition. 

On the other hand, the impact of automation on 
work could be more disruptive than we anticipate for 
several reasons: 

 The development of automation technologies, 
include AI, could accelerate or break through new 
frontiers. AI researcher today say that machine 
learn have unlocked more rapid improvement in 
the technology than could have be imagine even a 
few year ago. Improvements in machine capability 

in area such a natural language understand 
and generation could mean that more work might be 
automate more rapidly than we estimate here. 

 While we assume that wage level will play a major 
role in determine automation adoption, company 
may also adopt these technology for other reasons, 
include their capacity to exceed human performance 
capability in some areas. This would mean more 
rapid automation adoption than we model, particularly 
in low-wage economy and for low-wage work in 
advanced economies. 

 Displaced worker might not find new work quickly, 
or at all, because they lack the skill or educational 
requirements, or because other barrier such a 
cultural preference or geographic mobility stand 
in their way. There be few example of large-scale 
retrain and redeployment of midcareer workers. 
Moreover, labor market may not work a well a 
they need to do to help displace worker find 
new employment. 

 The assumption we make on future consumption 
growth and spending on infrastructure and building 
might be too optimistic. In the past decade, actual 
GDP growth in nearly all advanced economy have 
be low than forecast. Continued sluggish growth, 
rise geopolitical tensions, or a new recession could 
make our future job creation scenario too optimistic. 

A number of other caveat to our finding should also be 
noted. We have not make assumption in our model 
about sector trends, such a the growth of ecommerce 
in retailing, or the impact of fiscal constraint on public 
sector employment. We also do not model change in 
work structure, such a the growth of the gig economy, 
or activity within an occupation that could change 
a a result of technological innovation. Our analysis of 
wage trend be base on current average wage for each 
occupation in each country, and we do not model wage 
over time by occupation base on the dynamic of labor 
supply and demand. Finally, we do not model change 
skill requirement for occupation or analyze the “skill 
bias” of automation technologies, that is, whether they will 
enable high-skill worker at the expense of low-skill ones, 
or vice-versa.3

We live in an age when machine answer customer inquiries, help doctor understand 
X-rays, lip-read good than human professionals, and sort trash into compost and 
recyclables—an age, too, when the public debate about automation and it impact on the 
workplace raise anxious questions. First be the existential one about the future of work 
itself. Given rapid advance in technology include robotics and artificial intelligence, will 
there be enough work to ensure full employment? Second be the question about what those 
job will be, and which skill will be need for them. The third be what all this could mean 
for wages. 

We seek to address these question in this report through, first, an analysis of automation 
potential and scenario about the extent of adoption of current work activity by 2030 
and, second, an analysis of potential future labor demand. We look at 46 countries, 
represent almost 90 percent of global GDP. To illustrate the potential impact at a country 
level, we use six a exemplar of country that vary by sector and occupation mix, GDP per 
caput growth, wage rates, and demographics: China, Germany, India, Japan, Mexico, and 
the United States.23 

In this chapter, we focus on automation’s potential to transform the workplace. Building 
on our previous automation work, we create a range for the number of hour that could be 
displace by automation by 2030, and seek to identify the work activities, occupations, and 
sector that be most—and least—susceptible to be automate (see Box 1, “Automation’s 
rapid advance and it limitations—for now”).24 

Among the finding of our new research be that a much a 30 percent of the hour 
work globally could be automate by 2030, depend on the speed of adoption, with 
15 percent of current work activity be automate in our midpoint scenario. The speed 
of adoption depends on factor include technical feasibility, the pace of technology 
development, costs, and social and regulatory acceptance. These result differ significantly 
by country, reflect the mix of activity currently perform by worker and prevail 
wage rates, range from 9 percent in India to 26 percent in Japan in the midpoint adoption 
rate scenario.

Automation be not a new phenomenon; industrial robot 
have be a fixture on factory floor for several decades, 
and software algorithm help logistics company 
optimize the route planning of delivery in a faster and 
more efficient manner than human route planner could. 

Recent development in robotics, artificial intelligence, 
and machine learn be noteworthy for the advance 
they represent, however. We be on the cusp of a new 
automation age in which technology not only do thing 
we thought only human could do, but can increasingly 
do them at a superhuman level. In just the past year, 
a project by Google’s DeepMind and the University of 
Oxford have apply deep learn to a huge data set 
of BBC program to create a lip-reading system that 
be substantially more proficient than a professional 
human lip-reader.1 Researchers at Stanford University 
have developed a deep learn system that be able 
to diagnose pneumonia from chest x-ray good than 
expert radiologist work alone.2 Robot “skin” make of 
a piezotronic transistor mesh developed by the Georgia 
Institute of Technology and cover in thousand of 
mechanical hair can “feel” texture and find object by 
touch.3 Companies be use advanced facial analysis to 
monitor emotional response to advertisement and other 
digital medium content, via a webcam.4 

AI be already be deployed in synthetic biology, cancer 
research, climate science, and material science. For 
example, researcher at Vanderbilt university have 
use computer to exceed the human standard in 
predict the most effective treatment for major 
depressive disorder and eventual outcome of breast 
cancer patients.5 

Three factor be drive the technological advances: 

 Machine-learning algorithm have progress in 
recent years, especially through the development of 
deep learn and reinforcement-learning technique 
base on neural networks. 

 Computing capacity be increase exponentially 
and have become available to train large and more 
complex model much faster. Graphics processing 
units, originally design to render the computer 
graphic in video games, have be repurposed to 
execute the data and algorithm crunch require for 
machine learn at speed many time faster than 
traditional processor chips. This compute capacity 
have be aggregate in hyper-scalable data center 
and make accessible to user through the cloud. 

 Vast amount of data that can be use to train 
machine learn model be be generated, for 
example through daily creation of billion of images, 
online click streams, voice and video, mobile 
locations, and sensor embed in the Internet 
of Things. 

Formidable technical challenge still lie ahead. While 
machine can be train to perform a range of cognitive 
tasks, they remain limited. They be not yet good at 
put knowledge into context, let alone improvising, and 
they have little of the common sense that be the essence of 
human experience and emotion. They struggle to operate 
without a pre-defined methodology. They can replicate 
fugue in the style of Bach, but cannot yet understand 
sarcasm or love.6 

One of the big remain technical challenge be 
mastery of natural language processing—understanding 
and generate speech. These capability be 
indispensable for numerous work activity but, despite 
great progress in area such a machine translation, 
machine still have far to go to achieve human level 
of performance. 

Beyond the development of technology, much work 
remains to be do integrate different capability into 
holistic solution in which everything work together 
seamlessly. Combining a range of technology will be 
essential for workplace automation, but engineering 
such solutions—whether for hardware or software—is a 
difficult process.

AUTOMATION CAN RAISE THE PRODUCTIVITY OF THE GLOBAL ECONOMY 
BUT WILL AFFECT EMPLOYMENT: A BRIEF RECAP OF OUR PRIOR RESEARCH 
In our January 2017 report, A future that works: Automation, employment, and productivity, 
we note that automation technology such a advanced robotics and artificial intelligence 
be powerful driver of productivity and economic growth which can help create economic 
surplus and increase overall societal prosperity. Key finding of that report include: 

 Automation could accelerate the productivity of the global economy by between 0.8 and 
1.4 percent of global GDP annually, assume that human labor replace by automation 
rejoins the workforce and be a productive a it be in 2014. Automation on it own will 
not be sufficient to achieve long-term economic growth aspiration across the world; 
for that, additional productivity-boosting measure will be needed, include rework 
business process or develop new product and services. Nonetheless, the 
productivity growth enable by automation can ensure continued prosperity in age 
nation and provide an additional boost to fast-growing ones.25 

 For companies, the deployment of automation can deliver benefit in the form of labor 
cost savings, but also in myriad other performance-enhancing ways. It can enable firm 
to get closer to customer and predict maintenance needs, sharply reduce the cost of 
operation in some activity and extend the life of exist capital assets. Automation 
can also increase scale and speed. Nissan, for example, have halve the time it take 
to move from final product design to production thanks to an automate system, while 
BMW have reduce machine downtime by 30 to 40 percent—effectively generate fresh 
economy of scale with minimal investment—through AI-enabled condition-based 
maintenance.26 Exhibit 1 compare the estimate potential performance and labor cost 
reduction benefit from automation for a number of key process within some sectors. 
These finding be base on estimate of potential in case study inform by our work 
with industry. 

 Overall, our analysis suggest that roughly 50 percent of the time spent on activity 
that people be paid almost $15 trillion to do in the global economy have the theoretical 
potential to be automate by adapt currently demonstrate technology—in other 
words, the technical capability already exist, although an integrate solution to 
automate each particular activity might not yet have be developed nor deployed. We 
estimate the potential for technology to automate the more than 2,000 work activity 
in about 800 occupation across the economy, by adapt currently demonstrate 
technologies. (We examine work activity individually rather than whole occupations, 
since occupation consist of a range of activity with different potential for automation). 
Certain category of activity be more susceptible to automation than others.27 While 
less than 5 percent of occupation can be fully automated, about 60 percent have 
at least 30 percent of activity that can technically be automate (see illustration, 
“Automation: A global force that will transform economy and the workforce”). 

 Our automation analysis found significant variation among sector of the economy, 
and among the occupation within those sectors. For example, almost one-fifth of 
the time spent in US workplace involves predictable physical activity and be prevalent 
in such sector a manufacturing and retail trade. Accordingly, these sector have a 
relatively high technical potential for automation by adapt currently demonstrate 
technologies. Even within sectors, there be considerable variation. In manufacturing, for

 Technical feasibility be an essential element of automation but four other factor also

adoption and the impact on employment to 2030 can vary among countries. The other 
factor be economic and social: the cost of develop and deploy automation 
solution for the workplace—that be actually develop integrate solution for 
specific use cases; labor market dynamic include the supply, demand, and cost of 
human labor; the net economic benefit of automation, which include performance 
benefit beyond labor substitution such a high throughput, raise productivity, and 
heighten safety; and regulatory and social acceptance. Labor market dynamic in 
particular play an important role in the national variation around automation adoption, 
since wage rate vary widely by country, even for similar occupations. The relative cost 
of automation compare with the cost of labor will affect adoption: if qualify worker 
be in abundant supply and significantly less expensive than automation, this could be 
a decisive argument against automation in that situation. Consequently, in the period 
to 2030, we expect advanced economies, with wage level that be relatively higher, to 
adopt automation early than many emerge economies, especially if adoption require 
expensive hardware solutions. That said, our automation model do consider the 
continue improvement in automation technologies’ capability over time, a well a 
decrease costs.

Our automation model applies the factor list above to a range of scenarios, bookended

may turn out to be extreme. However, they do enable u to model a spectrum of outcomes. 

While about half of all work activity globally have the technical potential to be automate 
by adapt currently demonstrate technologies, accord to our prior research on 
automation, this will not happen overnight. Taking into account the technical, economic and 
social factor affect the pace and extent of automation, described above, the proportion 
of work actually displace by 2030 will likely be lower. We estimate that up to 30 percent 
of current work activity could be displace by 2030, with a midpoint of 15 percent, or the 
hour of about 400 million full-time equivalents. Indeed, the range of outcome be particularly 
wide in 2030 in our model; in the event of late automation adoption, the percentage of 
work activity displace by 2030 would be close to zero. Among countries, too, especially 
between advanced economy and emerge ones, the range be wide. Exhibit 2 highlight 
both how we arrive at our range of automation scenarios, and the model we use

For the six country we use a exemplar in this report, the hour that could be automate

Japan to 9 percent in India (Exhibit 3). Developed country Germany (24 percent) and the 
United States (23 percent) be not far behind Japan, follow by China (16 percent) and 
Mexico (13 percent). In general, wage rate be the big determinant of the difference 
in automation scenario among countries; high wage rate make automation more 
economically attractive. In addition, the mix of activities, which be related to the mix of sector 
and occupations, also affect the model rate of automation. Among the main difference 
between emerge and advanced economy be the importance of agriculture in the former. 
In Germany and Japan, manufacturing also have high automation potential. That said, 
emerge economy could leverage automation technology aggressively in an effort to 
leapfrog their economic development. China, for example, have few robot per worker than 
the global average, but receive nearly one-third of all robot shipment in 2016.28

account for the large majority of them.

1. Jobs lost, job changed: Impact of automation on work McKinsey Global Institute30 

Automation will displace worker with different educational attainment across 
a wide range of occupation 
Just a there be a wide variation in automation’s impact on country and sectors, so, too, it 
effect on specific occupation will vary. Those profession highly dependent on the work 
activity we identify a most susceptible to automation—physical work in a predictable 
environment, or data collection and processing—are likely to be the most affected, 
especially if automation adoption occurs earlier, which we anticipate to be the case in 
country with high wage such a Japan, Germany, and other advanced economies. By 
comparison, occupation that require application of expertise, interaction with stakeholders, 
management and coach of others, or a high degree of social and emotional response will 
be less susceptible to automation in the period to 2030. 

Occupations incorporate significant amount of physical work in predictable environment 
include production worker and building and ground cleaners, a well a office support 
(such a clerk and administrative assistants), be likely to face significant displacement 
of their activity by automation, while doctors, health aides, and other care provider and 
professional include engineer and business specialist be less likely to experience a 
much displacement. 

The current level of educational requirement for occupation tends to be correlate with 
the likelihood that their activity can be automated. The technical automation potential for 
occupation require less than a high school diploma be 55 percent, whereas for those with 
a college degree, that potential be far lower, at just 22 percent. Occupations require some 
post-secondary education generally include work activity that be less automatable than 
those require a high school diploma and some experience, and so on (Exhibit 4).

31Jobs lost, job gained: Workforce transition in a time of automationMcKinsey Global Institute 

••• 

Countries and company have compelling reason to embrace automation, since the 
technology will give a much-needed boost to productivity in the global economy. 
Depending on the pace of adoption, however, automation technology in the workplace 
could displace worker in a wide range of sectors; in our most aggressive scenario for early 
adoption, almost one-third of work hour in the global economy could be automate by 
2030—although other, late adoption scenario have less dramatic outcome in that time 
frame. Economic and social factor beyond technical feasibility affect adoption, and these 
could lead to strong variation in adoption rate among sector and countries. Under these 
circumstances, what will be the future of work? Will the global economy create enough 
additional job to offset those lose to automation, regardless of when adoption take place? 
And if so, what sort of job will those be, require which skills, and pay what wages? In 
the next two chapters, we highlight finding of our analysis of future labor demand and the 
complex workforce transition that automation will likely set in motion. 



1. Jobs lost, job changed: Impact of automation on work McKinsey Global Institute32 

An automate wool factory in France, circa 1949 

© Robert Doisneau/Gamma-Legends/Getty Images 



For centuries, the arrival of new technology in the workplace have spark workers’ fears— 
and, sometimes, violent backlash. Already in 1589, England’s Queen Elizabeth I refuse to 
grant a patent to a stock frame invent by William Lee because she be supposedly 
concerned about the effect on hand knitters.29 In the early 19th century, textile worker in 
Britain and France smash automate loom in their factory and printer struck to protest 
the arrival of steam-powered presses.30 

Leading thinker in the past, from David Ricardo to Karl Marx and John Maynard Keynes, 
raise concern about the effect of technological change on employment, and opinion poll 
show that anxiety have come to the fore again, amid rapid advance in robotics and artificial 
intelligence.31 A number of prominent academic and technologist argue that the late 
wave of automation technologies, include artificial intelligence and machine learning, will 
be particularly disruptive to the workforce.32 

In this chapter, we examine the historical impact of technology on employment, skills, and 
wages. History do not necessarily repeat itself, but it do provide valuable context 
and possible lesson for the future of labor demand in a time of automation. Among those 
lesson be that technological innovation in the past have enable the creation of many 
more new job than it have destroyed, raise productivity, spur sustain increase in 
living standards, and bring about a shift in the balance of work and leisure. However, 
the transition have not always be smooth: for example, real wage stagnate for nearly 
50 year in 19th century England during the Industrial Revolution there, and only picked up 
again at a time of substantial social policy reforms. Charles Dickens among other novelist 
use the harsh reality of everyday life for displace and other worker a material for 
his works. History also show that robust aggregate demand and economic growth be 
essential for job creation. New technology have raise productivity growth, enable firm 
to low price for consumers, pay high wages, or distribute profit to shareholders. This 
stimulates demand across the economy, boost job creation.

2. Lessons from history on technology and employment McKinsey Global Institute34 

LARGE-SCALE SECTOR EMPLOYMENT DECLINES HAVE BEEN MORE THAN 
OFFSET BY OTHER SECTORS EMPLOYING WORKERS 
Since the First Industrial Revolution begin in England in the 18th century, the economy 
of Europe, the United States, and other country have undergone two profound wave 
of structural change. Mechanization enable a revolution in agriculture and in industry, 
prompt a migration of worker from the countryside to cities. A second structural shift 
have occur in the past 60 year a the share of manufacturing employment have decline 
in some country even a growth in service sector accelerates.33 

The employment shift accompany this process of structural transformation have be 
very large. In the United States, for instance, the agriculture share of employment decline 
from 58 percent of total employment in 1850 to 2.5 percent of employment today (Exhibit 5). 
In just 40 years, between 1880 and 1920, the share of agricultural employment decline 
25 percentage points. During the same decades, other sector be be transform by 
mechanization and electrification a well: the share of miner and household workers, for 
example maid and servants, also declined, although these shift affected few workers. 
Since 1960, when the second wave of structural transformation began, manufacturing fell 
from 27 percent of total US employment to 9 percent today, a automation and global trade 
transform manufacturing and a demand for service exploded. 

The pattern be broadly similar in other countries, although there be some notable 
difference in the pace. China’s shift sector mix in recent year have be especially rapid: 
agricultural employment fell a a share of total employment by 32 percentage point in just 
25 years, from 60 percent in 1990 to 28 percent in 2015.34 In Mexico, the agriculture share 
of employment decline from 52 percent in 1960 to 13 percent in 2015, although in contrast 
with China, the decline have be gradual and continuous across decades. In Japan, 
agricultural employment decline from a 31 percent share of total employment in 1960 to 
3.5 percent in 2015, while manufacturing’s share of total employment drop from it peak 
in 1973 of 25 percent to 13 percent in 2015. 

Throughout these large shift of worker across occupation and industries, overall 
employment a a share of the population have generally continued to grow. New industry 
and occupation emerge to absorb worker displace by technology, although a we 
discus below, the transition have not always be smooth. 

Magnitude of potential job dislocation from automation through 2030 be 
not unprecedented 
When we compare historical sector employment to potential labor displacement from our 
automation model, we see that even in the early automation scenario, future rate of labor 
displacement from automation within specific sector be not unprecedented. For example, 
our analysis show that a number of sector in different countries, include agriculture 
in China, Germany, and Japan, and manufacturing in the United States, have decline by 
30 percent and more over a period of 15 years. Our analysis of scenario of automation 
displacement over the 15 year from 2016 to 2030 be within the same range (Exhibit 6).
