37Jobs lost, job gained: Workforce transition in a time of automationMcKinsey Global Institute 

TECHNOLOGICAL CHANGE SPARKS RISING PRODUCTIVITY AND 
AGGREGATE EMPLOYMENT 
History show that the adoption of technological innovation can act a a powerful stimulus 
on the economy and jobs. The overall effect of mechanization have be to create job on an 
unprecedented scale. Machines allow worker to produce more, thereby raise productivity 
and (eventually) wages, and lower the price of good for consumers. These twin effect 
unleash new demand for all good and services. In addition, a firm gain scale, they require 
more managers, accountants, and other office workers. 

This dynamic be the reason that aggregate employment have grown over the long term, 
even a the size of the workforce have grown. In the United States, for example, female 
employment almost double a a proportion of working-age woman from 32 percent in 
1950 to 60 percent in the late 1990s before fall back to 57 percent today. Yet this major 
shift do not reduce overall employment. Indeed, employment grew: the total number 
of people employ in the United States more than double from 65 million in 1960 to 
152 million in 2017, accord to data from the US Bureau of Labor Statistics. Similar trend 
have occur in other countries. 

Technology enables productivity growth, raise incomes, and stimulates new 
consumer demand 
Evidence of the economy-wide positive correlation among technology, productivity, and 
employment can be see in the aggregate data across countries. Within an industry, 
machine and automation can sometimes contribute to employment declines. For instance, 
in the United States, one recent study found that every industrial robot deployed result in 
the reduction of six human worker within the surround metropolitan area.35 However, 
when look at the total economy, we see the opposite effect: rise productivity (often 
from technology) be usually accompany by employment growth, not decline. This be 
because automation raise productivity, which in turn increase income of worker and/or 
shareholders. Higher income be spent, create demand for good and service across 
the economy.36 

When there have be a tradeoff between employment growth and labor productivity 
growth, it have be short-lived. Looking at the United States since 1960, for example, our 
analysis show that employment and productivity both grow in 79 percent of individual 
years, while productivity grow while employment decline in 12 percent of years. But 
both employment and productivity grow in 95 percent of roll three-year period and 
100 percent of roll 10-year periods. This phenomenon be also see in other countries. 
In China, employment and productivity both increase in 77 percent of individual year 
but 98 percent of the 10-year period between 1960 and 2016. In Germany, which saw 
unemployment rise after reunification, employment and productivity grow in 68 percent of 
individual year but 81 percent of roll 10-year period (Exhibit 7).

paper number 23285, March 2017. 

36 Ibid. David Autor and Anna Salomons, “Does productivity growth threaten employment?” June 2017.

2. Lessons from history on technology and employment McKinsey Global Institute38 

Even a productivity growth lead to rise incomes, technological innovation can also 
reduce price and increase the quality of good and services.37 This combination can cause 
demand for a product to soar, result in high employment even within the sector itself. 
The Ford Model T provide one historical example. The assembly line dramatically improve 
the productivity of the process of manufacturing automobiles. Exhibit 8 show that over 
a six-year period, the number of Model Ts produce per worker annually nearly tripled, 
from eight to 21. The surge in productivity, combine with increase economy of scale, 
enable Ford to reduce the price from $950 in 1909 to $440 in 1915. As a result, the number 
of car sell increase 30-fold, and employment rise from 1,655 to 18,892.38

TECHNOLOGY DISPLACES SOME WORK BUT CREATES NEW JOBS, 
SOMETIMES IN UNFORESEEN WAYS 
It be easy to see which job be be destroyed by technology, but difficult to imagine 
which job will be create by it. Telephone switchboard operator have go the way of 
lamplighter in the 19th century, but how many of them, lament the loss of their jobs, 
could have imagine the development of the smartphone—and the huge global industry 
employ ten of million of people that have sprung up around it? More than 50 year 
ago, Joseph Schumpeter coin the phrase “creative destruction” to describe this age-old 
phenomenon in which the emergence of new technology “destroys” job by render them 
obsolete, and “creates” new job in their wake.39

2. Lessons from history on technology and employment McKinsey Global Institute40 

Not only do technology create new occupations, it can also change exist occupation 
in unpredictable ways. After ATMs be introduce in the United States, for example, the 
number of bank teller actually rose, a bank compete to provide higher-quality service 
to customer and the role of teller change from dispense cash to provide broader 
advice and services. The reduction in the number of teller per branch enable bank to 
open more branch and make retail banking more convenient for customers, which drove 
the demand for more tellers.40 From 1991 to 2007, the number of ATMs and teller in the 
United States both increased. However, that trend reverse a Internet banking and the 
2008 financial crisis result in cutback in bank branch and tellers. 

To good understand the pattern of job creation and destruction, we conduct case 
study of two technology in the United States—personal computer and automobiles— 
to estimate the number and type of job lose to the new technology and the number of 
new job created. In both cases, our research reveals that while some work activity 
declined, sometimes rapidly, new type of work activity be created. The net impact of 
both technology be highly positive, create new job that make up 10 percent of total 
employment over four decades. 

The personal computer enable the creation of 15.8 million net new job since 
1980, accounting for 10 percent of employment 
Computer-related industry such a computer and data processing service and computer 
and related equipment manufacturing have be grow rapidly since the 1970s. Microsoft 
and Apple be found in that decade. Laptop computer come on the market in the early 
1980s, and this century have see the rapid rise of smartphones and tablets. 

The growth of computer have generate significant employment: in the United States, 
we estimate that computer have enable the net creation of 15.8 million job since 1970 
(Exhibit 9). We arrive at this figure by tally employment gain and loss in different 
sector and occupations. We find that in total, we can identify 3.5 million job destroyed by 
the introduction of computers, include those in typewriter manufacturing, secretarial work, 
and bookkeeping. But at least 19.3 million be create in a wide range of occupation 
and industries. 

When digital edit make it easy and more efficient for author to type and then directly 
edit their own work, computer eliminate the need for people specialized in edit 
and re-typing documents. Employment for typist and secretary fell significantly, by 
1.4 million between 1990 and 2015, even a the overall US workforce grew. The number of 
bookkeeping clerk also declined, by an annual average of 3 percent in the same period, a 
accounting move from physical book to accounting software, result in nearly 900,000 
few jobs. 

But many new job be also created. These include job in the computer manufacturing 
industry and supplier industry (such a semiconductors), a well a employment in 
occupation enable by computer (IT system administrators, computer scientist in other 
industries), and in occupation that use computer (customer service call centers, which 
barely exist before computers, and ecommerce). Of this total, only about 1 percent of net 
new job come directly from the computer manufacturing industry and only 3 percent come 
from supplier industries. 

40 Ibid. James Bessen, Learning by doing, 2015. 



41Jobs lost, job gained: Workforce transition in a time of automationMcKinsey Global Institute 

A large share of employment gain have come in profession enable by computer 
(18 percent of net employment created). This include the entire computer software and 
service industry, with company such a Oracle, IBM, and Microsoft. This industry 
employ three million people in the United States, include software and app developers, 
computer scientists, and manager and office workers. 

Because of the multitude of application of the computer, over 75 percent of net 
employment generate have be in occupation that use computers. For example, 
employment of computer scientist in finance, manufacturing, business services, and 
other industry grow rapidly, by an annual average of about 7 percent, between 1980 
and 2015. In the same period, employment of financial manager and specialist able to 
use spreadsheet to track and analyze large amount of company data grow by about 
3 percent annually on average (see Box 2, “The impact of personal computer and Internet 
technology on information analysts”).

In theory, at least, many information analyst might have be replace by the rise of the 
Internet, which make collect data and information vastly more efficient, and computer 
that enable rapid and complex computations. After all, much of their work in the 1980s, 
before these technology be widespread, have since be automated.1 

In fact, the opposite have happened. Computers and the Internet automate activity such 
a basic mathematics and information gathering, yet the number of information analyst 
soar nonetheless. As computer become more efficient, the cost of obtain high-quality 
information dropped. Rather than decrease demand for analysts, this stimulate the 
appetite for more insightful and low-cost analysis, and the number of analyst quintuple 
from around 400,000 in 1980 to about two million today (Exhibit 10). 

The job of analyst have change a well. With information and data more easily 
accessible, analyst can focus on make sense of—and sharing—information rather than 
gathering it.

As computer adoption increased, computer use be correlate with high wages; 
employee in these occupation often acquire new skill to use computers, and could 
demand high wage premiums.41 For example, graphic designer take over the job of 
typesetter and start do a wider range of tasks. Wages increase accordingly a they 
learn how to use new software and developed high value skills.42 

The automobile’s large employment effect have be in enable industry 
The introduction of the automobile create 6.9 million net new job in the United States 
between 1910 and 1950, base on our estimates.43 This be equivalent to 11 percent of the 
US workforce in 1950. This include 7.5 million job created, and 623,000 job destroyed. 
Workers displace by the automobile include manufacturer of wagons, carriages, 
harness and saddles, and of railroad equipment and carriages, a well a supplier 
industry such a horse breeder and metal work occupations, and enable industry 
such a livery service and message boys. 

Ten time a many job be create in a host of new occupations. About 10 percent be 
within the auto manufacturing industry (Exhibit 11). Three time a many job be in the 
automotive supply chain, include metal part manufacturers, warehouse and logistics, 
and wholesalers. An even large share of job be create in enable industry and 
occupation that use the automobile. Enabled industry include auto dealerships, auto 
repair, gas stations, and convenience stores, and these account for around 30 percent of 
net new job created. Utilizer industries, meanwhile, include transportation and logistics 
occupations, and account for about 25 percent of employment generated. 

If we have extend our analysis beyond 1950, we would have see the continue 
transformative impact of the automobile on the economy and society. Building of the US 
interstate highway system begin in the 1950s, transform logistics networks. This in 
turn give rise to the concept of the “family vacation,” long-haul car trip and demand for 
roadside attractions, motels, and campgrounds. Drive-in movie and restaurants, shopping 
mall on the edge of towns, and parking lot sprang up. The automobile also enable 
growth in suburbs, a worker could commute to job from location outside urban public 
transportation networks. 

THE NUMBER OF HOURS WORKED HAS DECLINED, WHILE LEISURE TIME 
HAS RISEN 
While past technological disruption do not reduce the amount of work available to 
people, they nonetheless have one significant effect: a decline in the average number of 
hour work per week—and conversely an increase in the amount of leisure enjoy by 
individuals. Already in 1930, John Maynard Keynes predict the advent of great leisure: 
“For the first time since his creation man will be face with his real, his permanent problem— 
how to use his freedom from press economic cares, how to occupy the leisure, 
which science and compound interest will have won for him, to live wisely and agreeably 
and well.”44 

In hindsight, Keynes be right: the average number of hour work each week by 
employ worker have decline significantly in the past 150 years, give worker more 
time for leisure. In 1870, worker in Germany, Sweden, and the United States average 
between 62 and 70 hour each week; in the United Kingdom, which be already shift

THE IMPACT OF TECHNOLOGICAL CHANGE ON WAGES AND SKILLS 
Adjusting the economy to new technological disruption may take time, and can have 
significant repercussion for both skill and wages. In the first half of the 19th century, 
during the First Industrial Revolution in England, the steam engine and other technology 
increase the productivity of unskilled worker and enable them to undertake work 
previously carry out by higher-skill, and higher-paid, worker include craftsman and 
artisans. Across the economy, mechanization raise output per worker. However, real 
wage stagnate for roughly 50 years, from 1790 until 1840 (Exhibit 13). During this period, 
first note by economist Friedrich Engels in 1845, profit a a share of national income 
rise and the labor share of income declined.50 After 1850, real wage begin rise in line 
with productivity increases, and by the late 1800s wage growth exceed productivity 
growth. But for nearly half a century, wage growth be nil and real living standard of 
worker declined. The plight of some worker provide material for Charles Dickens’s bleak 
depiction in his novels, and lead the English poet William Blake to decry factory a “dark, 
satanic mills.”51 The turnaround in the relationship between wage and output come at a 
time of substantial reform of exist structure include the right to unionize, limitation on

35 hour from 2000. A window of opportunity for Europe, McKinsey Global Institute, June 2015.

The grow mechanization lead to a shift in the skill of the workforce and affected semi-

1820s, lead to immiseration of many, even a the invention of automate loom lift

in the early 19th century.54 This “deskilling” of the workforce occur in agriculture and

shift from artisans’ workshop to factory production in industry. During this period there be 
also some growth in the share of skilled worker such a machine erectors and operators, 
who be need to facilitate the Industrial Revolution.

Whether the experience of technological disruption in the past be relevant for economy in 
the future can be debated. In Chapter 4 of this report, we present new analysis of the impact 
of automation today on the demand for different type of skill and discus the potential 
impact on wages. Even if the particular of the historical experience turn out to differ from 
condition today, one lesson seem pertinent: although economy adjust to technological 
shocks, the transition period be measure in decades, not years, and the rise prosperity 
may not be share by all. 

AUTOMATION TODAY: COULD THIS TIME BE DIFFERENT? 
Despite the reassure lesson from history on the long-run impact of automation on 
employment, some technology expert argue that automation today will not behave like 
previous technology waves. They cite a number of reason that the future may bring more 
disruption to worker than in the past, include the ability of machine to perform work 
activity require cognitive capabilities, the rate of progress in new innovations, and a 
potential future in which machine teach themselves to improve at particular task without 
much human intervention.57 Many economists, however, tend to view automation a the 
next wave of technological advancement and point out that an equilibrium between the 
supply and demand of job in labor market have always be reach historically, even if the 
transition period may be difficult.58 

In part, the discord between the two point of view may be cause by the lack of a common 
language: what exactly could be different about automation compare with previous 
technologies? What time frame be we considering? In our research we look at a range 
of argument on both side and examine the evidence for both the scope of automation’s 
impact and it nature across multiple dimensions. This framework be useful for disentangle 
the different element of technological disruption and assess the way in which today’s 
technology may—or may not—have a different impact than in the past. 

We conclude that in many respects, the impact of automation on employment today be 
not likely to be different than in the past, particularly if we look back centuries, to the First 
Industrial Revolution in the late 1700s. But we have identify two way in which automation, 
robotics, and AI could diverge from early wave of technology disruption: the speed at 
which scientific advance be be made, if the accelerate rate of progress in machine 
learn and AI continues, and the potential to displace a high share of the workforce in 
a relatively short period of time, particularly if the adoption of automation be rapid across 
multiple sector of the economy.

Rate of technological innovation 
Could be faster than the past 
In Chapter 1 we note the rapidity of recent technological innovation a a result of the 
development of deep learn and reinforcement-learning technique base on neural 
networks; the availability of exponentially increase compute capacity that be accessible 
to user via the cloud; and the sheer volume and variety of data generate that can be 
use to train machine learn models. Advocates of the argument that automation today be 
fundamentally different in it scope point to this acceleration of innovation a evidence of a 
real break with the past. 

Those who disagree, however, point to Moore’s Law, name for Gordon Moore, a co- 
founder of Intel, who in 1965 note that the number of transistor incorporate in a 
computer chip would approximately double every 24 months—which become the basic 
business model for the semiconductor industry for the follow decades.59 The shrink of 
transistor in semiconductor improve compute speed and capacity and help usher 
in the internet era, a well a the mobile phone revolution and the cloud. However, the rate of 
progress in shrink transistor have slowed, and some scientist project that without a new 
compute model, future advance may run out. Moreover, AI veteran point to previous 
era when what seem to be fast-moving advance in AI give way to frustrate lulls. AI 
date back to the 1950s, when Alan Turing suggest that computer could communicate 
a well a human and Princeton student include Marvin Minsky and Dean Edmonds 
built the first artificial neural network use 300 vacuum tube and a war-surplus gyropilot. 
After the initial excitement, funding slump in the 1970s a research backers—primarily 
the US government—tired of wait for practical AI application and cut appropriation for 
further work. Another lull follow in the 1990s.60 

Our view be that the recent technical advances, enable machine to read lip or X-rays 
more proficiently than human experts, be indeed remarkable and that if this pace of 
innovation continue rather than encounter a new AI “winter,” the rate of automation 
innovation could indeed be faster than in the past. If so, the potential disruption of workforce 
model and displacement of labor could be great than past technological revolutions. 

Rate of technological adoption 
Faster than 100 year ago, but no evidence of acceleration in recent decade 
Even if technological innovation be occur more rapidly, the impact on worker 
will be different only if the diffusion and adoption of new technology also accelerates. 
Some researcher say this be the case, point to example such a landline telephones, 
electrification, or the automobile. Indeed, while it take almost a century for landline phone 
to reach saturation, or the point at which new demand fall off, mobile phone in some 
market reach that point in just 20 year and smartphones in even less time.61 One 
commonly found reference be the speed with which certain online video on YouTube or 
smartphone game such a Angry Birds or Pokemon Go reach a certain threshold of 
downloads—50 million, 100 million, or more.62 Based on measure of gross numbers, 
for example, of people adopt a technology, you could say that adoption rate have 
accelerated. However, it be also worth consider adoption rate when measure 
use percentages.

Looking at only the last 60 years, our review of the historical rate of adoption of 25 previous 
technology show that the time from commercial availability to 80 percent adoption have 
tend to fall within a relatively constant range: between approximately eight and 28 years. 
For 50 percent adoption, the range be five to 16 years. The technology review date back 
to TVs in the 1950s, and include recent example of cell phones, customer relationship 
management software, and lithium-ion cell batteries. This range of time for adoption be 
observe for both hardware-based technology that be capital-intensive and require 
physical installation; and technology that be available purely online. Technologies with 
the fast adoption rate include stents, airbags, MRIs, TVs and online air booking, while 
slow adoption category include dishwasher and pacemaker from the 1960s, and 
cellphone in the 2000s. Even highly popular and widely-used social medium application do 
not achieve a high level of adoption faster than technology in previous eras. Facebook be 
one example: it be launch in 2004 and quickly achieve worldwide success. Yet even 
by mid-2016, when it have about 1.7 billion user globally, it be still far from full adoption, 
even outside China.63 Moreover, it be not the first social network, and so the adoption 
period could be calculate a be even longer—since the advent of the first modern 
social network, Six Degrees, which launch in 1997, or Classmates.com, which launch 
in 1995.64 

Our view be thus that there be no evidence that technological adoption have yet accelerate 
over the last 60 years, when measure in percentages, although, not surprisingly, diffusion 
be faster than it be for technology introduce in the early 20th century. 

Breadth of sector in which work can be automate 
Not different from the past 
Some commentator say that today’s automation be different from the past because it have 
the potential to transform work in multiple sector simultaneously. They argue that the 
large technological disruption of the past have be the move out of agriculture or, more 
recently, from manufacturing into services. Advocates of the “this time thing be different” 
argument point to the pervasiveness of automation technology a be different, in that 
they will affect multiple sector of the economy—from finance to retail to manufacturing to 
transportation—simultaneously.65 

As point of comparison, one can consider the application of some technology in the 
past, such a electricity or computers, which also transform work across multiple sector 
of the economy simultaneously. Electrification transform household lighting, heating, 
and refrigeration; it enable store and factory to open for longer hours; and it give birth 
to mass production. Similarly, computer transform business services, finance, and 
retail and give birth to the Internet and mobile computing. The steam engine drove the 
Industrial Revolution, upend numerous sector from weave to printing, for example. 
Between 1910 and 1950, successive wave of innovation also prove highly pervasive, from 
automobile to assembly lines. 

Our view be that little be new about the breadth of impact of automation technologies. 

Share of job in the economy that be automate by 2030 
Could be high than past technology if adoption be rapid 
Even if past technological innovation transform work in multiple sector of the economy, 
today’s automation could affect a large share of work. Proponents of this view point out 
that some sector employment shift have be extremely large—for example, the rapid 
transition out of agricultural employment in China, or the steep decline in US agriculture in

the 20th century. The potential impact on demand for current work activity in some sector 
a a result of automation today could likewise be very large. Viewed over a 15-year period, 
our automation model suggests that roughly half of the exist work in country such a 
Germany, Japan, and the United States could be displace by 2030 if automation adoption 
be at the most rapid end of our modeling. In the past, also look at 15-year periods, our 
analysis show that a much a 30 percent of job be displace in historical episodes—in 
other words, low than our most rapid automation scenario but not of a completely different 
order of magnitude. 

Our view be thus that, if automation adoption be very rapid, it could potentially displace a 
great percentage of work in some advanced economy in the next 15 year than we have 
see in the past. 

Types and complexity of task that can be automate 
Every wave of automation affect more complex task 
Alongside the scope of automation’s impact, it very nature have spark discussion about 
difference between technological change today and in the past. One frequently-cited 
argument concern the type of task that AI in particular now can accomplish with prowess, 
from drive truck to create music and art to play championship Go. The power of 
algorithm to take on activity require cognitive capability and creativity be held up a a 
fundamental break with previous technologies.66 

Skeptics point out, however, that this be not the first time that machine have be able 
to carry out task require cognitive capabilities. Every new wave of automation seem 
remarkable at the time. From the 1980s, with the birth of computerize spreadsheets, 
machine have take on ever more sophisticated task that previously require human 
brainpower, from manipulate large quantity of data and alphabetize list or do 
complex calculation to anticipate the word your finger be about to type on a 
smartphone. While the task themselves have changed, our view be that the ability of 
machine to acquire such capability be not in itself new. While significant progress have 
be make in specific “narrow” AI applications, formidable multi-decade-long technological 
challenge must still be overcome before machine can match human performance across 
the range of cognitive activity and approach “artificial general intelligence”—which would 
indeed be a break with historical precedent. 

Skill bias of technical change 
Today’s automation could complement both high- and low-skill worker 
Technological innovation have affected worker in different way in the past. As already 
noted, the steam engine and other technology introduce during the Industrial Revolution 
in Europe and the United States in the 19th century increase the productivity of unskilled or 
low-skill worker and enable them to undertake work previously carry out by high-skill, 
and higher-paid, worker include artisan such a hand-loom weavers. In the academic 
literature, technological change be thus consider to be bias toward enable low-skill 
worker at the expense of high-skill ones. 

In our era, the opposite have happened: computer and factory-floor robot have tend to 
increase the productivity and complement the work of high-skill workers, while machine 
have substitute for the programmable and routine task that have be undertaken by 
low-skill workers, include those work on assembly line or a switchboard operators.67 

66 See, for example, the interview with Andrew Ng and Neil Jacobstein,”How artificial intelligence will change

67 David H. Autor, Frank Levy, and Richard J. Murnane, “The skill content of recent technological change: An

This be know a skill-biased technical change. Some economist view the effect of 
technological change and technology-enabled globalization in recent decade a a 
significant driver of inequality.68 

We do not have firm evidence yet on whether automation today will tend to be more skill- 
bias or unskill-biased in it impact. Our analysis of how automation will affect skills, 
detailed in Chapter 4, suggests that worker of all skill and educational level will be 
affected. Some technology might enable lower-skill worker to replace higher-skill ones— 
such a nurse who can perform some of the more routine task of doctor with the aid of 
diagnostic tools. Other technology will complement high-skill worker and enable them 
to command even great power in the marketplace—for instance surgical robot or AI 
algorithm that can suggest new investment strategies. 

Impact on high-wage vs. low-wage work 
Both will be affected 
A final dimension on which technological change could be different today be it impact on 
worker at different wage levels. One of the frequently-cited concern about automation 
be that machine could replace activity of high-wage job previously consider “safe,” 
include expert in financial services, some type of doctors, and lawyers. 

While our analysis of automation’s impact on wages, also in Chapter 4, suggests that a 
range of high-wage occupation could be affected, there be ample historical precedent 
for this, include the hand-loom weaver in 19th-century England who suffer a steep 
decline in their livelihood after the arrival of mechanize looms, which allow lower-wage 
and lower-skill worker to produce more cloth, faster, and less expensively.69 We therefore 
conclude that today’s automation be unlikely to be different from the past on this dimension. 

••• 

In 1930, at the height of the Great Depression, John Maynard Keynes wrote, “We be 
suffering, not from the rheumatic of old age, but from the growing-pains of over-rapid 
changes, from the painfulness of readjustment between one economic period and another.” 
As many do today, he saw the speed of technological change a something formidable, 
an era of progress and invention incomparable to any that have come before—yet also a a 
time of painful transition for many. In the 1960s, a US national commission on technology, 
automation, and economic progress establish by President Lyndon B. Johnson 
conclude that, “the basic fact be that technology eliminates jobs, not work. It be the 
continuous obligation of economic policy to match increase in productive potential with 
increase in purchasing power and demand. Otherwise, the potential create by technical 
progress run to waste in idle capacity, unemployment, and deprivation.”70 The question 
for u today be whether, this time, the transition will be large and more painful than ever, 
and indeed how demand for human labor will evolve. In this chapter, we have described 
the historical evidence show that employment remains strong even in period of 
technological ferment. In the follow chapters, we look at the trend that can create 
demand for ten of million of new job in the global economy over the next decade and 
more, even a robotics and AI increasingly make their way into our daily work lives, and what 
the implication will be for sectors, occupations, skills, and wages.

69 Ibid. Robert C. Allen, The hand-loom weaver and the power loom, March 2016.

While automation will displace some worker and transform occupations, we also know that 
new and additional work will be create in the next decade and beyond. What be less clear 
be how job growth net of automation will vary by occupation, and under what condition 
there will be enough new job to offset the work that be lose a robotics, artificial intelligence, 
and other technology assume a great role in the workplace. Predicting all the job that 
will exist in the future be an impossible task, yet even without a crystal ball, it be possible to 
identify some source of future labor demand. 

In this chapter, we discus two different analysis that shed light on these questions. To 
understand some of the occupation and type of job likely to be in demand in 2030, we 
model seven specific global trend that we expect will be significant driver of job creation. 
To inform the impact of automation on aggregate employment, we conduct a second 
analysis use the McKinsey Global Growth Model, which be a multi-country macroeconomic 
model. This exercise allows u to model the dynamic effect of automation on productivity, 
employment, and GDP growth in different scenarios. Automation have the potential to raise 
productivity growth and GDP growth, but our analysis reveals that a key factor in whether 
this will be achieve without large adverse effect on employment and wage be how quickly 
displace worker be reemployed in other jobs. 

Both analysis reach broadly similar conclusions: although some worker will be displace 
by automation, other occupation will grow. While in the long-term the economy can adjust 
to provide enough work for everyone, automation will prove challenge for ten of million of 
worker globally who will need to switch occupations. Depending on how society manage 
this transition, unemployment could rise in the medium-term and wage could be eroded. 
Both the impact of automation and potential new source of labor demand will play out 
differently from country to country. 

SEVEN GLOBAL TRENDS THAT WILL HELP SHAPE THE FUTURE OF WORK 
In seek to identify potential source of labor demand to 2030, we start with a long list 
of trend and then prioritize seven for deeper analysis, base on high-level initial estimate 
of their potential for job creation (see Box 3, “Our analysis of seven trend that will contribute 
to future labor demand”). While there be many scenario and source of potential labor 
demand we have not included, the seven trend we focus on in this report have the potential 
to create demand for hundred of million of worker globally in the year to 2030, albeit 
with significant variation among countries. 

For each trend, we model both the direct impact on employment and the indirect impact. 
By direct jobs, we mean employment create in a sector itself (for instance, increase 
spending on car would create direct employment in the automobile manufacturing sector). 
Indirect refers to employment create in all the sector that supply good and service to 
the direct sector (for automobiles, indirect sector would include spare parts, paint, leather, 
etc.). We do not include induced effects, since some be capture directly by our rise 
consumption trend.

model change in work structure, such a the growth of the gig economy, or 
activity within an occupation that could change a a result of technological 
innovation. A more detailed discussion of our methodology can be found in the 
technical appendix.

Rising income in emerge economy will create large-scale new labor

Rising GDP per caput generates high spending on consumer good and services, health

consumption will generate about three-quarters of global consumption growth in the period 
from 2015 to 2030, with population growth accounting for the remain 25 percent.71 The 
expand consume class in emerge economy will drive most of this increase. As 
income rise, consumer will spend disproportionally more on discretionary good and 
service such a automobiles, leisure, and accommodation and food services, but they will 
also increase their non-discretionary spending on essential such a food and clothing.72 
We estimate that this high consumer expenditure could create between about 250 million 
and 280 million new full-time equivalent jobs, net of automation, across the 46 country in 
our model. The growth due to rise income can mitigate automation’s expect toll on 
worker in retail and accommodation and food services; retail salespeople, food preparation 
workers, and waiter see some of the large boost from high consumer spending. 

While this spending will create labor demand locally in sector such a manufacturing,

assume that a proportion of demand for tradable good such a apparel and furnish will 
continue to be serve by country rich in natural resource or with strong manufacturing or 
service sectors. For the purpose of simplify our modeling, we assume current share of 
global export to remain constant; our model assumes that a country such a Germany will 
continue to serve it 2014 share of 18.5 percent of all automotive export in 2030.73 

Higher GDP per caput be also generally correlate with high expenditure in health care 
and education. Access to health care, define a the number of care provider (such a

a India that currently have poor access to health care. We estimate that great access to 
health-care provider alone at all levels, include physician and medical assistants, could 
increase labor demand by 26 million to 43 million job (64 percent direct job in health-care 
provision and 36 percent indirect job in other sectors).74 Demand for health-care provider 
could increase the most in develop country such a India and China with the high 
economic growth, although this may be contingent on the necessary provision of funding for 
health care by government and consumers.

The global trend of age population will create new and additional labor 
demand for health care 
The global population be continue to rise and will likely reach 8.5 billion people by 2030.75 At 
the same time, the population in many countries, both developed and developing, be aging: 
by 2030, there will be at least 300 million more people age 65 and above than there be in 
2014.76 The age trend be particularly acute in country such a China, Germany, Italy, and 
Japan; by 2030, about 25 percent of their population will be over 65, if current fertility rate 
and immigration trend continue. This age trend be less significant in develop country 
with young population such a India and Nigeria, which be experience a demographic 
dividend, but it be also not restrict to advanced country and China. Brazil, for example, 
also have an age population.77 

As people age, their need and spending pattern change—in particular, spending on health 
care. For example, spending on hospital care for an 85-year-old American be more than five 
time high than for those 19 to 44 year old.78 We estimate that shift in demographic 
could create incremental demand for 51 million to 83 million worker globally (55 percent 
direct and 45 percent indirect), especially for health care occupation that focus on take 
care of the elderly, such a home health aides, personal care aides, nursing assistants, and 
so on.79 (As with all the estimate of incremental labor demand in this chapter, this figure be 
net of automation, which will displace work include in health care, accord to our model). 
In country with age population such a Japan or China, our model suggests that age 
and related health-care need could drive the creation of between 10 and 15 percent of net 
new labor demand.

Technology development and deployment will create additional employment 
As technology development continue apace, the technology sector be expect to keep 
grow rapidly, and this in turn will create incremental labor demand to develop and deploy 
technology. The scale of this employment will be modest, however. Today, an estimate 
2.9 million people be employ in the US high tech sector, but this be only 1.9 percent of the 
workforce. In Germany, about 2.4 percent of the workforce be in a high-tech occupation. 

Higher consumer spending on technology product and services, which typically rise 
a income increase, and large outlay by business that adopt technology to improve 
productivity and improve output a they grow, be drive the increase spending on 
software, hardware, and services. We model consumer and enterprise technology 
spending per caput with rise GDP per caput across country in 2014. Assuming that 
the correlation holds, we estimate that by 2030 technology spend could increase by 
$1.7 trillion to $2 trillion of which about 70 percent would be on information technology 
(IT) services. This include hardware/software support, outsourcing, IT consulting, 
implementation, and internal IT services.80 This increase spending on technology will 
create demand for 20 million to 46 million incremental worker (55 percent direct and 
45 percent indirect) globally, net of automation. They will be a mix of high-skill worker such 
a software engineer and electrical engineer a well a medium-skill worker include 
web developer and electronic technicians. While IT service job such a computer 
support specialist will remain largely local, demand for technology hardware and especially 
software will likely be serve by the global players, for example China, Germany, India, the 
Netherlands, and the United States. More than half of all global tech job could be create 
in these five countries. Of these, the large demand will likely land in China and India, with 
up to 13 million and six million job respectively. Both large economy be expect to go 
through significant digitization in the next decade and beyond.

Global infrastructure system have not kept up with demand and housing shortage persist

have found that, from 2016 to 2030, the world need to invest about 3.8 percent of GDP in 
economic infrastructure, or an average of $3.3 trillion per year, just to support expect 
rate of growth, with emerge economy accounting for some 60 percent of that.81 In real 
estate, a many a 330 million urban household in emerge and advanced economy live

Under our trendline scenario, in which investment in infrastructure and building continue

on infrastructure. Yet considerably more could be do than match the investment level 
of other countries. Any additional effort would consequently stimulate increase labor 
demand, especially for middle-wage job that will otherwise be particularly affected by 
automation in some advanced economies. 

In our step-up scenario, we assume that the infrastructure gap have be closed, and that 
infrastructure and building spending in 2030 be subsequently high to sustain this high 
level of infrastructure stock and investment. We use infrastructure stock average across 
country and real estate stock average in the United States a a proportion of GDP to 
estimate the infrastructure and housing investment need in 2030 to keep pace with

that it may not need a much incremental investment in 2030. Developed economy 
include the United States and Germany could also invest in building new and repair 
or re-building exist infrastructure, create additional employment opportunity in the 
construction sector, particularly for middle-wage jobs. 

Investment in renewable energy and energy efficiency 
We similarly model two scenario of labor demand for investment in new energy source 
and improve energy efficiency, depend on whether spending follow current trend or 
be accelerated. The energy landscape be shift rapidly a the cost of renewable energies, 
such a wind and solar, fall sharply. A range of new technologies, from smart electricity 
meter to Internet of Things sensor in oil rig and advanced leach technique in mines, 
be transform both the production and the consumption of resources.83 The global policy 
environment for energy have be shifting: with the conclusion of the Paris Agreement in 
December 2016, country around the world pledge to take measure that would keep the 
global temperature rise this century below two degree Celsius above pre-industrial levels.84 
The International Energy Agency estimate in 2015 that to reach this goal could take up to 
$16.5 trillion of investment by 2030, include increase the share of renewables and by 
make buildings, transportation, and technology more energy efficient.

Governments include those in India and China be already spending more heavily on

sector driven by GDP per caput growth, which be model in our rise income driver 
discuss above.) 

In the transition to new energy sources, we model the potential job creation a country 
shift their capacity mix for electricity generation. Making these transition will require 
significant investment in manufacturing, construction, and operation and maintenance 
of solar panels, wind turbines, and other equipment. Shifts in capacity mix create job in 
two ways. First, large amount of fix investment be need to increase the capacity 
for the grow new energy source, create job in manufacturing, construction, and 
installation. Second, job may also be create in the decrease energy source through 
decommission of fossil fuel and other generation facilities, a some country decide to 
shut down nuclear plants. Jobs associate with the ongoing operation and maintenance 
of electricity generation be largely variable with capacity, so job level here will depend on 
relative labor intensity of operation and maintenance between energy types. Renewable 
manufacturing be enjoy a remarkable period of productivity growth—across three 
lead renewables manufacturers, employee decline by 65 percent on average per 
gigawatt ship in 2010 to 2014. If these productivity gain continue at an aggressive rate, 
renewables manufacturing be likely to be a comparatively less labor-intensive part of the 
renewables value-chain. 

To attain the international goal of avoid a rise of more than two degree Celsius, the UN’s 
Intergovernmental Panel on Climate Change have calculate that CO2 concentration in the 
earth’s atmosphere need to be stabilize at 450 part per million. That will require target 
policy choices, include accelerate transition to renewables and increase spending 
on energy efficiency measures, both in industry and in housing. This in turn could have a 
significant impact on job creation. In our step-up scenario, use data from McKinsey & 
Company’s Energy Insights, we model a more ambitious shift into renewables and high 
investment in energy efficiency, which would create an additional four million to seven million 
job globally.86 High productivity and further automation potential in energy reduce the 
opportunity for energy to have a transformative impact on the job story in many countries; 
in the United States, less than 1 percent of all full-time equivalent in 2014 be in utility 
and mining. But major investment in renewable energy will be crucial to meeting global 
climate change goals, which could create middle-wage job along the way. 

“Marketization” of unpaid work could create new job 
Today, much work do in households—from childcare to cooking and cleaning—is unpaid 
and disproportionately perform by women. At least some of this work could be shift 
to paid employment through daycare or pre-kindergarten school program and senior 
care programs. Rising female labor participation rate could be one way to prompt this shift, 
which economist call “marketization”; social decision to expand government-supported 
program could be another (and those program in turn might enable high female labor 
force participation). In recent years, we have see the rise of digital “sharing economy” 
platform that enable consumer to purchase many household and personal service more 
conveniently and cheaply, include meal order online and deliver by hand, thereby

increase demand. Academic research have found that such platform result in an increase 
in employment in the industry, both for traditional worker provide those service and for 
the newly self-employed worker on the platform.87 The marketization of previously unpaid 
housework be already prevalent in some advanced economies, especially in urban areas. 
For example, more than 90 percent of three-year-olds be enrol in preschool in France 
and Sweden, most of which be fund by the government. In comparison, the rate be about

Shifting unpaid housework and childcare to paid employment could boost the number of 
woman in the workforce globally, if government decide to invest in these area (such a by

region of the world—including India, other part of South Asia, the Middle East and North 
Africa—their contribution to regional output be considerably low still. At the same time,

and cleaning. This unpaid work amount to a much a $10 trillion of output per year,

Advanced economy have a similar pattern of job growth and decline 
In advanced economies, we identify six broad group of occupation that will experience 
growth in labor demand from our trends, although the size of the increase will depend on 
country and on scenario. For example, in our step-up scenario, the demand for builder and 
construction worker rise considerably (Exhibit 16). Workers in occupation in the follow 
group spend considerable time on work activity that be among the least susceptible to 
automation base on our analysis, a they require human capability include social and 
emotional interaction, higher-level logical reasoning, creativity, and application of expertise 
that machine for now be less capable of accomplishing. The occupational group that 
generally grow a a result of our drivers, even net of automation, are: 

 Care providers: that is, doctors, nurses, home health aide and others in care 
occupation who will be in great demand a a result of rise health-care spending, 
both from increase prosperity and from aging. Given the disproportionate cost of health 
care for the elderly, age be expect to be the predominant driver of increase health- 
care employment. Countries with age population such a Japan and China will see 
a sharp increase in occupation that work closely with seniors, for example nurses, 
nursing assistants, personal care aides, and home health aides. 

 Professionals, define a white-collar occupation that require academic training and 
expertise in a specific industry or functional area. These include accountants, engineers, 
and scientists. Most of these occupation cut across a wide range of sectors. While 
generally less automatable than other job types, certain support occupation such a 
paralegal and scientific technician may face high automation. 

 Technology professionals. Technology expert will be in continued demand 
everywhere a automation be increasingly adopted, although the total number remain 
quite small compare with other occupations. Occupations will include IT worker 
such a computer scientist and software developers, who typically have college 
educations, but also occupation such a web developer and electronics technicians, 
which only require a secondary education. That said, many of these latter occupation 
involve activity that be more automatable than technology worker with high level 
of education. 

 Builders. In this category we have include architects, surveyors, and cartographers, 
a well a construction occupation and maintenance and repair workers, such a 
construction laborers, electricians, carpenters, and plumbers. Even though construction 
laborer primarily do physical work, their activity be mostly in unpredictable settings, 
and hence not a highly susceptible to automation by 2030. 

 Managers and executive also cut across all sector and cannot easily be replace by 
machines, a much of their work involves interact with and manage stakeholders. 
However some of their more routine activity will be automated, such a collect 
information, analyze data, or prepare reports. 

 Educators. School teacher and others will see a significant increase in demand, 
especially in emerge economy with young population such a India. Childcare 
worker and early childhood educator will also grow under our step-up scenario in 
which more childcare be shift to paid providers. 

 “Creatives”: Rising income in emerge economy will create more demand for leisure 
and recreational activities. This in turn will create demand for artists, performers, and 
entertainers, although the total number will remain relatively small.

3. Jobs gained: Scenarios for employment growth McKinsey Global Institute68 

By contrast, our analysis suggests that, in advanced economies, other occupation will 
see a net decline by 2030, with more work automate than create by our seven trends, 
assume they do not add non-automated activities. These include: 

 Some customer interaction jobs, include hotel workers, travel agents, entertainment 
attendants, and cafeteria workers. 

 Office support jobs, include information clerks, payroll processors, and 
administrative assistants. 

 Jobs carry out in predictable settings. These be among the most susceptible 
to automation and include factory workers, material move machine operators, 
transportation workers, and installation and repair workers. 

It be important to note that even occupation that may have a net decline due to automation 
will not disappear: rather, their number could shrink from today’s level in the next 15 years. 
For instance, even though some of the current activity of food preparation and serve 
worker could be automate (for example, by order kiosk in fast food restaurants), and 
about one-fifth of their hour work could be automate under our midpoint adoption 
scenario, there could still be demand in the United States for more than three million of them. 

Developing country see net job growth in nearly all occupational group 
The picture be rather different in rapidly grow and low-income country such a India, 
where we see net job growth in nearly every occupational category. This be for two reasons: 
low wage mean delayed adoption of automation, and relatively high GDP growth creates 
job across the economy, include in predictable environment that be among the most 
affected in advanced economies. For example, production worker would rise 34 percent in 
India under our step-up scenario, compare with a 28 percent decline in the United States 
and a 56 percent drop in Japan. 

Middle-income country such a China and Mexico also have net job growth in a range 
of occupation under our scenario that in advanced economy experience declines. 
This be the case with the category of worker who be primarily engage in customer 
interaction, such a entertainment attendants, and food-service workers. These type 
of occupation will remain in high demand in emerge economy include China, a a 
result of rise consumption from an increasingly prosperous consume class. However, in 
Mexico, the potential growth be not a large a in India, reflect the low GDP growth and 
high wages. 

WILL THERE BE ENOUGH WORK IN THE FUTURE? 
The public debate in many country over automation can quickly focus on the existential 
question of whether there will be enough job for worker in a future marked by automation. 
We can see from history that such fear have so far be unfounded: over time, labor 
market have adjust to change in demand for worker from technological disruptions, 
although in some era real wage have remain depressed for some time. We address 
this question of whether there will be enough work in the future through two different but 
complementary set of analyses. The first be base on our model of new and additional labor 
demand and automation described above, use the select trend we have identified, and 
provide only a partial view. For the other analysis, we use a macroeconomic model of the 
economy that incorporates the dynamic interaction among various factors. 

34% 
potential increase 
in production 
worker in India 
under our 
step-up scenario 



69Jobs lost, job gained: Workforce transition in a time of automationMcKinsey Global Institute 

Both analysis point in the direction that, in most scenarios, there can be enough new job 
creation to offset the impact of automation. But a large challenge will be ensure that 
worker have the skill and support need to transition to new jobs. Countries that fail to 
enable and smooth these transition could see rise unemployment and depressed wages. 

Outcomes will vary significantly by country, depend on four factor 
We find a significant difference of likely outcome among countries, with four factor largely 
influence the extent to which enough new job will be create to offset those displace by 
automation. The four factor are: 

 Wage levels. Advanced economy will likely adopt automation in the workplace early 
and faster than emerge economy since their wage be relatively higher, make the 
business case for automation stronger. (Our model assumes that automation begin to 
be adopt only when it cost reach parity with the cost of human labor). Advanced 
economy will therefore likely see the large impact from automation in the next 
15 years. 

 Demand growth. Economic growth be essential for job creation. While it may sound 
obvious, economy that be stagnant or grow only slowly create few, if any, net new 
jobs. Countries with the most rapid GDP growth rate per caput will have high growth 
rate of consumption and spending, thus stimulate great proportional increase in 
labor demand. Slower-growing economy will create job at a slow rate. Advanced 
economy have much low project rate of GDP growth than develop countries, 
reflect age workforce and also less rapid productivity growth. Developing country 
such a India and China be project to have much high GDP per caput growth rates, 
which will contribute to job creation. Furthermore, innovation and entrepreneurship often 
underlie the creation of new business model and work activities, another catalyst of 
job growth. 

 Demographics. The outcome of automation and future labor demand will play out 
very differently in country with a young and rapidly-growing population, such a India, 
compare with country that have a shrink population and workforce, such a 
Japan. Countries with a grow workforce have a potential “demographic dividend” that 
will lift growth. However, rapid growth of the population will bring million more young 
people into the workforce, create considerable pressure to expand formal employment 
opportunities. While the impact of automation will be less, give low wage rate in 
these countries, automation will make this already considerable challenge more difficult. 
In Germany and Japan, by comparison, age be reduce the working-age population, 
which reduces the need for economic growth and the potential impact of automation. 

 Mix of economic sectors. The automation potential of national economy differs 
among country depend on the structure of their economy and, within the 
economy a a whole, on the mix of sectors, occupations, and their constituent work 
activities. Japan, for example, have a high automation potential than the United 
States because the weight of certain sector that be highly automatable, such a 
manufacturing, be high in Japan; 17 percent of job in Japan be in the manufacturing 
sector compare with 9 percent in the United States. And within the manufacturing 
sector itself, Japan be more susceptible to automation because a large proportion of the 
job involve task that can be more easily automated, such a production. 



3. Jobs gained: Scenarios for employment growth McKinsey Global Institute70 

These factor combine to create different outlook for the future of work in each country. In 
general, country with similar characteristic across these four factor could see broadly 
similar outcome from automation adoption, albeit with nuance for cultural and other 
differences. Some cluster be apparent. For example, developed country with age 
population and high wage that will accelerate automation adoption—such a Germany, 
Italy, and Japan—could follow a similar trajectory, a will developed country with young 
populations, such a Canada, France, Sweden, the United Kingdom, and the United 
States. Across the Middle East, country such a Kuwait, Saudi Arabia, and the United 
Arab Emirates share similarity on demographic and automation adoption and, to a lesser 
extent, on GDP per capita. Developing country with the young populations, include 
Egypt, Kenya, and Nigeria, will face similar challenge to grow aggregate demand, give 
their fast-growing workforce. 

Our scenario of new and additional labor demand, net of automation, while 
incomplete, suggest ample job creation to 2030 
We compare the number of job create by the seven select trend identify above with 
the number of job expect to be displace by automation. We be conscious that this 
exercise paint an incomplete picture. While our seven trend model the major source of 
new labor demand, they do not account for induced job demand creation nor the creation 
of novel work activity and occupations. One research study have found that, each year, 
roughly 0.5 percent of the US labor force be employ in an occupation that do not exist 
in the prior year—in other words, perform an entirely new set or combination of work 
activities. By 2030, this would imply that 9 percent of the US labor force could be employ 
in occupation that do not exist today.90 Just a many occupation today, such a search 
engine optimization, app designers, and website designers, would have be impossible 
to imagine in the pre-Internet era, we cannot foresee the new occupation that will arise in 
the future. 

In order to address the question, “Will there be enough work in the future?,” we compare the 
net effect of job displace by automation, job create by the seven trends, the creation 
of new work and other unsized labor demand, and demographic change in labor force 
size. Even limit our estimate of new and additional job creation to the select factor 
that we model, we find that our focus country could generate enough labor demand to 
offset the impact of automation and take into account change in the size of labor forces.91 
Nevertheless, our model from the seven trend indicates that the transition toward 2030 
look quite different depend on the country (see Exhibit 17). 

For instance, Japan be rich but project to grow slowly to 2030. It face the combination of 
slow job creation come from economic expansion and a large share of work that can be 
automate a a result of high wage and the structure of it economy. However, Japan will 
also see it workforce shrink by 2030 by four million people. In the step-up scenario, and 
consider the job in new occupation we cannot envision today, Japan’s net change in 
job could be roughly in balance.

around 200,000 job a year, create positive net employment of 80,000 job per year. More than half of the 
new job be high-skill. Shaping the future of work in Europe’s digital front-runners, McKinsey & Company, 
October 2017.

3. Jobs gained: Scenarios for employment growth McKinsey Global Institute72 

Like Japan, the United States and Germany also face significant workforce displacement 
from automation by 2030, but their project future growth—and hence new job creation— 
be higher. However, the United States have a grow workforce. In the step-up scenario, and 
consider new occupation that may arise, it be roughly in balance. Germany’s workforce 
will decline by three million by 2030, and it will have more than enough labor demand to 
employ all workers. 

At the other extreme be India: a fast-growing develop country with relatively modest 
potential for automation over the next 15 years, reflect low wage rates. Our analysis 
find that most occupational category be project to grow in India, reflect it strong 
economic expansion. However, India’s labor force be expect to grow by 138 million 
people by 2030, or about 30 percent. Employing these new entrant in formal sector job 
will require job creation on a much large scale than in the past. Automation will make 
this challenge more difficult; some fear “jobless growth” will make the challenge greater. 
However, our analysis suggests that India can create enough new job to offset automation 
and employ new entrants, for example by undertaking the investment in our step- 
up scenario. 

China and Mexico have high wage than India, and so be likely to see more automation. 
China still enjoys robust economic growth and will have a shrink workforce; like Germany, 
China’s problem could be a shortage of workers. Given net job creation a well a decline 
labor forces, China and Germany may need to explore different option such a accelerate 
automation adoption or immigration in order to fulfill the expect creation of grow future 
labor demand. Mexico’s rate of future economic expansion be more modest than China’s, 
and it workforce will grow by 15 million by 2030. Like the United States and Japan, our 
result suggest that Mexico may need the extra job creation from the step-up scenario plus 
innovation in new occupation to make full use of it workforce. 

Our macroeconomic model highlight the critical importance of rapid 
reemployment of worker displace by automation 
The analysis of net job creation give expect automation rate and the seven trend be an 
informative but incomplete exercise, even after adjust for labor supply change and the 
creation of new occupations. This be because the analysis above do not take into account 
dynamic interaction among the trends, such a the impact of automation investment and 
automation-related unemployment on GDP growth rate or economy-wide average wage 
rates. Thus, to develop a perspective on the potential net impact of automation and job 
creation potential of the economy, we use a general equilibrium model in order to triangulate 
our result with the analysis from the seven trend (see Box 4, “Modeling the economic 
impact of automation”). 

The overall result of the general equilibrium model be the same a the result from the seven 
trends: it show that labor market will generally be flexible enough to absorb the worker 
displace by automation. Furthermore, like the outcome of the analysis from the seven 
trends, difference arise between country in the expect transition toward 2030. The 
general equilibrium model point toward a strong distinction in the expect transition 
between the advanced economy and the emerge economy we focus on in this report. 



73Jobs lost, job gained: Workforce transition in a time of automationMcKinsey Global Institute 

In advanced economies, all scenario result in full employment by 2030, but the 
transition may include a period of high unemployment and wage adjustment 
For the three advanced economy we focus on in this report—Germany, Japan, and 
the United States—economies be flexible enough to absorb most if not all the displace 
worker by 2030 in all reemployment scenarios. The pace at which displace worker be 
reemployed be critical: low reemployment lead to high medium-term unemployment, 
while in the high reemployment scenario, the labor displace by automation will be 
reemployed fast enough such that the unemployment rate do not rise (Exhibit 18). 

Automation will also have a wage impact. Our model result in a temporary increase 
in wage in all reemployment scenario because of increase productivity a a result of 
automation. But, a with the unemployment rate, wage will also depend on the pace of 
reemployment. In the low reemployment scenarios, wage fall in the long-run in response 
to high medium-term unemployment. This lead to a low labor share of income, a 
the gain of automation in this scenario primarily accrue to capital owners, not laborers. 
Conversely, in the high reemployment scenario, wage growth be expect to persist, 
and thus the decades-long trend of decline labor share of income slows or even revers 
by 2040. 

Box 4. Modeling the economic impact of automation 

1 The structure of the model be anchor in the academic literature on economic growth models. For detail see 
Shifting tides: Global economic scenario for 2015–25, McKinsey & Company, September 2015. 

We use McKinsey & Company’s Global Growth Model to model the dynamic impact 
of automation on the economy of our six focus countries.1 This be a supply-side general 
equilibrium macroeconomic model that cover more than 100 country with data from 
1960 through 2015. 

In the model, we directly include three factor by which automation affect economic 
growth: labor displacement, result in worker lose their jobs; capital investment need 
to implement the automation technologies; and an increase in productivity growth, a firm 
employ more capital per worker. These input to the Global Growth Model be derive 
from our automation research. To calculate the impact on productivity, we estimate the 
imply productivity growth that would be need to maintain constant total output with 
few workers, under the rationale that firm would not adopt the new technology unless it 
produce at least the same level of output. In reality, this be likely to be an underestimate of 
the impact. Our experience work with firm and our prior automation research show 
that automation often result in high quality output and significantly high level of output 
a well. 

We also model how quickly displace worker be employ in new jobs, or the 
reemployment rate. Not every displace worker will enter unemployment; some will have the 
skill and the opportunity to transition quickly into a new role at the same company or with a 
new firm. Indeed, each year million of US worker leave a job and find a new one. Between 
2013 and 2015, 66 percent of displace worker in the United States found a new job by 
the end of the period; 49 percent of worker displace between 2007 and 2009, during the 
financial crisis, be reemployed by the begin of 2010. 

We model four reemployment rate scenarios—low, medium, high, and full reemployment. 
The reemployment rate model differ by country and be estimate from literature and 
adjust for labor market flexibility factors. 



3. Jobs gained: Scenarios for employment growth McKinsey Global Institute74 

In emerge economies, automation’s impact on employment be small than in 
advanced economy 
The impact of automation be lesser in China, India, and Mexico than in the advanced 
economy we focus on, accord to our simulation. First, automation rate be expect 
to be lower, lead to small percentage of displace workers, low level of capital 
investment, and small productivity increases. As such, unemployment be not expect to 
rise significantly, nor be model wage or GDP growth sensitive to the automation effect 
under any of the reemployment scenarios. 

For more detailed analysis of each of our six focus countries, see the country impact section 
start on page 91. 

••• 

Automation will displace job around the world by 2030, but in our analysis demand for 
certain type of labor—from care provider to builders—will also increase, spur by the 
rise consumer class in emerge economy and the grow health-care need of age 
population in nation from Germany and Italy to China and Japan, among other trends. Our 
dynamic model of the US and other economy suggests that enough new job will be 
create to return to full employment by 2030, but the transition could be difficult. Depending 
on the rate at which displace worker be reemployed, the unemployment rate could rise in 
the short- to medium-term, and wage may fall thereafter. Moreover, our analysis highlight 
difference between the sort of job that will be lose from automation, and those that will be 
gain from a range of trends. Even if there be enough work to go around in 2030, million 
of individual may need to find new job and possibly acquire new skills. We examine these 
trend in the next chapter.

Midcareer retrain will be a major challenge 

© Hero Images/Getty Images 



The combination of labor displacement by automation and change demand for 
occupation that we have outline in the previous chapter will have enormous implication 
for individual workers. We estimate that 60 million to 375 million individual around the world 
may need to transition to new occupational category by 2030 , in the event of midpoint or 
early automation adoption (although that number would be negligible in 2030 in our edge- 
case slow automation adoption scenario). Nearly all job will involve a shift mix of task 
and activities. 

Within occupations, the mix of activity and the capability require will skew toward 
more personal interaction and more advanced level of cognitive capabilities. Educational 
requirement will also change: net of automation, a great share of job in the future will 
likely require high level of educational attainment. In advanced economies, that include 
increase demand for job that currently require a college degree (or other advanced 
training). At the same time, employer and worker may need to take a more fine-grained 
approach to identify skill that be the most important. 

Our result also suggest that income polarization in advanced economy include 
the United States could be exacerbated.92 We do not dynamically model how wage 
might change for individual occupation by 2030, but base on current wages, we find 
that in most advanced countries, middle-wage job may decline the most a a result of 
automation. Growing occupation in these country will tend to be either those that be less 
remunerative, for example, retail salesperson or childcare workers, or quite the opposite: 
highly paid jobs, such a software engineers. In develop economy include China, 
however, we see the opposite trend. Here the strong job growth will likely be for middle- 
wage occupations, include service and construction jobs. 

THE MIX OF ACTIVITIES WITHIN OCCUPATIONS WILL CHANGE 
For nearly all occupations, automation will change the mix of activity that human perform, 
a some task be take over by machine or software. Over time, occupational definition 
may change, a the boundary between different occupation become blurred. Already, 
physician assistant and register nurse carry out many task that doctor use to do, 
such a handle routine case or give shots. For their part, doctor now write up memo 
and enter data rather than dictate to assistants. 

With automation, such shift may become more pronounced, although the specific will 
vary by country. In Germany, worker of the future will likely spend more time on activity 
that require apply expertise (+2.9 billion full-time equivalent hours), interact with 
stakeholder (+1.5 billion hours), and manage people (+1.4 billion hours), and less time 
on predictable physical activity (-2.6 billion hours), collect data (-2.5 billion hours) and 
processing data (-2.1 billion hours), where machine already exceed human performance 
(Exhibit 19). In other words, many of the rote activity that have dominate the workplace 
in the post-industrial age will be take over by machines. Work activity will shift to human 
interaction and work in unpredictable environments—and will also require increase 
application of expertise. Similar shift will occur in other high-wage, advanced economies.

Card, eds., 2011.

advanced-economy counterparts, such a interact with stakeholder and apply 
professional expertise.

only a relatively limited number of jobs. This will be a challenge for education, training, and 
skill assessment models, which for now do not always emphasize “soft skills” such a social 
and emotional reason and sensing.

4. Implications for skill and wage McKinsey Global Institute80 

Additional job will often require a high level of performance across many capabilities. For 
instance, machine will be able to perform work activity require basic level of retrieve 
information and understand natural language—but job require high level of these 
capability will grow. In advanced economies, physical capability will be less in demand 
a a percentage of the activity hour demand in the economy. In develop economy 
such a India, give the increase in demand for physical activities, demand for physical 
capability will accordingly grow a a percentage of the new activity hour demanded. 
The shift in activity and underlie capability require will touch almost all job in the 
economy, to vary degrees—but especially in advanced economies. As one example, the 
work of retail salesperson in these economy could change significantly; about 20 percent 
of their current work activity could be automate in the midpoint scenario. The rote 
aspect of the job such a processing transaction and gathering product information may 
be automated. Retail worker instead may turn their attention to the more people-focused 
side of the job: greet customer and answer questions, for example, or suggest 
new products. 

The capability shift be not limited to front-line jobs. Many worker in occupation with high 
educational requirement who spend much of their time collect and processing data 
could experience a significant shift in their work activities. Financial managers, for example, 
could spend less time monitoring cashflow or approve expenditures, and instead 
have time to focus on more managerial function such a supervise employee and 
advise others on business matters. Professionals of all stripe be quickly realize the 
grow importance of “soft skills”—although understand the implication of numerical 
calculation will continue to be important. On LinkedIn, the professional networking site, for 
example, professional be increasingly develop and marketing themselves around these 
softer skills, which be less automatable (Exhibit 21).93
