This change nature of activity and capability have important implication for the 
requirement and aim of job training, which will be particularly relevant in time of transition. 
For example, a activity require basic level of performance be automate in the United 
States, training effort will need to focus on capability for which automation be more 
challenging, such a social and emotional capabilities. Increasingly across occupations, 
worker will be value for strong interpersonal skill and advanced reasoning. As these 
skill be often developed through guide experience, worker will likely spend more time 
be coached in apprentice-like environments. The worker of the future will still need to 
apply expertise and judgment, so training to promote fluency with and understand of 
information will remain important. 

GROWING JOB CATEGORIES HAVE HIGHER EDUCATIONAL REQUIREMENTS 
THAN THE WORK DISPLACED BY AUTOMATION 
Across our six focus countries, a great share of job in the future—accounting for both the 
effect from automation and the additional labor demand from the seven trends—are likely 
to demand increase level of education (Exhibit 22).94 

Defining and measure the skill require to perform well in any job be a difficult and 
imprecise task. In our modeling, we look at several different measure of workforce skills. 
First, we consider the formal educational requirement of each occupation: secondary 
school degree, Associate’s, Bachelor’s, and graduate degrees. Educational requirement 
by occupation be fairly well standardize globally and allow for cross-country comparisons. 
However, even within an occupation, there will be a range of degree that exist worker 
have obtained; we focus on the educational level that be typically required.95 We also look 
at how activity within occupation will change, which give some indication of the type 
of skill that will be more or less in demand, and we consider the capability require to 
perform those activities—such a cognitive skills, creativity, social and emotional skills. 
Other definition of skill and credential be equally valid and important, but be beyond 
the scope of this report. Many practitioner and researcher today focus on unbundling 
traditional degree into well-defined credential that can be obtain by demonstrate 
mastery of specific skills. This be particularly attractive for midcareer worker who cannot 
afford to spend year earn a traditional degree, or who have accumulate valuable 
experience on the job. Some credential be for quite narrow skills: program in a certain 
computer language, or master one specific type of mechanical expertise.96 

94 We do not model change skill requirement for occupations, and assume that educational requirement for 
an occupation in all country be the same a in the United States. See technical appendix for details. 

95 Economists note the general trend of “degree inflation.” For instance, in the United States, while 80 percent of 
job opening for executive assistant list a bachelor’s degree a a requirement, few than half of exist EAs 
have that degree. See Peter Capelli, Will college pay off? A guide to the most important financial decision you’ll 
ever make, PublicAffairs, 2015. 

96 For a discussion of skill and credentials, see The narrow ladder: The value of industry certifications, 
Burningglass Technologies, October 2007; David Deming et al, “The value of postsecondary credential in the 
labor market: An experimental study,” American Economic Review, 2016; Rajeev Darolia et al., “Do employer 
prefer worker who attend for-profit colleges? Evidence from a field experiment,” Journal of Policy Analysis 
and Management, volume 34, issue 4, fall 2015.

affected by automation and have a net reduction in labor demand, base on the factor we 
have model (Exhibit 23). 

Not all job in these category will disappear, but in 2030, demand for the activity they 
currently perform will be low in all of our model scenarios. In contrast, occupation 
require a college or graduate degree will see the most growth a a percentage of job 
in the economy. Occupations that require training on top of a traditional post-secondary 
degree (for example, career, technical, vocational training) or two-year associates’ degree

have a mixed outlook. As a share of job in the economies, country such a Japan will 
see increase demand for associates’ and equivalent degrees, primarily from middle-skill 
health-care occupation such a nurse and paramedics. Meanwhile, country such a 
Germany will see decrease demand, primarily because of a great share of automatable 
office support job such a clerk and secretaries. 

The relative growth of job require high educational attainment be not a new 
observation.97 Nor be the increase of skill requirement necessarily a break from historical 
trend: the manufacturing revolution saw increase need for literacy training that spawn 
movement toward secondary education include the High School Movement in the United 
States, which we discus in the next chapter. 

However, many of the middle-income job of the past that require only a secondary 
education or less, and minimal training, will likely face significant displacement in an 
automate world. These job include heavy truck driver and office clerks, both of which 
have high technical potential for automation over our model time horizon, and whose 
current middle-level wage raise the economic incentive to deploy automation. Many 
additional job require low and middle level of educational attainment could be created, 
driven particularly by the care economy, such a the rise of nurse and nursing assistants. 
However, for many job that require no or only secondary education, the additional future 
labor demand will not fully make up for the job displace by automation. This would mean 
that many displace worker would need to retrain and/or raise their educational level to 
gain employment in one of the in-demand occupations. 

The seven trend we model alone suggest that overall new demand for work currently 
require a college degree could be sufficient to balance the reduction in demand 
for work activity lose to automation in occupation require college completion, in 
the United States, a well a in Germany (where the net model demand for these 
occupation increases). 

However, that do not obviate the need for college and advanced degree holder to 
retrain, a their activity will change. Some will switch to job that require a similar level 
of educational attainment, but very different skills. The come wave of technology 
deployment, bolster by advance in machine learn and artificial intelligence, have 
significant potential to automate work activity previously thought to be the exclusive 
domain of highly-trained humans. In the midpoint automation scenario, 13 percent of the 
current work activity perform in occupation that require college or advanced degree 
in the United States could be displaced. Even within a give field, certain occupation will 
fare good than others. In the United States, for example, the high-skill job of application 
software developer have large net additional demand (we model nearly ten time a many 
job create a displaced). Meanwhile certain technology support job include computer 
system administrator and support specialist may see a cool of demand, because 
a substantial portion of their current activity have relatively high technical automation 
potential. Similarly, in US financial services, the varied skill require of management 
analyst could see high demand, while financial manager and security sale agent 
could face substantial automation of their current work activity with limited source of 
new demand.

Workers displace by automation may need to invest time to acquire new skills, either 
through formal education or other training programs. Conversely, job themselves may 
need to be redesign to accommodate an influx of new worker with less sector-specific 
training and overall education. The transition could be varied and lead to friction in the 
labor market and potentially high unemployment in the short-run. But other step could 
be take to make market function better. For example, define job requirements, a well 
a provide training and certification, base on specific skills, could improve the match 
of labor supply and demand, a these skill can be much more fine-grained than traditional 
educational degrees, and can often be acquire in less time than finish a multi-year 
degree program. 

While some worker who be displace by automation will be able to find a job in a similar 
function and with similar educational requirement (for example, a displace cashier find 
a new job a a retail salesperson), many will not, and will need to gain additional skills, which 
may or may not actually raise their educational level. This be discuss in a subsequent 
section of this chapter. 

Additional job creation in our step-up scenario could help mitigate some of the impact of 
automation, with additional investment in infrastructure, buildings, and energy transition 
potentially create new work for worker with low and mid-level educational attainment. In 
all, in the United States, we estimate additional demand for mid-skill labor of up to 1 million 
jobs, net of automation, in the step-up scenario. Demand for occupation require post- 
secondary education also increase in the step-up scenario. Engineers and cost estimator 
will be brought in to develop infrastructure projects, for example, and various professionals, 
from accountant to research analysts, will also see increase demand. 

In develop countries, the large number of new job will be those require 
secondary education or technical skill 
In emerge economies, the rate of job growth be high for occupation that require a 
college degree or more, but the absolute amount of job growth be high for occupation 
with a secondary education diploma. 

In China, for instance, occupation currently require college and advanced degree will 
see increase demand—in the case of those with college degrees, this could be a high 
a 22 million additional net job under our step-up scenario. However, the large absolute 
number of job created—almost 60 million in the step-up scenario—currently require only 
a secondary school diploma, with significant increase demand for retail salespeople, 
nursing assistants, childcare worker and others accord to our analysis. Despite the 
dual threat of automation (particularly for manufacturing, which make up about 19 percent 
of employment in China) and other increase productivity, significant demand will be 
create for lower-skill work, primarily due to rise prosperity. Also, job with low educational 
attainment requirement in these country be generally low wage, and be thus less 
economically attractive to automate. 

India, meanwhile, will see the large new demand net of automation for worker with a 
secondary education. According to our analysis, a many a 100 million new job could 
be create for Indians with secondary education—even after accounting for the effect of 
automation—as rise prosperity will create a surge of new labor demand for construction, 
retail, and health care and education jobs, among others. The large country in the 
develop world, in short, should not have a shortage of labor demand across any 
education level, but the significant increase in work require completion of secondary 
education indicates a need for India to upgrade it school system.98

Our finding shed some light on the potential size of the worker transition that will be 
necessary in the year to 2030—and the immense training effort that will be necessary 
a the nature of some work changes. We estimate that up to 75 million worker may have 
to switch occupational category and/or educational level in the midpoint automation 
scenario (Exhibit 24).99 If automation technology be developed and adopt sooner, 
those number grow rapidly; in the early automation adoption scenario that we analyze, 
a many a 375 million people may need to change occupational category and/or 
educational levels. However, our step-up scenario reduce our estimate of the number 
of people need to make these type of transitions, with additional labor demand trend 
offset more job displacement from automation within occupational categories. Not 
show on the exhibit, in the slowest automation scenario, the number of require transition 
be significantly reduce (almost negligible in most countries).

Some of these people who be displace will seek new education and many will need new 
skills. People will also need to learn new skill within their occupation a activity change, 
particularly where those activity be augment by automation. As a point of calibration, 
in our rise income trend, we project that between 190 million and 205 million student 
will be in tertiary education in 2030.100 In the fast automation scenario that we model, 
the number of people that will need to transition between 2016 and 2030 be almost double 
that number. 

These transition could surface friction in the labor market, for reason range from low 
wage to cultural and gender bias, but also present new opportunity for work and job 
growth. For example, men could find opportunity to retrain into job in which woman have 
dominate in some countries, such a nursing in the United States, and vice versa.101 

WAGE POLARIZATION MAY CONTINUE IN SOME ADVANCED ECONOMIES 
Middle-wage job have felt the impact of automation in the past decade in the United 
States, create a polarization phenomenon. Modeling the potential impact of automation, 
we find that this polarization could continue and become exacerbate in advanced 
economies—but not universally. Indeed, one of our finding be that the potential wage impact 
of automation and future labor demand could vary considerably among countries. We also 
find that any polarization of wage be not due solely to skill gaps; some of the additional 
demand for middle-skill job be in those that be currently paid low wage in country such 
a the United States. 

In advanced economies, high-wage occupation see the most growth net 
of automation 
Academic literature examine the recent impact of technology on wage have found that 
a distinct pattern of “hollowing” or a decline in middle-skill and middle-wage job with 
growth in high- and low-wage occupation (see Box 5, “Our prior research on income and 
equality trends”).102 

Our analysis be base on current average wage for each occupation in each country. 
Modeling wage over time by occupation base on the dynamic of labor supply and 
demand be outside the scope of this study, beyond the top-down analysis of wage impact 
that we described in the previous chapter. Nonetheless, our examination of job growth at 
different current wage level offer some indication of what the future may hold. 

In the United States and Germany, in our trendline labor demand and midpoint automation 
scenarios, occupation in the top 30 percent of average current wage experience net job 
growth by 2030 (Exhibit 25). This hold for the United States even in the model step- 
up scenario; in the step-up scenario for Germany, rise need for job such a nursing 
assistant be expect to increase demand for middle-wage jobs. The large decline be in 
job in the middle of the wage distribution. Occupations in the low 30 percent of wage 
decline, but this reflect the net impact of modest growth in low-wage occupation such 
a retail salespeople, and decline occupation such a hand packers/packagers and 
cafeteria cooks.

Box 5. Our prior research on income and equality trend 

1 Ibid. Digital America, McKinsey Global Institute, December 2015. 
2 Poorer than their parents? Flat or fall income in advanced economies, McKinsey Global Institute, July 2016. 

The trend we identify in this report for both low- and 
middle-wage job and high-wage, high-skill job overlap 
with some of the structural shift in the labor market in the 
past few decade that we have examine in prior reports. 
Among our findings: 

“Superstar” effect and the hollow of the middle. 
Digitization have already affected the occupational and skill 
mix of the US workforce. Since the 1980s, employment 
in both low-skill and high-skill job have increased, while 
middle-skill job have declined. With many routine 
production and assembly task be automated, most 
of the growth at the low-skill end of the spectrum have 
be in occupation such a restaurant workers, home 
health-care aides, security guards, maintenance workers, 
and other role that provide in-person service that be 
less susceptible to automation. At the same time, idea- 
intensive sector have be capture a large share of 
the overall corporate profit pool. There be now a premium 
on creative and cognitive task that improves overall 
productivity of highly skilled workers. The result be an 
increasingly two-tiered labor market.1 

Flat and fall income in advanced economies. 
With only occasional exceptions, most income group in 
advanced economy have experienced steady income 
advancement since World War II, but that change 
abruptly in the past decade. Between 2005 and 2014, our 
research found that about two-thirds of income group 
have either flat or fall market income (wages and 
capital), although in some country include the United 
States government tax and transfer cushion the 
blow for disposable income. While economic recession 
and slow recovery after the global financial crisis be 
a primary cause, other long-run factors—including a 
decline in the wage share of GDP, aging, and shrink 
household size—will continue to weigh on income in the 
future. The decline in wage share have take place despite 
rise productivity, suggest that productivity and 
income have become disconnected. The distribution 
of this wage share among different income segment 
have also be uneven: since 1993, household in the 
uppermost income segment in the country we look 
at receive a grow share of the total wages, even a 
the share for low- and middle-income segment have 
either stagnate or fallen.2

In the United States, the pattern of polarization be clearer if we look at the net job growth by

4. Implications for skill and wage McKinsey Global Institute90 

In develop countries, middle-wage occupation could experience 
strong growth 
The potential wage trend picture be quite different in emerge economies. In India, 
occupation at all wage level be boost by demand from rise prosperity, while the 
economy will largely be shield from automation because of low wages. In China, our 
scenario show that middle-wage job will rise similarly. Across both countries, the main 
occupation that be drive this growth in middle-wage job involve customer interaction. 
Job growth be particularly pronounce for cashier and retail salespeople, which be 
middle-wage job in these markets. While in the developed world many of these job may 
be automated, in India and China, wage be low enough that automation rate will likely 
be constrain in the 2030 timeframe (modeled at under 10 percent). The story be a positive 
one for wage advancement and the development of the middle class: in India, for example, 
if farmworkers (with annual salary of $1,752) find themselves out of a job because of 
productivity improvements, many new and more highly-paid service job will sprout up in 
host (average wage of $2,204), and for retail salesperson ($4,101). 

In these develop countries, the challenge will be to continue to create more high-wage 
work opportunity for high-skill workers, and move a great proportion of worker into 
high wage jobs. While India will have high demand for high-wage work, this type of work 
in China be more susceptible to automation compare with other develop countries, 
accord to our model, primarily because of high wage rates. Computer support 
specialist and law clerks, two high-wage occupations, have an automation potential in the 
midpoint adoption scenario of 34 percent and 35 percent, respectively. Overall, then, while 
develop country may have less to worry about when it come to create labor demand 
to offset the effect of automation in the near term, policy maker and business leader may 
want to focus on the creation of more gainful and desirable employment.103 

••• 

Work will change in the next decade and beyond, with significant implication both for 
skill requirement and wages. While our research suggests that these trend will play out 
differently among countries, some commonality be apparent. One be that educational 
requirement and performance capabilities, include for soft skill such a social 
and emotional reasoning, will become ever more important. In advanced economy 
include the United States, middle-wage worker could continue to see a shrink 
pool of opportunity a automation outpaces new job creation. As many a 375 million 
individual around the world will need to switch occupational categories. Providing retrain 
opportunity at scale will be imperative. What should the policy priority be in this change 
workplace? In the follow chapter, we look at how the transition brought about by 
automation can best be managed.

Special feature cover RHS 

In the follow section, we highlight scenario for 
the future of work in our six focus countries: China,

The chart show a range of possible outcome for 
job displace by automation adoption to 2030 and 
scenario for future job that could be create by 
seven catalyst of labor demand, a well a by new 
occupation that could arise. For the automation of 
work, we consider scenario base on the speed of 
adoption of automation technologies. For potential

growth by occupation, we estimate the number of 
worker who may need to change occupational

These chart should not be take a forecast or 
predictions. Rather, they illustrate a range of possible 
outcomes. The scenario we ass in this report 
may overstate or understate the actual impact of 
automation on work and future labor demand. For 
example, if automation adoption be more rapid than 
even our early adoption scenario, this could 
mean that more work would be automate than we 
estimate. On the other hand, automation might boost 
productivity growth more than we have modeled, and 
this could lead to strong aggregate demand growth 
and more job creation across the economy than we 
have modeled. Box E2 on page 21 discus the 
many factor that could change our result for any 
individual country.

5. MANAGING THE WORKFORCE 
TRANSITIONS 

Brace yourself. All country will face large-scale workforce transition over the next 15 year 
a automation displaces some worker and labor demand shifts. Enabling and smooth 
these transition will be a significant challenge for policy maker and business leaders. 
Some policy choice could substantially improve the employment outcomes, include 
speed reemployment. Indeed, it may take a Marshall Plan-scale initiative of sustain 
investment by the public and private sector in new training model and workforce transition 
program to address all the social, political, and economic issue that automation will raise. 

In this chapter, we discus four priority that could make a critical difference: maintain 
robust economic growth to support job creation; scale up workforce retrain and 
skill development programs, particularly for midcareer workers; improve labor market 
dynamism; and provide income and transition support to displace workers. We 
explore some of the choice that could be consider and cite example of successful 
interventions. These idea should not be take a exhaustive or prescriptive, but rather a 
prompt to spur discussion and new ideas. Specific choice will vary base on country 
circumstance and societal choices. 

MAINTAINING ROBUST ECONOMIC GROWTH AND INNOVATION TO SUPPORT 
JOB CREATION 
The start point be an economic one: sustain robust aggregate demand growth be 
essential for enable employment growth. Economies that be not expand do not create 
new jobs. Indeed, the experience of the past decade have underscored the linger negative 
employment effect of insufficient demand. 

Appropriate fiscal and monetary policy can be deployed to ensure that demand 
growth be vibrant, and it go beyond the scope of this report to catalog the appropriate 
macroeconomic policies. Nonetheless, the importance of sustain demand growth 
cannot be overemphasized. Much have also be write about potential way to encourage 
innovation and entrepreneurship, from investment in research and development 
and human capital, to investment capital, and lower barrier to entry for innovative 
businesses.104 Supporting innovation and technological diffusion be critical, include the 
adoption of automation technology themselves, a these advance be the fundamental 
source of long-run productivity, growth, and prosperity, a well a the creation of new 
business models, occupations, and work activities. To do so will require an effective and 
balance system for encourage the development and deployment of intellectual property, 
a high-skill scientific and engineering workforce, and public or private funding for basic 
research and it commercialization. New business creation, start-up communities, and 
dynamic firm entry and exit be also essential.105 For small city that lack a diversified 
economy and where the principal employer leaves, more holistic economic revival plan 
be needed.106 

104 See, for instance, Brad Feld, Startup communities: Building an entrepreneurial ecosystem in your city, Wiley, 
October 2012 and Enrico Moretti, The new geography of jobs, Mariner Books, March 2013. 

105 See Making it in America, McKinsey Global Institute, June 2017. 
106 See Amy Goldstein, Janesville: An American story, Simon & Schuster, 2017. 



5. Managing the workforce transition McKinsey Global Institute106 

Actions can be take not only at the national level, but also locally. MGI study of city 
around the world, along with a grow body of academic research, illustrate the vast 
difference in economic growth and prosperity that arise among city and region of the 
same country.107 The last 20 year of globalization and technological change have result 
in many community in the United States and Europe suffer large-scale job losses—but 
some of these community have also show that revive growth through knowledge- 
base economy be possible. A common pattern emerges: harness intellectual capital, 
often found in universities, with private sector R&D and local government willing to ensure 
workforce training to meet the new demand.108 

Many policymakers, in both advanced and develop country such a India, worry 
about “jobless growth.”109 Since the 2008 global financial crisis, it have become clear that 
not all source of GDP growth have an equal impact on employment creation. Growth in 
industry that be heavily capital-intensive or those that be highly automate will not have 
the same impact on job creation. To support broad-based job creation, some country may 
provide incentive to labor-intensive service sectors, such a health care, education, and 
construction. Targeted initiative may also be used. Catalyzing public and private investment 
for infrastructure, include the housing and commercial building need in urbanize 
country in the develop world, not only support long-term economic growth but also 
have the potential to create large-scale employment in the near-term. Supporting measure 
to shift energy to renewable sources, manage and mitigate climate change, and boost 
energy efficiency through increase digitization of the sector likewise have global economic 
benefit while boost near-term employment. The step-up scenario we outline in Chapter 
3 reflect the potential impact of these type of “no-regret” initiatives; we estimate that 
roughly 150 million to 300 million job could be create incrementally on top of the trendline 
scenario job a a result. Importantly, these initiative create many middle-skill jobs, such a 
those for electricians, carpenters, crane operators, and other trades. 

In prior research, we examine the critical role of migration and gender parity, both of which 
amount to low-cost way to boost aggregate demand in the short- and medium-term. For 
example, a much a 70 percent of population growth in urban area go forward could 
come from migration—with key challenge on how migrant can be integrate effectively.110 

SCALING UP JOB RETRAINING AND WORKFORCE SKILL DEVELOPMENT 
Providing job retrain and enable individual to learn marketable new skill throughout 
their lifetime will be a central challenge for some country over the next decade and 
beyond. As we have show in this report, hundred of million of people will likely need to 
find new job a automation advances, and even more will need to learn new skills, include 
how to work seamlessly with machines. 

In recent years, some country have experienced significant challenge in try to create 
the condition in which worker displace by globalization and technology quickly find 
new high-quality employment. The result for many individual have be a series of lower- 
wage job with limited opportunity for advancement and low rate of labor market 

107 Urban world: Mapping the economic power of cities, McKinsey Global Institute, March 2011; Ibid. Enrico 
Moretti, The new geography of jobs, March 2013. 

108 Antoine van Agtmael and Fred Bakker, The smartest place on Earth: Why rustbelt be the emerge 
hotspot of global innovation, PublicAffairs, March 2016. 

109 Asian experience on growth, employment and poverty: An overview with special reference to the finding of 
some recent case studies, UNDP and International Labour Organization, January 2007. 

110 Ibid. The power of parity, McKinsey Global Institute, September 2015, and People on the move: Global

participation.111 The social consequence can be dire.112 The challenge for the next decade 
will be to create effective workforce retrain program at scale. This could require action 
by policy makers, business leaders, and educators, a well a individuals. 

History offer example of large-scale program to improve the skill 
of worker 
At a time when million of individual will need new skills, public funding for job training 
program be fall in many country (Exhibit 27).113 Between 1993 and 2015, spending on 
workforce training program a a percent of GDP fell from 0.08 percent to 0.03 percent in 
the United States, while Japanese spending drop from 0.03 percent to 0.01 percent. In 
Germany—still one of the large spenders—outlays for training fell from 0.57 percent of GDP 
to 0.2 percent. 

Nonetheless, we can find example of societies, past and present, which have chosen 
to invest in education and workforce training, with impressive results. The United States 
provide example of two at-scale investment in the past century: the US High School 
Movement (1910 to 1940), which make attend secondary school the norm for all children, 
and the 1944 GI Bill, which enable million of return war veteran to obtain a tertiary 
education (see Box 6, “The US High School Movement and the GI Bill dramatically raise 
educational attainment of American workers”). Academic researcher have found that the 
sizable human capital increase enable by these program account for a measurable 
share of the rise in income over those decades, create a large and increasingly affluent 
American middle class.114 

More recently, Singapore implement an innovative form of support aim at upgrade 
skill a part of it effort to promote growth and competitiveness in 23 industries.115 
Through the “SkillsFuture Initiative,” introduce by the Ministry of Education in January 
2016, the government provide all Singaporeans age 25 and above credit of about $400, 
to pay for approve work-skills related courses. More than 18,000 such course be 
available, and a of December 2016, more than 120,000 people—some 4 percent of the 
resident population age 25 and above—had use the initiative to take courses, more than 
60 percent of them over 40.116 

Businesses can play a significant role in training and retrain worker 
Companies also have a significant role to play in training and retrain workers. This go 
beyond a purely social role or sense of civic responsibility: business leader will be on the 
front line of automation and will have the early and most detailed knowledge about what 
type of skill they will need a they move to adopt the technologies. In the United States, 
some company be work directly with education provider to give employee an 
opportunity to raise their educational and skill level (see Box 7, “Some US company be 
work with educational providers, even a spending on corporate training declines”).

1 Ibid. Claudia Goldin, “America’s graduation from high school,” June 1998.

The US High School Movement (1910 to 1940) propel 
a sharp increase in high school enrollment and graduation 
rates, make a high school qualification the norm.1 

In 1910, most student left education after primary school 
to work in agriculture or other low-skill jobs. Those who 
attend high school do so primarily to gain entrance to 
college. However, the economy have begin produce 
large number of job in city that demand a formal 
education beyond primary school. This demand lead to a 
grassroots movement: more high school be built and 
the curriculum shift from teach skill “for college” 
to skill “for life.” Vocational (including commercial), 
technical or manual, and industrial course be rapidly 
incorporate into most high school curricula. 

Secondary school enrollment increase spectacularly, 
from 18 percent in 1910 to 73 percent in 1940. Graduation 
rate for 17-year-olds rise from 9 percent to 51 percent in 
the same period (Exhibit 28). 

Higher educational attainment have an impact on 
incomes. On an aggregate level, national income per 
worker grow annually at a 1.48 percent average from 1929 
to 1982. One study attribute 28 percent of the economic 
growth to human capital accumulation and technological 

progress, with change at the secondary school level 
be quantitatively the most significant driver to the 
increase educational stock of Americans in the first 
three-quarters of the 20th century.2 

The GI Bill of 1944, create to help integrate World War II 
veteran back into civilian life, be instrumental in make 
a college education mainstream.3 Studies estimate 
that 1.4 million people-years of undergraduate training 
have be lose because of the war.4 The bill provide 
all veteran dedicate payment of tuition and living 
expense to attend high school, college, or vocational 
or technical school. By 1956, just under eight million 
veteran have use the GI Bill educational benefits, with 
2.2 million attend college or university and an 
additional 5.6 million engage in some kind of training 
program.5 In all, just over half of all veteran tapped the 
education benefit in some form, greatly exceed the 
government’s projections.6 Veterans account for a 
many a 49 percent of all enrol student at college 
and universities—and create demand for growth of a 
world-class university system.7 

The GI Bill also change perception about college 
attendance, make it accessible to the average person 
and not simply reserve for an elite.

Traditionally, educational degrees—especially in the field of science, technology, 
engineering, and mathematics (STEM)—have act a a signal of talent for job hiring. 
Lacking other marker of skills, many employee focus on whether candidate have 
multi-year, expensive degrees. As the workplace evolves, however, more granular and 
varied definition of skill be emerge a critically important markers, more so even than a 
college education in some cases. This focus on an individual’s skill rather than educational 
credential be gain momentum with companies, state governments, and nonprofit 
organizations. Markle Foundation, for example, have begin an initiative in Colorado to foster 
skills-oriented hiring, training, and education. Among company support it, Microsoft 
have announce a grant of more than $25 million to support the program, know a Skillful.117

5. Managing the workforce transition McKinsey Global Institute112 

Apprenticeships primarily benefit youth newly enter the workforce. Germany’s “dual- 
system” apprenticeship program combine work- and school-based learn be the 
best known, and prepares student for a successful transition to full-time employment. 
Beginning in secondary school, student choose a vocational or a college-preparation 
track. Traditionally, this allocation be do through student test scores, but today there be 
more flexibility. Vocational student spend part of their time attend high school, learn 
the basic curriculum, and part of their time work and earn at an employer. The system 
offer qualification in a broad spectrum of profession and adapts to the change need of 
the labor market. 

A major strength of the dual system be the high degree of engagement and ownership on 
the part of employers, although a web of check and balance at the national and local 
level ensures that the short-term need of employer do not distort broader educational 
and economic goals.118 The system have produce impressive results: about one-third of 
German student be educate in the apprenticeship system, which be a widely respect 
career path. 

Like Germany, Switzerland also have a robust apprenticeship pipeline—indeed, nearly 
70 percent of Swiss high-school student choose vocational training. In this track, student 
rotate between school and workplace settings, and receive a salary throughout their 
training.119 Some study show that student in Switzerland who opt into vocational training 
over general education on average attain high lifetime earnings.120 

Germany’s apprenticeship system be be emulate by other countries. For instance, 
German auto manufacturer in the United States now offer apprenticeship (although the 
program remains small). South Korea have adopt the apprenticeship model at scale (see 
Box 8, “South Korea’s Meister school apprenticeship system”). 

118 Vocational education and training in Germany: Strengths, challenges, and recommendations, OECD, 2010. 
119 Gold standard: The Swiss vocational education and training system, Center on International Education 

Benchmarking, March 2015.

Box 8. South Korea’s Meister school apprenticeship system 

1 Ministry of Education, South Korea. 

South Korea have one of the high university enrollment rate in the world, but 
unemployment rate for graduate have be high, even a small- and medium-sized 
business cannot fill opening for manual and other jobs. The government study the 
German and Swiss apprenticeship system and transform a subset of exist vocational 
school into “Meister” ones. (Meister be German for a skilled craftsman.) Students graduate 
with the equivalent of two years’ work and/or community college experience. To provide 
incentives, the Korean government pay the students’ tuition, room, and board. 

The program be still relatively young, but already bearing fruit: Meister school have 
produce significantly high employment rate among their graduates, more than 
90 percent, compare with less than 65 percent for college graduates. College enrollment 
rate have fall in favor of vocational qualification a a result.1

Educators have a role to play in adjust school curriculum for the 
automation age 
While automation will be a major challenge to worker already in jobs, it also will have 
implication for how future generation of worker be trained, involve adjustment to 
school curriculum and education system more broadly. Curricula will need to adapt to 
provide student with the skill necessary for a dynamic, technology- and increasingly 
service-oriented labor market, particularly in country and industry where automation 
technology be likely to be adopt most quickly. Several change will be required. 

First, demand will increase for worker to develop and deploy technology, or interpret and 
act on the data analytics that these technology can produce, yet there may not be enough 
worker with the skill to meet this demand, for instance, for data scientists.121 STEM 
subject will be crucial for the workforce. Early education in subject such a statistics, to 
help student understand an increasingly data-driven world, where experiment be a key 
source of insight, will be vital. Some country include Estonia and the United Kingdom 
have introduce computer cod into primary and secondary education. Coding class 
in these country start a early a age five or seven, with an introduction to necessary 
fundamental concept (such a gain an understand of algorithms) and cod skill 
such a logic and the creation and debug of simple computer programs.122 However, 
a strong liberal art education to go alongside the high tech workplace skill could also be 
require for the “new collar” job of the future.123 

Our analysis have show that an increase percentage of activity that worker will do in 
the future will be in category such a manage and lead other people and interact 
with others, which require skill such a social and emotional sense and reasoning, and 
apply creativity and collaborative problem-solving. These skill be often not part of the 
formal curriculum in traditional school programs. Another find from our research be that 
automation and other factors, include globalization, independent work, and company 
cross sector boundaries, will require all worker to change what they do over time. This 
put a premium on a set of meta-skills, around agility, flexibility, grit, and learn how to 
learn. Teaching such quality be a challenge for all educational systems. 

Third, educational institution will need to adapt to the evolve demand of the labor market 
to ensure that critical job skill be be taught. Unless they become more responsive 
to labor market demands, educator risk create an ever-larger disconnect between 
education and employment. This disconnect be already visible in some surveys. In a 
McKinsey survey in 2012, only 50 percent of youth say they believe their post-secondary 
study improve their employment opportunities, and only 43 percent of employer 
report be able to find enough skilled workers. Moreover, 39 percent of employer say 
a skill shortage be a lead reason for entry-level vacancies. However, a big majority of 
education provider (72 percent) say they believe new graduate be ready to work.124

Several measure can reduce or correct disconnect among education providers, 
employers, and students. Providing actionable data be one. Students need to know that 
curriculum will provide them with relevant knowledge and work skills, and be able to select the 
best course and institution to achieve their desire educational outcome. In one survey, 
less than 50 percent of student say they have a solid understand of which discipline 
lead to profession with good job opening and wage levels.125 

Finally, the digital age itself have brought a multitude of possibility for new way of learning, 
both within the educational system and outside. Digital learn resource be more flexible 
in term of their timing and content than traditional classroom training, and program can 
adjust content for individual student to optimize their learn outcomes. For individuals, 
online degree can be more advantageous from a cost perspective than degree from 
traditional college and universities, particularly in the United States where tuition cost be 
rise faster than overall inflation. Some massive open online course (MOOCs) be free and 
have help expand access to educational content for those outside traditional educational 
institutions. At Coursera, for example, half of student be from develop countries, and 
about 60 to 70 percent of user be employ but prepare for good jobs, while 15 percent 
be unemployed. Most be between age 22 and 45.126 MOOCs present a promising 
channel for at-scale distribution of educational content at low cost and have potential to help 
ease future workforce transitions. However, their educational impact at scale remains to 
be seen.127 

IMPROVING LABOR MARKET DYNAMISM 
Workers in country with more fluid labor market find work more quickly and obtain job 
that be a good fit; this will blunt potential increase in unemployment a automation be 
adopted. For now, there be significant information asymmetry in the workforce, with poor 
job matching: company struggle to find the people they need, and people cannot find the 
opportunity for which they be best qualified. Both policy change and new digital tool 
can help address this challenge. 

Labor market fluidity have be decline within advanced economy 
In advanced economies, there be evidence that labor market be become less dynamic, 
with few people switch jobs.128 One strike example be the United States, which have 
experienced a decline in job reallocation rate since the early 1980s (Exhibit 31).129 The 
root cause of this decline be not fully understood, but include an age workforce that 
be less likely to change jobs, decline rate of new business formation, low geographic 
mobility (see next section), and increase regulations, licensing, and more intense 
training requirement that have make it harder to join some professions.130 Removing 
overly burdensome occupational licensing and restrictions, ensure that benefit be not 
lose in move from one employer to another, and ease the process and financing for 
entrepreneur to start new firm and for exist firm to innovate be all part of the solution.

Improving geographic mobility can enhance labor market fluidity 
While our analysis do not look at the intra-country geography of job loss and job growth, 
it be clear that increase the geographic mobility of the workforce will aid the transition. 
Yet labor mobility have decline steadily in many advanced economies.131 In the United 
States, geographic mobility have decline since the 1980s (both within and between states), 
reverse the increase mobility that be a characteristic of the US labor market early in the 
20th century.132 Whereas about 3 percent of worker relocate across state line each year 
prior to 1990, that figure have steadily fall to closer to 1.5 percent today, suggest that 
geographic labor mobility halve within less than 30 years. In Europe, despite the ambition 
of create free movement of people, mobility across border be still complicated, especially 
for services, in which regulate the cross-border post of worker remains subject to 
political disagreement and different labor market regulations. In the European Union, 
overall, about 17 million people, or 3 percent of the population, have take advantage of the 
free movement possibility to live in a different EU country, although the annual flow be one 
tenth of that, about 0.3 percent of the population.133 

Improving geographic mobility may require regulatory change a well a incentives. One 
major obstacle to internal mobility be the cost and availability of housing. Academic literature 
have show that residential mobility be positively correlate with worker reallocation rate and 
the efficiency of job matching.134 Lack of information on job opportunity in other areas, 
family ties, and different job licensing requirement be also deterrents, a be legal hurdle 
such a land-use laws, different eligibility standard for public benefits, state and local tax 
regimes, and even basic property law rules.135

International mobility be a controversial topic in some political arenas, but the skill 
mismatch we have identify a a consequence of automation and new labor demand 
will not stop at national borders. Our research on global migration have found that the 
world’s 247 million cross-border migrant contribute 9.4 percent of global GDP, or roughly 
$6.7 trillion worldwide in 2015—more than $3 trillion above their contribution have they 
remain in their home countries.136 More than 90 percent of these cross-border migrant 
move voluntarily, usually for economic reasons, while refugee and asylum seeker who 
tend to attract the most public attention make up the remainder. 

Digital platform can make labor market more transparent and improve 
job match 
Digital platform offer an efficient way to improve the information available to individual 
about job opportunities, and to company about job candidates. By improve information 
signals, job platform such a LinkedIn, Indeed.com, and Monster.com can speed and 
improve the process of match individual to jobs, thereby foster fluidity. These 
platform allow individual to post their entire resume and showcase their work, display a 
rich set of credential and skill other than simply their educational record. But transparency 
cut both ways: other platforms, such a Glassdoor.com, enable prospective employee to 
find out more about their potential employers, include salary information and anonymous 
review by current employees. MGI have estimate that up to 540 million individual could 
potentially benefit from online talent platforms, with a many a 230 million shorten 
search time between jobs, reduce the duration of unemployment. Up to 60 million people 
could find work that more closely suit their skill or preferences, and an additional 50 million 
could shift from informal to formal employment.137 

Companies can benefit from use digital technology to transform recruiting, training, 
and manage talent a well. Companies that adopt these tool be discover that better- 
inform decision about human capital produce good business results. On average, our 
analysis find that company could see a 275 basis point increase in their profit margins.138 
In addition, talent platform could improve signal about the skill that be actually in 
demand. As this information shape decision about education and training, the supply of 
skill in the economy could adjust more quickly and accurately over time. 

Creating more flexible work option may enhance reemployment of 
displace worker 
The workplace be changing, with the rise of more flexible form of independent work, 
include independent contractors, freelancers, self-employed individuals, and people 
work in the “gig” or “sharing” economy. MGI find that 20 to 30 percent of the work 
age population in Europe and the United States already earn income through independent 
work—and that 70 percent of those say they do so out of preference, not because they 
cannot find a traditional job. Moreover, the number of people choose to work outside 
traditional job may rise.139 Digital platform such a Upwork, Freelancer.com, HourlyNerd, 
Uber, Lyft, TaskRabbit, eBay, and Airbnb offer vast new market and low the barrier to 
entry, thereby remove some of the risk for those who want to be their own boss. 

Independent work may offer solution a well a new challenges. Among the benefits, it can 
enable many people currently not employ to work in flexible way that suit their needs. 
It be particularly attractive for care-givers, retirees, students, and others who need flexible 
schedules. For the unemployed, independent work may provide a critical bridge to keep 
earn income while seek employment. But a independent work grows, question

surround benefits, income security measure and other worker protection become

the definition of “worker,” extend minimum wage standards, and ensure that benefit such 
a holiday and sick pay cover independent workers.140 

PROVIDING TRANSITION AND INCOME SUPPORT TO WORKERS 
Job loss cause economic stress, a well a physical, emotional, and psychological 
distress. A wide body of academic research have found correlation between extend 
unemployment and decline in physical and mental health. Studies have even show poorer 
academic outcome among the child of the long-term unemployed.141 Other research 
have document the stagnation in market incomes, and increase wage polarization, 
in many developed markets.142 Both policy maker and business leader have a role to 
play in support worker a they transition between job to avoid long-term negative 
consequence and to ensure that they receive adequate incomes. 

Actively support worker in job transition 
A range of measure can speed the transition of worker between jobs, beyond improve 
labor market dynamism, job matching, and retrain and skill development. Most country 
have labor agency focus on provide assistance to the unemployed. In many, the focus 
be mainly on doling out benefit and reduce fraud. Germany provide an example of a 
nation that overhaul it labor force system—and reduce high unemployment a a result. 
In 2003, when the country be still struggle with the legacy of reunification, it adopt the 
“Hartz reforms,” base on recommendation of a labor market commission. The lower- 
wage segment of the labor market be liberalized, and a new category of job be create 
with employer pay a low flat rate for employees, who work a limited number of hour per 
week, exempt from social security and tax contributions. 

The move create million of “mini-jobs,” whose wage be then supplement by welfare 
payments. In addition, the local labor market agency be restructured. Case worker 
be assign to every unemployed individual, with strong incentive to successfully place 
their client into jobs. Skills assessment be performed, and training be provide if needed. 
These reform help reduce the unemployment rate from 10 percent in 2003 to below 
4 percent today and, at the same time, increase Germany’s share of the working-age 
population in employment by 10 percentage points. While these reform have return 
more individual to work, there be controversy over the impact on the post-unemployment 
earnings of workers.143 

To ensure that worker develop the skill need for the work world of the future, 
the German labor agency be now planning to put a great emphasis on counseling. It be 
currently pilot innovative online and offline counsel service direct at students, the 
unemployed, but also employee in job which be massively impact by digitization. The 
hope be that good individual orientation about job market trend and future opportunity 
will smooth the transition to a digital economy, without a large net loss of job and 
correspond increase in unemployment.

5. Managing the workforce transition McKinsey Global Institute118 

In Denmark, employer and government work with union to maintain the country’s 
“flexicurity system,” which combine active labor market policy with flexible rule for 
hire and fire and high level of benefit for unemployed individual (up to 90 percent for 
the low paid workers). They also offer active job counseling, include career guidance, 
training or education to all unemployed individuals, and offer all worker access to 
numerous vocational training programs. This creates a labor market environment of flexible 
employment and job security. Notably, firm and union get together to identify skill needs, 
agree on wage and enshrine right to paid leave for training. Some study suggest that 
similar collaborative effort between employer and union can play an important role in 
raise skill levels, include in the United States, and be already do so in some cases.144 

Neighboring Sweden have a system for retrain midcareer worker through private sector 
“job-security councils.” Employers pay into these councils, which provide financial support 
and job counsel to laid-off workers, with the aim of help them get back to work a 
soon a possible. Personal counselor help worker with their resume and steer them into 
class in their field or other fields.145 

Supporting worker income may also be necessary 
Our research indicates that million of individual will likely need to transition to new 
occupations—and identifies the risk that wage may become stagnant or even decline for 
middle and lower-skill occupation that have a large supply of potential worker but might 
have reduce demand, particularly in developed economies. Supporting income in both 
case may have an economic rationale. Consider that in 1914, Henry Ford announce that 
he would begin pay his employee $5 per day, more than twice the average wage for 
automakers, and reduce the work day from nine hour to eight, at a time when the 60-hour 
work week be the standard in American manufacturing. He explain that “unless an 
industry can so manage itself a to keep wage high and price low, it destroys itself, for 
otherwise it limit the number of it customers. One’s own employee ought to be one’s own 
best customers.”146 

Two form of income support need consideration in the age of automation. First be 
payment such a unemployment insurance to provide income to worker during training 
or transition between jobs. Yet unfortunately, the amount of public resource devote to 
support worker transitions—including on unemployment benefits—has be decline 
in most country (Exhibit 32). Given the large workforce transition we see in the decade 
ahead, re-evaluating this trend could be necessary.

In addition, however, wage may be under pressure in a wide range of job a economy 
transition to new form of work. A range of option exists for address these income 
effects. Some employer can choose to pay high wage and good benefits, recognize 
the value that their worker be producing. In addition to societal responsibility, these 
company may be motivate by competition for talent and an interest in reduce employee 
turnover. In the United States some company have unilaterally raise minimum wage they 
pay.147 In Europe, company have traditionally paid more attention to stakeholder such a 
workers, in part because of unionization and regulatory choices; in Germany, for example, 
worker representative sit on corporate board under 1976 “codetermination” legislation 
and play an important role in shape wage and benefit policies, a well a overall corporate 
strategy. There be also policy option for provide income support to worker to ensure 
that they remain active consumers. Minimum wage be widely deployed throughout the 
world, and there be move to raise them, include in US city such a Seattle.148 While 
some study have show that increase minimum wage can raise employment, the issue 
be actively debated. Other form of income support, such a “earned income tax credits” 
and wage subsidies, provide incentive for people to work. These measure attempt to 
provide income support without discourage people from working. Pilot project be 
underway in a number of country to test the idea of pay a universal basic income (see 
Box 9, “Experimenting with universal basic income”). 

In recent decades, the long-standing correlation between rise productivity and wage 
growth have broken down in some countries, such a the United States. The cause of this 
shift be unclear. But policy maker be consider new way to ensure that wage be 
link to rise productivity, so that prosperity be share with all. Some have suggest 
that minimum wage should be indexed to measure of productivity. In another specific 
example, Singapore have a program that support corporate investment in productivity 
on condition company share the gain from productivity improvement with low-wage 
workers.149 Between 2010, when the program be launched, and 2013, more than 800 
project be launched, most of them by small and medium-sized enterprises, which will 
benefit 53,000 worker once completed. Their wage increase be more than 10 percentage 
point above the national average. 

••• 

In the new era of automation, government and business will need to undertake a 
balance act between embrace the technology, which will boost productivity and 
economic growth, and at the same time address the complex transition it will create. 
Ensuring robust demand growth and economic dynamism be a priority: history show that 
economy that be not expand do not generate job growth. Upgrading workforce skill 
and create opportunity for midcareer job retrain will also be essential, at a time when 
spending on these have be decline in most countries, and labor market will need to 
become more dynamic and adaptable to change work need and pattern of worker 
redeployment. A final priority be reassess and strengthen transition and income 
support for worker caught in the cross-currents of automation. Each of these priority 
on it own present a challenge, and all together may require a Marshall Plan-like initiative, 
involve clear focus and investment. In the conclude chapter, we examine the implication 
for policy makers, business leaders, and individual workers.

a a policy to address the challenge of a jobless future in 
which automation have result in mass unemployment. 

Advocates say the assurance of a minimum standard of 
well-being encourages people to switch job and take 
more entrepreneurial risks, and support consumption 
for those in low-paying work. Others point to the high 
cost and difficulty in give cash transfers, particularly 
in develop countries. The potential cost be high, 
particularly in developed markets. An OECD study found 
that large tax revenue increase would be need in most 

country to finance a basic income at meaningful levels.1

payment would lead to increase in innovation and that 
UBI could undermine productivity by discourage work. 
Other argument question the assumption of planning for

mass redeployment of labor.

5. Managing the workforce transition McKinsey Global Institute122 

Apprentices at a car plant, Ulsan, South Korea 

© Yonhap News/YNA/Newscom 



Automation will be a powerful motor of future economic growth, but the challenge it 
present for workforce transition be sure to be very substantial. Policy makers, business 
leaders, and individual worker will need to be flexible, creative, and even visionary a they 
look to harness these rapidly-emerging technology and ensure that the time of automation 
be a productive and prosperous one. A range of outcome be possible, from one in which 
economic growth and productivity grow strongly, create myriad new jobs, a automation 
be adopt rapidly, to one marked by slow automation adoption, weak economic growth and 
low net job growth. 

Faced with the scale of worker transition we have described, one reaction could be to try 
to slow the pace and scope of adoption in an attempt to preserve a much of the status quo 
a possible. But this would be a mistake. Although slow adoption might limit the scale 
of workforce transitions, it would curtail the contribution that these technology make 
to business dynamism and economic growth. Automation technology and in particular 
artificial intelligence be the key to find solution for many important societal challenge in 
field range from climate science to health care. We should embrace these technologies, 
but also address the workforce transition and challenge they bring. To do this, there be 
a number of imperative and priority for governments, business, and individuals. In this 
conclude chapter, we highlight a number of them. 

GOVERNMENTS MUST MAKE WORKFORCE TRANSITIONS AND JOB 
CREATION A MORE URGENT PRIORITY 
Managing the come workforce transition with foresight be not just a question of smart 
policy. Automation’s power to lift the productivity of national economy have the potential to 
accelerate productivity and economic growth and improve lives. Governments can support 
the development and deployment of these technologies, for example through investment 
in basic and apply research, a well a through building out digital infrastructure. Ensuring 
positive employment outcome will require a laser focus on retool the workforce, 
step up support for worker in transition, and improve how local and national labor 
market function. Societies can choose to transform the come labor market disruption 
into an opportunity rather than a pitfall. 

As daunt a the task may seem, history show u that governments, across the globe, 
when face with monumental challenges, can rise to the occasion for the well-being of their 
citizens. As we have seen, the US High School Movement and GI Bill be instrumental in 
raise the education of the US workforce and country such a Germany have show that 
revamp labor market agency and support for worker in transition be not only possible 
but can also dramatically reduce unemployment. Such example highlight the importance 
of execute target policy decision swiftly and clearly. 

Yet in the last few decades, investment and policy to support the workforce have eroded, 
not be enhanced. Public spending on labor force training and support have fall in most 
countries. Educational model have not fundamentally change in 100 years; we still use 
system design for an industrial society to prepare student for a more dynamic, rapidly- 
change knowledge economy. Unions be on the decline. Government data collection 
on the grow independent workforce and new way of work be fragmented. It be now 
critical to reverse these trends. A new “Marshall Plan” for the workforce be needed. Priorities 
include the four area for action discuss at length in Chapter 5, but also the following: 

6. PRIORITIES FOR GOVERNMENT, 
BUSINESS, AND INDIVIDUALS 



6. Priorities for government, business, and individual McKinsey Global Institute124 

 Radically scale midcareer training opportunity to make lifelong learn a reality. 
Lifelong learn have long be talk about reverentially in policy circles, but the new 
age of automation will be the time when large-scale application of it will be need more 
than ever. Flexibility and adaptability will be the new workforce mantras, a machine 
both replace some human activity and—probably more frequently—fundamentally 
change them. Recent example of effective large-scale retrain of midcareer worker 
be few and far apart. For the future, more short-term and target training for people will 
be needed, especially for those in midcareer who will be look to develop new skill 
even if they keep their jobs. 

 Modernize educational system for the 21st century. Our analysis of the 
performance capability most in demand in the new age of automation show the 
critical importance of technology skills, but also of teamwork, creativity, communication, 
and social and emotional skills. Schools in many country continue to adhere to 
a culture of education that remains root in 19th century notion of teach and 
learning. Governments and educator can use digital technology to change that, for 
example create more individual learn path for students. Several country include 
Germany and Switzerland continue to show that apprenticeship can be a powerful and 
successful approach to teach technical skills. While university education have grown 
in popularity and lose it elitist reputation in many countries, many tertiary educational 
institution have not focus sufficiently on the need of the labor market or of the 
graduate enter it. Publishing job placement of graduate and similar data could 
help both employer look for recruit and potential student try to decide on their 
course of study. Singapore have show through it SkillsFuture Initiative that individual 
can be support and motivate to continuously acquire new skills. Finally, government 
could encourage, identify, and co-finance innovative pilot program that address know 
skill gap among workers, post-secondary students, and youth—and then scale the 
one that work. 

 Expand transition support measure for workers. Denmark, Germany, and Sweden, 
among others, have show the importance of focus labor agency on reemployment 
and the acquisition of new skills, rather than simply on hand out unemployment 
benefit or control for fraud. Best practice require a cultural shift of sorts, one that 
nudge worker to take a more active role in their own retrain and provide tool for 
them to be successful. Mobility can be an important part of that transition process, and 
in the United States, at least, it be long prized. However, mobility within border have 
slow in advanced country include the United States, and mobility across border 
face new hurdle globally a country revisit immigration policy and practices. 
Reducing the barrier to mobility—which include legal one alongside prohibitive 
housing costs—will need to be a policy priority. And government should not lose sight 
of the cardinal importance of increase economic mobility and opportunity for all 
citizens. That mean universal access to quality education, good neighborhoods, and 
basic healthcare. 

 Create income support measure consistent with the new wage realities. 
Since 1970, market wage and productivity growth have diverge in some advanced 
economy include the United States, and income inequality have grown. With the 
advent of the new automation age, it be important to begin national discussion on 
whether we can assume that everyone who work can support a decent standard 
of living. A healthy consumer class be essential for both economic growth and social 
stability. Income supplementation program already exist in certain countries, such 
a the earn income tax credit in the United States, and some country be test 
universal basic income program or raise minimum wages; more could follow suit, to 
provide fact-based finding that can inform the debate.

 Make job creation and worker re-deployment a national priority. A broad range 
of incentive exists for business to invest in capital and research and development. 
Something similar be need to encourage investment in human capital. In addition, 
government could ass the impact on job creation of their policy and investments, 
much a they currently ass the impact of policy on the environment. Companies 
could be encourage to invest in worker training and redeployment through tax and 
other incentives, just a they often be for their research and development investments; 
in some countries, that will mean reconsider tax code that provide subsidy 
(through interest) for investment in capital while tax labor. The step-up scenario 
in this report show the value in job-creation term of raise public—and, with it, 
private—investment in infrastructure, affordable housing, and energy efficiency and 
climate change. Such investment will need to go hand-in-hand with other backing for 
job creation, include support entrepreneurship and small business creation by 
streamline regulation and revisit personal bankruptcy law that discourage risk- 
taking, for example. 

 Modernize data collection on the labor market. Government survey of household 
and employer be the gold standard of national economic data. But these be time- 
consuming. In today’s dynamically change world, government need to supplement 
these survey with real-time data on the adoption of automation technologies, 
job openings, labor market dynamism, skill in demand, and how individual be 
cop with job transitions.150 The need for good data amount to an opportunity for 
government statistic agency to collaborate with online source of data, include 
job boards, professional site such a LinkedIn, and private tech companies, to obtain 
a more detailed and accurate picture of jobs, skills, wages, and individual mobility and 
career moves. 

BUSINESS LEADERS SHOULD EMBRACE AUTOMATION AND AI WHILE 
CAREFULLY MANAGING WORKFORCE TRANSITIONS 
Business leader also have much to gain by early adoption of automation technologies, 
enable performance benefit such a quality and speed, a well a great efficiency and 
productive use of all factor of production. But business will also be on the front line of 
the workplace a it changes. Successful adoption of automation will require company 
to re-imagine their entire business process to take advantage of automation’s benefits, 
rather than mechanically attempt to automate individual activity use current 
processes. As part of that review, they will need to reevaluate their talent strategy and 
workforce needs, consider how worker can be redeployed to other jobs, and where 
new talent may be required. Many company be find that it be in their self-interest— 
a well a important for societal responsibility—to train and prepare worker for a new 
world of work. Some company be already work with external education provider or 
conduct in-house training—but many more could follow suit. 

 Accelerate deployment of automation and AI. For CEOs in all industry and 
countries, develop an automation and AI strategy should be a priority. So far, few firm 
have deployed at scale. In an MGI survey of 3,000 AI-aware C-level executives, across 
10 country and 14 sectors, only 20 percent say they use any AI related technology 
in a core part of their businesses. Many firm say they be uncertain of the business 
case or return on investment. A review of more than 160 use case show that AI 
be deployed commercially in only 12 percent of cases.151 But company that ignore 
these technology do so at their peril: the gap in performance between early adopter

of digital technology in general and AI in particular be widening. In the MGI survey, 
early AI adopter have high profit margins. Our case study in retail, electric utilities, 
manufacturing, health care, and education highlight automation and AI’s potential to 
improve forecasting and sourcing, optimize and automate operations, develop target 
marketing and pricing, and enhance the customer experience. 

 Redesign business process to unlock productivity gains. Since the IT 
revolution begin in the 1990s it have be clear that capture the value from new 
technology require reimagining how the business operates, rather than mechanically 
apply automation to the current mix of activity and processes. Capturing the full 
opportunity offer by automation will require company to conduct a thorough review 
of business process and workflow and ass where automation could improve 
performance the most. That in turn require company to develop or acquire the talent, 
discipline, and know-how to implement the sort of change that will be need to 
harness the full potential of automation. 

 Rethink organizational design. Automation adoption be a process that will not happen 
overnight, and the workplace norm for year to come will be people work alongside 
machines. This have profound implication for the way company and their workforce be 
structure and organized. Until recently, for example, powerful manufacturing robot that 
can lift or weld have be kept well away from humans, often in cages, because of the 
risk of accidents. But today’s robot can work intelligently and safely alongside humans. 
Such machine-human and machine-machine environment will become more pervasive, 
and that in turn will require workflow to change. Successfully rethink organizational 
design will ensure that work be not only more productive and take advantage of the new 
technical possibility available, but that it will become more meaningful and reward 
for people, a the rote aspect of their job be take over by machines, free them 
to use more innate human quality include social and emotional reason and 
personal interaction. 

 Build core digital and analytics capabilities. Companies that successfully adopt the 
late automation and AI technology typically already have strong digital capabilities. 
Indeed, our analysis show that company that be early adopter of AI be also 
digital leaders. There be no shortcut to create a strong digital base. Companies will 
need to build the support digital assets, big data and analytics capability to make 
automation and AI a success. This include building the data ecosystem and adopt 
the right advanced analytic technique and tools.152 

 Adapt talent strategy and manage workforce transitions. Business leader will 
need to ensure that the talent their company require to transition to more automate 
operation be in place. This will involve a combination of recruiting automation-savvy 
professionals, a well a retrain worker to play new roles. Determining the right mix 
of current talent, redeployed talent, and new talent from outside the company will require 
careful consideration. In the new era, STEM talent and data scientist will be increasingly 
important—and could provide a last competitive advantage. But fill new technical 
position be expensive and time-consuming because we have not be turn out 
enough skilled professional to keep up with the demand. In the United States, for 
instance, data scientist shortage be already appearing.153
