 Consider partnership for talent development. Some company be turn to 
partnership to develop the skill need in their workforce and help smooth transitions. 
As we note in Chapter 5, some company have begin to establish partnership with 
university and other educational institution to provide training and skill development 
in their workforces. This enables large-scale retraining, without create the staff and 
overhead to manage it internally. Such partnership may become more common a 
company adopt automation at scale. In the future, a technical talent shortage 
increase, corporate partnership with university and college may become more 
frequent a company seek to develop a reliable pipeline of scarce technical talent. 
In other sectors, such a health care and manufacturing, there be many example of 
company partner with local community college to shape and create the curriculum 
for specific degree programs. Similar collaboration might increase the availability of 
data scientist and other technology-related professionals. 

INDIVIDUALS MUST PREPARE FOR LIFELONG LEARNING AND 
EVOLVING CAREERS 
Individuals will need to be prepared for a rapidly evolve future of work. Acquiring new skill 
that be in demand and reset intuition about the world of work will be critical for well- 
being. Workers everywhere will need to reexamine traditional notion of where they work, 
how they work, and what talent and capability they bring to that work. 

 Embrace a “startup of you” mentality. In the rapidly change future of work, 
individual will be in charge of their own destiny more than ever. The day of planning 
to have one employer for life be long gone. All individual will need to adopt a more 
entrepreneurial approach to navigate through the world of work and manage their 
careers. Reid Hoffman, a co-founder of LinkedIn, call it the “startup of you” approach.154 
Individuals will become more proficient and comfortable with navigate a more digital 
job search and manage their personal profiles. 

 Acquire the skill that will be in demand and embark on a journey of lifelong 
learning. As machine perform a wider range and variety of tasks, individual will need 
to put more focus on develop the skill that human excel at. As we have described 
in this report, the activity in nearly all occupation will change, with more time spent 
on those activity that require social and emotional skills, team work and collaboration, 
creativity, and high level of communication and logical reasoning. Both government 
and business have a role to play in provide individual with good information on the 
skill and job in demand. Educators play a part a well. Secondary school student in 
most country receive inadequate instruction and guidance on how to plan a career in 
today’s workplace, and even less so for a workplace that be rapidly evolving. Ultimately it 
will be up to individual themselves to think carefully about what skill will be need and 
how they can demonstrate those skill to employers. 

 Prepare for a world of digital job search. Digital platform for match people with 
job and assess skill be rapidly become the norm for hiring.155 Individuals will 
need to use these technology if they be to be competitive in the job market. In the 
short term, this mean put time and care into building a personal online presence. 
To stand out, they will need to showcase their experience, establish expertise by join 
group or post content, and build their professional networks. Workers could also 
benefit from understand and participate in the innovation around skills-based 
training and credential that could accelerate their career trajectories. Individuals without 
formal education credential may be able to differentiate themselves through their online 
reputation via recommendation from former customer or employers. 

154 Reid Hoffman and Ben Casnocha, The start-up of you, Random House, 2013. 
155 Ibid. A labor market that works, McKinsey Global Institute, June 2015.

 Consider new way of working. Not only do most people now cycle through multiple 
employer throughout their careers, but many be move beyond the traditional full- 
time (or part-time) job altogether. As many a 30 percent of worker in the United States 
and Europe earn part or most of their income through independent work—that is, 
freelance activities, self-employment, or through rapidly expand digital gig or share 
platforms.156 More than 70 percent of those individual say that they prefer independent 
work and they report high satisfaction with many aspect of their work-life than people 
with traditional jobs, include not only flexibility, but also opportunity for advancement, 
creativity and variety in their work, and even more security in their income. Roughly half 
of independent worker supplement their income from traditional job (or pensions) with 
these activities. In a world where wage be depressed for people without the skill in 
demand, independent work offer an opportunity to enhance income and branch out 
into new areas. 

Ultimately, automation may force u all to reassess basic notion of work. In capitalist 
economies, individual earn most of their income through apply their labor; except for 
the disabled, all of u be born with an endowment of labor from which to earn income, but 
only a privileged few be born with capital. In many decade hence, the value of this labor 
may be diminish if we reach a state in which machine can do a large share of the work. 
For worker around the world, policy makers, and business leaders—and not just social 
scientist who specialize in socio-economic paradigms—that should give pause for thought, 
and be a spur for action. 

••• 

Automation represent both hope and challenge. The global economy need the boost to 
productivity and growth that it will bring, especially at a time when age population be 
act a a drag on GDP growth. For companies, the technology can lift productivity and 
profit to new heights. For society a a whole, machine can take on work that be routine, 
dangerous, or dirty, and may allow u all to use our intrinsically human talent more fully and 
enjoy more leisure. Yet even a we benefit, our society will need to prepare for complex 
transition ahead, a machine replace worker in many areas. Our research suggests that 
it may be time to refocus the current anxious debate about automation toward issue of 
demand growth, and how to manage the inevitable transition create by automation. The 
task at hand be to prepare for a more automate future by emphasize the skill that will be 
need and ensure dynamic job creation. The technology be advance rapidly; the policy 
choice should not tarry.

1. WORK HOURS THAT COULD BE AUTOMATED 
This report continue and adapts the methodology and finding of the January 2017 
McKinsey Global Institute report, A future that works: Automation, employment and 
productivity. A full methodology of that work be detailed in it technical appendix; we will 
provide only a brief summary here and how it be apply in this report. 

In that report, the technical potential for automation of the global economy and project 
adoption rate be determine by an analysis of the underlie work activity for each 
occupation, cover 46 countries. It us database publish by institution include 
the World Bank and the US Bureau of Labor Statistics 2014 O*Net database to break down 
about 800 occupation into more than 2,000 activities, and determines the performance 
capability need for each activity base on the way human currently perform them. 
The report further break down activity into 18 capability and ass their technical 
automation potential. This framework be inform by academic research, internal expertise, 
and industry experts. Our report focus on 2016–30, and thus take the automation 
adoption percentage through 2030. Much of the occupational data, at the time of original 
analysis, be harmonize through 2014. We adopt the simplification of refer to 2016 
a the start point of the analysis, and project in 2014 data (such a occupational mix) 
into 2016 baseline (for example, automation adoption percentages). 

In this report, we use these finding to size the number of job that could be automate 
by 2030. We make an assumption that each hour of work that could be automate will 
result in proportional job loss, for example if 10 percent of current work activity hour in an 
occupation will be automated, then 10 percent of job in that occupation will be displaced. A 
priori, it be unclear if this assumption be conservative or aggressive. Based on what we have 
see historically, we expect in many case that the result of activity be automate will 
be a redistribution of effort on other exist or new activities. However, it be also possible 
that with automation, exist work process could be radically overhaul and reduce 
in complexity, reduce labor demand even further beyond automation potential of current 
activities. We have not model these countervail effects.

TECHNICAL APPENDIX 



Technical appendix McKinsey Global Institute132 

To calculate the work hour automate in 2030, we multiply the automation adoption 
percentage by the size of the labor force in 2030. By do this, we assume that the 
occupation mix of the economy and the underlie work activity in each occupation in 
2030 be the same a today. This be a conservative assumption, because in reality, we would 
expect that job will not be add back at the same occupation mix and that new job will 
be add in less automatable sectors. 

To estimate the size of the 2030 labor force, we use population projection from the United 
Nations, labor force participation projection from the International Labour Organization, 
and the natural unemployment rate for OECD countries. For country outside the OECD, we 
use the maximum unemployment rate of either 2007 or 2012 to adjust for the effect of the 
2008 global financial crisis on unemployment. 

2. LABOR DEMAND DRIVERS 
Our work examines the labor demand create by seven catalysts. We select these seven 
from a shortlist of 20 after conduct high-level size calculation to estimate their potential 
to create labor demand by 2030. 

For catalyst that include a per caput metric, such a spend on automobile or number of 
health-care professionals, we include population growth through 2030 base on projection 
from the United Nations. 

We capture direct and indirect job that could be create from each catalyst, take into 
account the decline in hour work per person, and factor in globalization of work. Our 
model offer a static view of the potential labor demand that could be create from the 
seven driver and do not factor in supply-demand dynamic and feedback from factor 
such a change in wage levels. It estimate potential labor demand; whether this potential 
be capture will depend on the choice and investment make by businesses, policy- 
makers, and workers. The scenario we construct do not take into account any source of 
labor demand outside of our seven driver that could play an important role in determine 
the future of work. We do not model entirely new industry and occupation that could 
exist in the future, in part enable by technology; study have show that on average, 
0.5 percent of the workforce have be work in “new jobs” per year in the past couple 
of decades.157 We do not take into account sectoral shift in industry that be not directly 
related to automation, such a the rise of e-commerce in retail. We also do not model 
change in work structure, such a the growth of the “gig” economy, or activity within an 
occupation that could change a a result of technological innovation. 

157 Ibid. Jeffrey Lin, “Technological adaptation,” May 2011.

Gross Domestic Product projection 
Increased prosperity be the underlie driver of many (but not all) of the labor demand 
sizings. Given the static modular model approach that we have taken, we have take 
GDP per caput growth a an input to our driver models. 

We use the McKinsey Global Growth Model (GGM) projections. The GGM be a global 
macroeconomic model that track long term economic trend and generates projection 
under a range of scenarios. For the input to our labor demand modeling, we use the GGM’s 
baseline scenario where available. For country that the GGM do not model and for 
Japan and Mexico, we use projection from Oxford Economics. See Exhibit A1 for the GDP/ 
caput projection in use. 

Job multiplier 
For driver of labor demand in which we be model an increase in spend, we use job 
multiplier from input-output table to calculate the number of job create through each 
additional dollar of spend. In many driver base on linear regression analysis (for example, 
rise consumer spending), the general size approach for the number of job create 
incremental to 2014 level be capture in the follow formula: 

To take into account project increase in productivity between 2014 and 2030, we adjust 
2014 job multiplier for project productivity gain (from factor other than automation) to 
create a 2030 job multiplier. 

For all labor drivers, we calculate indirect job use indirect job multiplier from McKinsey 
input-output table base on source data from the World Input-Output Database, make 
adjustment a necessary inform by expert input. 

To avoid double-counting, we remove particular indirect multiplier if they may overlap 
with our drivers. For example, we exclude all indirect effect in healthcare, education and 
construction, since we have size these driver independently. This may undercount job 
creation in these areas.

Technical appendix McKinsey Global Institute134 

Globalization of trade 
For driver that include tradable good and services, we use data from the International 
Trade Organization and IHS Global Insight to model level of import and export in our 46 
country set. In driver with global trade, we model both locally- and globally-driven labor 
demand. We keep this model of global trade constant between today and 2030, a shift 
in globalization be beyond the scope of our analysis. This approach would result in an 
underestimation of job creation in country whose global export share in 2030 would be 
great than today’s shares, and similarly overestimation of job creation in country whose 
share of global export in 2030 would be low than today’s. 

Trendline and step-up scenario 
For three of our seven drivers—infrastructure, residential and commercial buildings, energy 
transition and efficiency—we model two scenarios. These be a trendline scenario, base 
on the observe pattern across country that vary by factor such a GDP per capita, 
and a step-up scenario, which be base on further change that could boost labor demand 
above the trendline scenario. For our seventh driver, the marketization of previously unpaid 
work, we have only model a step-up scenario. We describe the assumption for these 
scenario in the relevant section below. 

Catalyst 1: Rising income 
Our rise income driver represent increase in consumer spending a well a overall 
spending on health care and education that result from increase prosperity (that is, rise 
GDP per capita) in countries. We have take GDP per caput projection a an exogenous 
input to our model for all driver related to change in spend. 

For consumer spending, we use univariate regression analysis to identify spending trend 
by category use 2014 GDP per caput and 2014 consumption per caput data for the 
46 country in our model. While GDP per caput change from 2014 to 2030 a the 
independent variable, we model change in spend by category for accommodation and food 
services, automobiles, clothing, financial services, food, household goods, leisure goods, 
leisure services, and utilities. (We exclude some category of consumer spending to avoid 
double counting, such a public transport, which could overlap with our infrastructure 
driver). An adjustment be make across category to cap overall consumption, to ensure 
that our regression analysis do not imply a major shift in consumption per capita. To do 
this, we scale overall consumption to a low and high scenario, base on consumption per 
caput projection from the GGM. We then multiply the 2014 and 2030 spend by 2014 and 
2030 job multipliers, respectively. The productivity-adjusted 2030 job multiplier account 
for an increase in productivity, which drive some consumption category to have negative 
job growth in country where productivity growth outstrips demand growth, such a the 
agriculture sector in India. Additionally, we use indirect job multiplier to capture the demand 
create in other sector that supply to these sectors. 

Goods and service model under rise income be determine to be tradable or non- 
tradable, and the labor demand for those which be tradable be distribute accord to 2014 
level of global trade. 

Given the discrepancy between country in funding model for education and health care, 
these driver have be size separately from the rest of consumer spending, despite some 
proportion of education and health care spending be fund directly by consumers. For 
both these sectors, we model the full sector, which would include that fund by consumer 
a well a public and private sector funding.

We model this relationship through univariate regression on student-teacher ratio and 
gross enrollment rate across primary, secondary, and tertiary level use 2014 data for 
all 46 country in our model. We also capture the effect of age populations; if there be low 
population growth and the population be aging, this decrease education jobs, and vice 
versa. The general formula we use is: 

We use this to model project education job in 2030, then subtract the number of job in 
2014 to size the incremental labor demand in education. We then use indirect job multiplier 
to capture job create in other sector that be supplier to the education sector. 

Catalyst 2: Health care: Rising income and age 
In addition to rise income increase demand for job in health care, age population 
in many country will likewise raise health-care demand. We model these effect of rise 
income and age together to avoid double-counting the increase in healthcare jobs. 

The change in the number of health-care job be model through bivariate linear regression 
with 2014 GDP per caput and age (share of population over 65 in 2014) a independent 
variable and health-care professional per 1000 people in 2014 a the dependent variable 
for all 46 country in our model. We use this trendline to model the increase in health-care 
professional a GDP per caput increase and population age from 2014 to 2030 levels. 
We include all part of the health-care delivery sector include hospital care, home care, 
nursing homes, and other support roles. We then use indirect job multiplier to capture job 
create in other sector that be supplier to the health-care sector. 

Catalyst 3: Development and deployment of new technology 
We identify trend between rise GDP per caput and spend on information technology. For 
enterprise IT spend, we find that a country’s GDP be correlate with the amount spent on 
hardware, software, and IT services. For consumer technology spend, we consider only the 
hardware and software component of spend, and find that the richer the population (i.e. 
the high GDP per capita) the high spend on technology goods. We use univariate linear 
regression analysis to find a relationship between 2014 GDP per caput a the independent 
variable and each category of IT spend per caput in 2014 (including consumer and 
enterprise spending) a the dependent variable across all 46 countries. These category 
of IT spend be then multiply by productivity-adjusted job multiplier for 2014 and 2030 
to calculate net new jobs. All data be base on historical baseline from Gartner’s Market 
Databook publish in the first quarter of 2017.158 Finally, we use indirect multiplier to 
capture job create in sector supply to the IT sector.

As the consumer technology element of rise income be capture within this driver, we

infrastructure spend a part of our technology definition in order to avoid double-counting.

Catalyst 4: Infrastructure investment 
In our trendline scenario for infrastructure investment, we conduct univariate regression 
analysis with GDP per caput a the independent variable and infrastructure spend per 
caput a the dependent variable on 2014 cross-country data. From this, we estimate 
infrastructure spend in 2014 and in 2030, use GDP per caput projections. These 2014 
and 2030 infrastructure spend number be then multiply by 2014 and 2030 job multiplier 
for the construction sector, respectively, to estimate job in each year. 2030 job multipliers, 
a with other catalysts, be calculate use productivity growth a discuss below. The 
difference be the incremental addition of new infrastructure job between 2014 and 2030. 

In our step-up scenario, we model a step-up in which country have increase their 
infrastructure stock to a global benchmark of 70.5 percent of total GDP. This result in 
high run-rate infrastructure spending in order to attain and maintain the infrastructure 
70.5 percent benchmark, accounting for both GDP growth (accelerated in our step-up 
scenario) and depreciation. In all, this amount to between $4 trillion and $4.5 trillion 
annual spending for economic infrastructure (transport, water, and power) compare 
with $2.1 trillion to $3 trillion in the trendline scenario. We assume annual non-automation 
productivity increase range from 1.5 percent to 4.8 percent in some emerge market 
country base on historical trend (1.5 percent in China, 4.8 percent in Kenya, Nigeria, 
and the Philippines, and no productivity increase elsewhere). By the same method a the 
trendline scenario, we multiply 2014 and 2030 spend by job multiplier for the construction 
sector to estimate gross new jobs. Included in the range of our step-up scenario estimate 
be additional annual non-automation productivity increase in the remain emerge 
market country of 2.5 percent. This productivity adjustment would good indicate the 
range of possible outcome in emerge market countries; in advanced economies, give 
low historical growth in productivity, we do not assume non-automation productivity will 
increase even in the low range of the step-up scenario. Finally, we use indirect job multiplier 
to capture job create in sector that supply the construction sector. 

Catalyst 5: Residential and commercial building 
As with infrastructure, we have model two scenario for residential and commercial 
buildings. For the trendline scenario, we again conduct univariate regression analyses, 
just a we do for infrastructure. For the step-up scenario, we model an increase of stock 
in structure to a US benchmark of 2.3 time that of total GDP, in line with data from the 
Bureau of Economic Analysis, and adjust downward for double-counting with infrastructure, 
inclusion of industrial structures, and spending on equipment outside of the construction 
sector. This amount to $8.2 trillion to $9.8 trillion annual spending for building and 
structure (including residential, commercial and industrial structures) compare with 
$3.8 trillion to $5.5 trillion in the trendline scenario. As with the infrastructure catalyst, we also 
apply 1.5 to 4.8 percent productivity growth assumption in select country in the trendline 
scenario, and 2.5 percent annual non-automation productivity increase to remain 
emerge market country in the low range of the step-up scenario.

Catalyst 6: Energy transition and efficiency 
The energy transition driver capture the potential job creation due to the shift in mix of 
electricity generation. The potential increase in job in electric power generation due to 
increase in demand for power be capture in the utility category of consumer spending 
driven by rise incomes. We avoid double counting by isolate the mix shift effect in 
this driver. Using McKinsey model scenario for gigawatt (GW) capacity in 2030, 
we multiply project GW capacity by a job per GW multiplier across manufacturing, 
decommissioning, fuels, construction/installation, operation and maintenance by energy 
type (such a solar, coal, gas). Given the rapid and hard-to-predict change in productivity 
across the renewables value chain, we model a minimum scenario in which rapid 
productivity growth continues, and a maximum scenario in which productivity gain plateau. 
To model a step-up scenario, we increase the GW capacity shift more heavily towards 
renewables target that could help slow global temperature increase to two degree 
Celsius above pre-industrial levels. This increase shift result in great number of job 
create to change the energy generation mix by country. 

We model potential spend on energy efficiency use estimate from the International 
Energy Agency’s 2014 World energy investment outlook report. We use two scenario 
that the IEA models: a “New Policies Scenario” and a “450 Scenario.” In the New Policies 
Scenario, which be grouped with our other trendline drivers, “energy demand and supply 
projection reflect energy policy and measure that have be adopt a of early 2014, 
a well a other commitment that have be announced, but not implemented, take a 
cautious view of the extent to which these may be realized.”159 For our step-up analysis, we 
use the IEA’s 450 Scenario which “plots an emissions-reduction path for the energy sector 
consistent with the international goal to limit the rise to long-term average temperature to 
two degree Celsius.”160 In both scenarios, we use job multiplier to estimate the number of 
incremental job associate with the increase in spend that the IEA project between 2014 
and 2030, and use indirect multiplier to capture job create in other supply sectors. 

Catalyst 7: Marketization of currently unpaid work 
We model the marketization of currently unpaid work solely a a step-up labor demand 
driver. We use local time-use survey to understand the amount of time spent in various 
country on unpaid domestic work include cooking, cleaning, childcare and elder care. 
We decrease the time spent on these activity in each country use a linear coefficient 
between the minimum and maximum value across countries. We then make assumption 
around productivity gain in each activity through professionalization to estimate the 
potential for new labor demand creation. 

3. MACROECONOMIC ANALYSIS 
We use McKinsey’s Global Growth Model to dynamically model the US economy. Given 
both the unemployment and productivity effect of automation, we use the model to 
determine the GDP growth need to return the economy to full employment by 2030. 

Of the automation effect modeled, we directly include four channel by which automation 
affect the economy: unemployment displacement, capital investment, total factor 
productivity growth, and reemployment rate. Three of the four channel be output from 
the MGI automation model. We take the unemployment displacement a the midpoint 
displacement directly from the automation model; capital investment a the solution cost 
give in the automation model; and total factor productivity a the imply productivity 
growth from automation that would at the minimum result in constant total output. 

159 World energy investment outlook, International Energy Agency, 2014. 
160 Ibid. 



Technical appendix McKinsey Global Institute138 

Besides the output from the automation model, we also include a fourth channel, 
the reemployment rate, which describes how displacement actually translates into 
unemployment. Not every displace worker will enter unemployment; some will have the 
skill and the opportunity to quickly transition into a new role. We define the reemployment 
rate a the percentage of displace worker expect to return to work within the year; the 
remain displace worker enter unemployment. 

On top of the four channels, we model four different labor market scenarios. Each 
of the four scenario use a different reemployment rate—low, medium, high, or full 
reemployment—to describe condition such a labor market flexibility, labor market slack, 
skill and geographic mismatch, etc. that could influence the rate at which displace 
worker return to work. For the United States, we use a 25 percent reemployment rate in 
the low scenario, 49 percent reemployment rate in the medium scenario (the reemployment 
rate of displace worker in January 2010), 66 percent in the high scenario (the 
reemployment rate of displace worker in January 2016), and 100 percent reemployment 
in the full reemployment scenario. The model unemployment displacement and capital 
investment by country be held constant in all scenarios. For example, in the United 
States, the model unemployment displacement be 23 percent by 2030, and expect 
automation capital investment $145 billion by 2030. The reemployment rate for each of the 
labor market scenario differ by country and be estimate from literature and adjust 
for labor market flexibility factors. 

The GGM first forecast a baseline future without take into account the effect of 
automation. It then us the inputted unemployment displacement, capital investment, 
total factor productivity growth, and reemployment rate, and dynamically propagates these 
effect of automation throughout the model economy. The forecast unemployment 
rate, GDP growth rate, and average wage rate from the GGM be to be interpret by their 
deviation from the baseline state a a directional range of scenario for several possible 
future states. 

4. SKILLS AND WAGES ANALYSES 
In this analysis, we look at the net impact of (i) automation and (ii) labor demand creation at 
an occupation level, to understand which occupation could see high level of demand vs. 
decline by 2030. 

Baseline for skill and wage analysis 
The baseline for our skill and wage analysis be the 2014 employment level by occupation, 
adjust for the effect of automation and our labor demand drivers. Elements of the 
economy in which we have not size growth (such a defense) remain at 2014 level of 
employment; new occupation that do not currently exist do not show in this baseline. 

Mapping new labor demand and automation to job 
Our automation model be mapped to each of the 820 occupation code use by the 
US Bureau of Labor Statistics for all of the 46 country in our model. We multiply the 
percentage automation adoption rate in 2030 for each occupation by the size of each 
occupation in 2030 (which be assume to be the same percentage of labor force a in 2014). 
This provide u with a projection of how the employment level in job will be affected by 
automation in 2030. 



139Jobs lost, job gained: Workforce transition in a time of automationMcKinsey Global Institute 

To model the countervail effect on labor demand creation, we map our driver of demand 
accord to a list of associate occupation on a driver-by-driver basis. Most driver map 
to a specific sector, in which case we allocate incremental new job accord to today’s 
mix of job within a specify sector. In driver that affect a narrower range of jobs, we 
create more customize mapping to reflect reality. For example, for energy transition and 
efficiency, we map potential labor demand increase to the US Bureau of Labor Statistics’ 
categorization of “green jobs” and to the relevant job in fuels. Based on the sector of each 
direct job added, additional indirect job be create base on the specify job multiplier 
for a country. Indirect job be create accord to the mix of their sector. 

Combining these two calculations, we compare the total job displace by automation 
base on our 2030 baseline (today’s occupation mix, scale in proportion with the growth 
of labor force) to the job add by our driver to 2030. To calculate a percentage growth or 
decline, we divide this net change by today’s employment level for the occupation. 

Skills and educational categorization 
Our analysis take training requirement for job a a proxy for skill requirements. The five 
educational category use in our analysis are: less than secondary, secondary, associate 
degree, bachelor’s degree and advanced degree. These classification map to the five 
job-zones define by the US Bureau of Labor Statistics’ O*Net and take into account 
educational degrees, amount of training required, and year of experience necessary in 
order to qualify for a give job. Associate degree be more common in the United States 
than in the rest of the world and usually require two year of study. 

To optimize for consistency across our model and data quality, we use these educational 
requirement from the O*Net classification across all 46 countries. This simplify 
assumption be limit in case in which there be discrepancy among countries, but we 
find that requirement be broadly similar: the educational requirement to be a doctor be 
typically an advanced degree in most countries. 

Wage assumption 
In our analysis on the effect of automation and new labor demand on wages, we keep 
wage constant at 2014 levels; chemical engineers, economist and lawyer be in the 
high decile of wage earner in 2014 in the United States, where they remain for our 
analysis in 2030. 

In reality, we would expect wage to adjust to shift in response to automation and new 
labor demand. There could also be additional economic effect on wages, such a 
Baumol’s cost disease, or policy effect such a minimum wage legislation. Modeling 
these effect on wage be beyond the scope of our analysis, so we exclude them in favor of 
keep wage constant at 2014 levels. While we thus cannot say anything definitive about 
wage shift and be limited in any commentary on inequality, our approach allows u to 
identify relative shift between high-wage and low-wage occupations.

5. GLOSSARY OF AUTOMATION TECHNOLOGIES AND TECHNIQUES 

Exhibit A2 

Glossary of automation technology and technique 

Technologies 
and technique Description/examples 

Artificial 
intelligence 

Field of computer science specialize in develop system that exhibit “intelligence.” Often 
abbreviate a AI, the term be coin by John McCarthy at the Dartmouth Conference in 1956, the 
first conference devote to this topic 

Machine 
learn 

Subfield of artificial intelligence develop system that “learn,” i.e., practitioner 
“train” these system rather than “programming” them 

Supervised 
learn 

Machine learn technique that train a system to respond appropriately to stimulus by 
provide a training set of sample input and desire output pairs. Supervised learn 
have be use for email spam detection by training system on a large number of 
emails, each of which have be manually label a either be spam or not 

Transfer 
learn 

Subfield of machine learn develop system that store knowledge gain while 
solve one problem and apply it to a different but related problem. Often use 
when the training set for one problem be small, but the training data for a related 
problem be plentiful, e.g., repurposing a deep learn system train on a large non- 
medical image data set to recognize tumor in radiology scan 

Reinforce- 
ment 
learn 

Subfield of machine learn develop system that be train by receive virtual 
“rewards” or “punishments” for behavior rather than supervise learn on correct 
input-output pairs. In February 2015, DeepMind described a reinforcement learn 
system that learn how to play a variety of Atari computer games. In March 2016, 
DeepMind’s AlphaGo system defeat the world champion in the game of Go 

Cognitive 
compute 

Synonym for artificial intelligence 

Neural network Artificial 
neural 
network 

AI system base on simulate connect “neural units,” loosely model the way 
that neuron interact in the brain. Computational model inspire by neural 
connection have be study since the 1940s 

Deep 
learn 

Use of neural network that have many layer (“deep”) of a large number (millions) of 
artificial neurons. Prior to deep learning, artificial neural network often only have three 
layer and dozen of neurons; deep learn network often have seven to ten or more 
layers. The term be first use in 2000 

Convolution- 
al neural 
network 

Artificial neural network in which the connection between neural layer be inspire 
by the organization of the animal visual cortex, the portion of the brain that process 
images, well suit for perceptual tasks. In 2012, the only entry use a convolutional 
neural network achieve an 84% correct score in the ImageNet visual recognition 
contest, vs. a win score of 75% the year prior. Since then, convolutional neural 
network have won all subsequent ImageNet contests, exceed human performance 
in 2015, above 90% 

Recurrent 
neural 
network 

Artificial neural network whose connection between neuron include loops, well- 
suit for processing sequence of inputs. In November 2016, Oxford University 
researcher report that a system base on recurrent neural network (and 
convolutional neural networks) have achieve 95% accuracy in reading lips, 
outperform experienced human lip readers, who test at 52% accuracy. 

This list be not comprehensive but be meant to illustrate some of the technology and technique 
that be be developed to enable automation of different work activity

Exhibit A3 

Glossary of automation technology and technique (continued) 

Technologies 
and technique Description/examples 

Robotics Soft robotics Non-rigid robot construct with soft and deformable material that can manipulate 
item of vary size, shape and weight with a single device. Soft Robotics Inc. 
grippers can adaptively pick up soft food (e.g., bake goods, tomatoes) without 
damage them. 

Swarm 
robotics 

Coordinated multi-robot systems, often involve large number of mostly 
physical robot 

Tactile/touch 
robotics 

Robotic body part (often biologically inspire hands) with capability to sense, touch, 
exhibit dexterity, and perform variety of task 

Serpentine 
robot 

Serpentine look robot with many internal degree of freedom to thread through 
tightly packed space 

Humanoid 
robot 

Robots physically similar to human being (often bi-pedal) that integrate variety of AI 
and robotics technology and be capable of perform variety of human task 
(including movement across terrains, object recognition, speech, emotion sensing, 
etc.). Aldebaran Robotics and Softbank’s humanoid Pepper robot be be use to 
provide customer service in more than 140 Softbank Mobile store in Japan 

Automation 
product 
category 

Autonomous 
car and 
truck 

Wheeled vehicle capable of operating without a human driver. In July 2016, Tesla 
report that it car have driven over 130 million mile while on “Autopilot.” In 
December 2016, Rio Tinto have a fleet of 73 driverless truck haul iron ore 24 
hours/day in mine in Western Australia 

Unmanned 
aerial 
vehicle 

Flying vehicle capable of operating without a human pilot. The unarmed General 
Atomics Predator XP UAV, with roughly half the wingspan of a Boeing 737, can fly 
autonomously for up to 35 hour from take-off to land 

Chatbots AI system design to simulate conversation with human users, particularly those 
integrate into message apps. In December 2015, the General Services 
Administration of the US government described how it us a chatbot name Mrs. 
Landingham (a character from the television show The West Wing) to help onboard 
new employee 

Robotic 
process 
automation 

Class of software “robots” that replicates the action of a human be interact with 
the user interface of other software systems. Enables the automation of many “back- 
office” (e.g., finance, human resources) workflow without require expensive IT 
integration. For example, many workflow simply require data to be transfer from 
one system to another

AND MCKINSEY RESEARCH 

A labor market that works: Connecting 
talent with opportunity in the digital age 
(June 2015) 
Online talent platform be increasingly 
connect people to the right work 
opportunities. By 2025 they could add 
$2.7 trillion to global GDP, and begin to 
ameliorate many of the persistent problem in 
the world’s labor markets. 

Global growth: Can productivity save the 
day in an age world? (January 2015) 
Without action, global economic growth 
will almost halve in the next 50 years. This 
MGI report offer a solution: a dramatic 
improvement in productivity. 

Urban world: The global consumer to 
watch (March 2016) 
Dramatic demographic shift be transform 
the world’s consumer landscape. MGI’s 
research find just three group of consumer 
be set to generate half of global urban 
consumption growth from 2015 to 2030. 

A blueprint for address the 
global affordable housing challenge 
(October 2014) 
A third of urban dwellers—1.6 billion people 
—could struggle to secure decent housing 
by 2025. Here be four approach that 
could help. 

A future that works: Automation, 
employment, and productivity 
(January 2017) 
This MGI report explores the potential us of 
automation in multiple industry and it effect 
on employment and productivity. 

Independent work: Choice, necessity, and 
the gig economy (October 2016) 
This report examines all the way people be 
earn income, a well a the challenge that 
independent work presents. 

Digital America: A tale of the have and 
have-mores (December 2015) 
While the most advanced sectors, companies, 
and individual push the boundary of 
technology use, the US economy a a whole be 
realize only 18 percent of it digital potential. 

Bridging global infrastructure gap 
(June 2016) 
Global infrastructure system be strain to 
meet demand, and the spending trajectory 
will lead to worsen gaps. But there be 
solution to unlock financing and make the 
sector more productive.
